[1] قوله : دعوا إلى الإسلام ؛ أي يدعوهم الإمام ومن معه إلى الإسلام، فإن أسلموا فذلك هو الثابت من فعل الرسول ، وقوله : ففي «مستدرك الحاكم» و«مصنف عبد الرزاق» و«معجم الطبراني» و «مسند أحمد»: «ما قاتل رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قوماً حتى دعاهم إلى الإسلام) وفي مسند أحمد عن فروة ، قال : أتيت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فقلت : أقاتل لمقبل قومي ومدبرهم، قال: نعم، فلمّا وليت دعاني فقال : لا تقاتلهم حتى تدعوهم إلى الإسلام». وعند أحمد والحاكم عن سلمان الفارسي : أنه انتهى إلى حصن أو مدينة ، فقال لأصحابه : دعوني أدعوهم كما رأيتُ رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يدعوهم، فقال لهم : إنما كنت رجلاً منكم ، فهداني الله للإسلام، فإن أسلمتم فلكم ما لنا، وعليكم ما علينا، وإن أبيتم فأدوا الجزية وأنتم صاغرون، فإن أبيتهم فإنا ننبذ إليكم على سواء، إن الله له لا يحب الخائنين ، ففعل ذلك ثلاثة أيام، فلمّا كان اليوم الرابع أمر الناس فغزو إليها ففتحوها (٢). [٢] قوله : فإلى الجزية ؛ أي دعوا إلى الجزية بأن يؤدّوها ويصيروا مطيعين لأهل الإسلام.
Chapter on the method of fighting. (If they are besieged) meaning the disbelievers, if Muslims besiege them, (they are called to Islam, then if they refuse, then to jizya, then if they accept, they have what we have and upon them what is upon us). [1] His statement: 'called to Islam' meaning the Imam and those with him call them to Islam. If they accept Islam, that is established from the action of the Messenger, and his statement: For in Mustadrak al-Hakim, Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq, Mujam al-Tabarani, and Musnad Ahmad: 'The Messenger of God never fought a people until he called them to Islam.' [2] His statement: 'then to jizya' meaning they are called to jizya by paying it and becoming obedient to the people of Islam.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage from the commentary explains the ruling for besieging non-Muslims. The matn states: if they are besieged, they are called to Islam. The commentator explains that the Imam and the Muslims call them to Islam. If they accept, that is the precedent from the Prophet. He then cites multiple hadith to prove this practice: the Prophet never fought a people without first inviting them to Islam. The second stage: if they refuse Islam, they are called to jizya—i.e., accepting subjugation and paying the jizya tax. If they accept jizya, they are granted the same rights as Muslims (protection, etc.) and bear the same obligations, except for obligations of worship as will be elaborated. The commentator emphasizes that this sequence is obligatory, based on the hadith evidence.

Key Terms
جزية (jizyah): A tax imposed on non-Muslims under Muslim rule in exchange for protection and exemption from military service.
ذمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim citizen of an Islamic state who pays jizya and enjoys protection.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that inviting non-Muslims to Islam and jizya before fighting is a wajib condition derived from the Prophet's consistent practice.

The Scope of 'Laha Ma Lana' for Dhimmis: Worship or Protection?
Explanation of the scope of 'laha ma lana wa alayhim ma alayna'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether dhimmis are addressed with acts of worship, state the three positions, and identify the mufta bihi view supported by the hadith of Ali (RA).

Core Idea
Dhimmis are protected in life and property, not obligated to perform worship.

When the Hanafi jurists state 'laha ma lana wa alayhim ma alayna' for dhimmis, does this mean they must pray and fast like Muslims? This lesson unpacks the true scope of that maxim by examining an intra-Hanafi debate over whether disbelievers are addressed (mukhattab) with the obligations of worship. The commentary from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies that the maxim is restricted to worldly matters, relying on the hadith of Ali (RA) and Quranic verses on jizya.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the apparent generality of the maxim
The maxim 'laha ma lana wa alayhim ma alayna' seems to include all obligations, including worship. Identify why this apparent meaning is rejected by consensus.
The commentator flags this immediately: 'la yuradu hadha al-hukm ala al-umum'.
2
Identify the three Hanafi positions on kuffar being mukhattab bi al-ibadat
The text presents three views: 1) Samarkand: not addressed at all (neither performance nor belief). 2) Bukhara: addressed in belief but not performance. 3) Iraqis: addressed in both. Classify each.
This is a classic intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on usul level.
3
Locate the dalil that restricts the maxim
The hadith of Ali (RA) is cited: 'They only paid jizya so that their blood be like our blood and their property like our property.' Explain how this proof limits 'laha ma lana' to protection of life and property.
Also support from Quran 9:29 and 2:256 (no compulsion in religion).
4
Connect the dispute to practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
What differs in real fiqh? If dhimmis are not obligated to worship, we do not compel them; if they were, they would be sinning for neglect. State the mufta bihi view.
Mufta bihi: they are not commanded to perform in this world, but must believe in the truth of Islam.

(۳) وهو قول عامة أهل الحديث والمعتزلة وقول مشايخ العراق من الحنفية والغزالي، وقال في «البرهان»: إنه ظاهر مذهب الشافعي. ينظر : «المیزان» (۱) : (۳۰۷)، و«المستصفى»(۲ : ۷۸)،

[Page 354] Commentary on the meaning that this ruling is not intended generally to indicate that acts of worship and other obligations are binding upon them, because according to us disbelievers are not addressed with acts of worship. But according to those who say they are addressed, the dhimmi and others are equal in that. [Footnote 1] The commentator's words: 'Know...' etc. Since the apparent meaning of his statement, 'They have what we have and upon them what is upon us' encompasses all rulings, and it is understood that they are commanded to perform acts of worship and the like, which is not the case by consensus, the commentator clarified the intended meaning. [Footnote 2] His words: 'Because the disbelievers are not addressed' is a cause for 'it is not intended', and the conclusion is that they agree that disbelievers are not obligated to perform acts of worship while in disbelief. Yes, they are addressed with faith and with punishments, except for the hadd of drinking as previously mentioned, and with transactions except for what is excluded regarding dealing in wine and pigs as will come in its place. As for acts of worship, the scholars of Samarkand said: They are not addressed with them at all, neither performance nor belief. The scholars of Bukhara said: They are not addressed with them in performance, but they are addressed with them in belief. The Iraqis said: They are addressed with both, so they are punished for abandoning their performance and abandoning belief in their truth. The detail is in the books of usul. [Footnote 3] His words: 'and others' because the harbi is also addressed according to him. [Footnote 4] His words: 'are equal' so there is no meaning to tying it to acceptance of jizya. [Page 355] And upon acceptance of jizya, we do not command them with acts of worship as we command Muslims. Rather, it is intended that what is obligatory for us toward them and what is obligatory for them toward us, when we attack their blood and property or they attack our blood and property, is what is obligatory among some of us toward others when attacked. This is because before acceptance of jizya we were attacking their blood and property and they were attacking our blood and property, so acceptance of jizya is only for the removal of this attack. This is supported by the fact that they made the proof of this ruling the saying of Ali (RA): 'They only paid jizya so that their blood may be like our blood and their property like our property.' [Footnote 1] His words: 'what is obligatory...' i.e., dealing with justice and fairness. In short, we have jurisdiction over them in punishments and transactions except what is excluded in its place, but not the faith and acts of worship, for we do not demand them from them. They only gave jizya to be protected in blood and property, so upon us is to refrain from attacking them in blood and property except by legal right, and their account is with Allah. [Footnote 2] His words: 'only for removal' this attack is indicated by His statement: 'Fight those who do not believe in Allah and the Last Day... until they give jizya willingly while they are humiliated' (9:29) and His statement: 'There is no compulsion in religion' (2:256). [Footnote 3] His words: 'supported by that' i.e., the exclusion of acts of worship, or restricting what was mentioned to justice and fairness in wealth and blood. The conclusion: The jurists, when they ruled that after acceptance of jizya they have what we have and upon them what is upon us, relied on the saying of Ali al-Murtada (RA) indicating that the people of dhimmah after acceptance of jizya have their wealth and blood protected and it is unlawful for us to attack them. Thus it is known that what they meant by their statement 'they have what we have and upon them what is upon us' is what we mentioned of refraining from attacking their wealth and blood, not that acts of worship are obligatory upon them as they are obligatory upon us. [Footnote 4] His words: 'the saying of Ali (RA)' mentioned in al-Hidayah and others. Al-Zayla'i said in its takhrij: [Rawiya].
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the hadith-based principle 'They have what we have and upon them what is upon us' as it applies to dhimmis (non-Muslim citizens of the Islamic state) after accepting jizya. The commentator, author of 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, clarifies that this statement is not to be taken in its general apparent meaning, which would imply that dhimmis are obligated to perform acts of worship like Muslims. Instead, it is a technical legal maxim restricted to protection of life and property and mutual obligations in civil and criminal matters. The passage first notes that the apparent meaning would also obligate acts of worship, but this is not the case by consensus. The commentator then explains that disbelievers are not addressed with the commands of worship (according to the Hanafi school). He then details three intra-Hanafi positions on whether disbelievers are addressed with worship: the Samarkand scholars say they are not addressed at all (neither performance nor belief); the Bukhara scholars say they are not addressed with performance but are addressed with belief (i.e., they are punished for not believing in the truth of Islam); the Iraqis say they are addressed with both, meaning they are punished for failing to perform worship in the Hereafter even if not obligated in the world. The commentator then emphasizes that the dhimmi and the harbi (non-Muslim at war) are equal in this regard according to those who hold they are addressed. He then clarifies that after accepting jizya, the state does not command dhimmis to perform worship; rather, the principle means that we must treat them justly in mutual dealings and protect their blood and property, just as they must not attack ours. The proof is the verse about fighting until jizya is given (9:29) and the verse 'No compulsion in religion' (2:256), as well as the saying of Ali (RA) that jizya makes their blood and property like ours. Thus, the ruling is that dhimmis are not required to perform acts of worship; they are only obligated to refrain from harming Muslims and to pay jizya in exchange for protection.

Key Terms
الجزية (al-jizyah): A tax paid by non-Muslim subjects (dhimmis) in exchange for protection and exemption from military service.
أهل الذمة (ahl al-dhimmah): Non-Muslims living under the protection of the Islamic state, having a covenant (dhimma).
مخاطب بالعبادات (mukhattab bi al-ibadat): Being legally addressed by the commands of Sharia regarding acts of worship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi position (mufta bihi), are dhimmis addressed with acts of worship?
A) Yes, they are addressed with both performance and belief.
B) No, they are not addressed at all.
C) They are addressed with belief but not with performance.
D) The Hanafi school has no consensus on this issue.

Key Takeaway
The principle 'laha ma lana' for dhimmis is limited to worldly protection and justice; they are not obligated to perform acts of worship in this life.

Meaning of Mutual Obligations After Jizyah
Explaining that jizyah implies protection, not imposition of worship

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi interpretation of the phrase 'lana ma lahum' and explain why jizyah does not impose religious obligations on dhimmis.

Core Idea
Jizyah ensures mutual security of life and property, not equality in worship.

In this passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah, the author clarifies a common misunderstanding about the status of dhimmis after they pay jizyah. The phrase 'lana ma lahum wa alayna ma alayhim' is often misread as full legal equality. Here we learn that it means reciprocal security, not shared religious duties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
After jizyah, we do not command dhimmis with acts of worship as we command Muslims. The ruling is that protection is obligatory, not imposition of worship.
2
Locate the Dalil
The author cites Quran 9:29 (fight until jizyah) and 2:256 (no compulsion in religion), along with a statement of Ali (RA) that jizyah makes their blood and property like ours.
The Quranic verses provide the basis; the companion statement supports the interpretation.
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah
The verse commands fighting until jizyah is given, implying that jizyah ends hostility. The phrase 'they are humbled' indicates they come under protection. Combined with 'no compulsion in religion', it shows that worship is not imposed.
The Hanafi proof relies on this combined reading.
4
Examine the Expansion
The author expands on the phrase 'lana ma lahum' by explaining that it refers to refraining from exposing their blood and property except by legal right, not to equality in religious duties.
This expansion distinguishes the Hanafi view from a literal reading.
5
Confirm the Hanafi Position
The jurists rely on Ali's statement to establish that the contract of jizyah guarantees protection. Thus, 'lana ma lahum' is limited to security, not worship.
This is the mufta bihi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعند قبول الجزية لا نأمرهم بالعبادات، كما نأمر المسلمين، بل يراد أنه يجب لهم علينا ويجب لنا عليهم إذا تعرّضنا لدمائهم وأموالهم، أو تعرضوا لدمائنا وأموالنا ما يجب لبعضنا على بعض عند التعرض ؛ وذلك لأن قبل قبول الجزية كنا نتعرض لدمائهم وأموالهم ، وكانوا يتعرضون لدمائنا وأموالنا ، فقبول الجزية ليس إلا لزوال هذا التعرض ، يؤيد ذلك أنهم جعلوا الدليل على هذا الحكم قول علي إنما بذلوا الجزية ليكون دماؤهم كدمائنا وأموالهم كأموالنا. قوله : ما يجب...الخ ؛ أي المعاملة بالعدل والإنصاف، وبالجملة : لنا الحكم عليهم في العقوبات والمعاملات إلا ما استثني في موضعه دون الإيمان والمعاملات فلا نطالبهم بهما، فإنّهم إنما أعطوا الجزية ليكونوا محفوظين دماً ومالاً ، فعلينا ترك التعرّض بهم دماً ومالاً إلا بحق الشرع، وحسابهم على الله . قوله : ليس إلا لزوال هذا التعرّض يدلّ عليه قوله تعالى : قاتلوا الذين لا يؤمنون بالله ولا باليوم الآخر ولا يحرمون ما حرم الله ورسوله ولا يدينون دين الحق من الذين أوتوا الكتاب حتى يعطوا الجزية عن يد وهم صاغرون [التوبة:29]، وقوله : لا إكراه في الدين [البقرة:256]. قوله : يؤيد ذلك ؛ أي خروج العبادات، أو تقييد ما ذكره بالعدل والإنصاف في الأموال والدماء، والحاصل : إنّ الفقهاء حيث حكموا بأنّ بعد قبول الجزية لهم ما لنا وعليهم ما علينا، استندوا بقول علي المرتضى الدال على أن أهل الذمة بعد قبول الجزية تحفظ أموالهم ودماؤهم ويحرم علينا التعرض بهم. فعلم به أنهم إنما أرادوا بقولهم : لهم ما لنا وعليهم ما علينا، هو ما ذكرنا من ترك التعرّض بأموالهم ودمائهم لا أنه تجب عليهم العبادات كما تجب علينا. قوله : قول علي رضي الله عنه ذكره في الهداية وغيره، قال الزيلعي في تخريجه: غريب، ولكنه أخرج عن علي بلفظ : من كان له ذمتنا فدمه كدمنا وديته كديتنا في سنن الدارقطني وسنن البيهقي ومسند الشافعي.
And upon acceptance of jizyah, we do not command them with acts of worship as we command Muslims, but rather it is meant that they owe us obligations and we owe them obligations when we expose their blood and property, or they expose our blood and property, what is obligatory between us and them upon such exposure. That is because before acceptance of jizyah, we used to expose their blood and property, and they used to expose our blood and property, so acceptance of jizyah is only to remove this exposure. This is supported by the fact that they made as evidence for this ruling the statement of Ali: 'They only gave jizyah so that their blood would be like our blood and their property like our property.' His statement: 'what is obligatory...' etc.; meaning dealing with justice and fairness. In summary: we have authority over them in penal and transactional matters except what is excepted in its place, but not in faith and acts of worship, for we do not require them in these. Indeed, they gave jizyah to be protected in blood and property, so upon us is to refrain from exposing them in blood and property except by right of the Law, and their reckoning is with Allah. His statement: 'only to remove this exposure' is indicated by Allah's saying: 'Fight those who do not believe in Allah nor in the Last Day...' [9:29] and His saying: 'There is no compulsion in religion' [2:256]. His statement: 'this is supported' means the exclusion of acts of worship, or restricting what was mentioned to justice and fairness in property and blood. The conclusion: The jurists, when they ruled that after acceptance of jizyah, for them is what is for us and upon them is what is upon us, relied on the statement of Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) indicating that the dhimmis after acceptance of jizyah their properties and blood are protected and it is forbidden for us to expose them. So it is understood that by their statement: 'for them is what is for us and upon them is what is upon us' they meant what we have mentioned of refraining from exposing their property and blood, not that acts of worship are obligatory upon them as they are obligatory upon us. His statement: 'the statement of Ali' is mentioned in al-Hidayah and elsewhere. Al-Zayla'i said in his takhrij: It is gharib, but al-Daraqutni and others narrated from Ali with the wording: 'Whoever has our dhimmah, his blood is like our blood and his diyah is like our diyah.'
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a ruling regarding the implications of accepting jizyah (the poll tax) from non-Muslims. The author explains that when non-Muslims accept jizyah, we do not require them to perform acts of worship as we require Muslims. Instead, the phrase 'they owe us what we owe them' refers to mutual protection of life and property. Before jizyah, both sides were in a state of war, exposing each other's blood and property. Jizyah is a contract that removes this exposure, establishing reciprocal security. The author supports this with the Quranic verse commanding fight until jizyah is given (9:29) and the verse 'no compulsion in religion' (2:256). He also cites a statement attributed to Ali (RA) that the purpose of jizyah is to make their blood and property like ours. The Hanafi jurists rely on this to mean that after jizyah, we must not transgress against them except by legal right; acts of worship are not demanded of them. The commentary clarifies that the phrase 'for them what is for us, and upon them what is upon us' in the texts of the jurists refers only to this protection, not to equality in religious obligations.

Key Terms
الجزية (al-jizyah): A poll tax imposed on non-Muslims (dhimmis) in exchange for protection and exemption from military service.
الذمة (al-dhimmah): The pact of protection and responsibility that the Islamic state grants to non-Muslims living under its rule.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They must now perform all acts of worship like Muslims.
B) The Muslim state owes them protection of life and property, and they owe obedience in secular matters.
C) They are free from any obligations toward the state.
D) They must be forced to convert to Islam.

Key Takeaway
Jizyah establishes a contract of protection, not a demand for worship.

Calling to Islam Before Fighting: Wajib or Mandub?
Clarifying the ruling on calling to Islam before combat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on calling non-Muslims to Islam before fighting, distinguish between those who have and have not received the call, and support the ruling with the relevant hadith evidence.

Core Idea
Fighting before the call is sinful; calling is wajib for the unreached, mandub for the reached.

This lesson examines the critical distinction Hanafi jurists make regarding the obligation to invite non-Muslims to Islam before engaging in combat. The general principle is that those who have never heard the message must be called first, while for those who have, the call is not required but recommended. We will explore the textual evidence from the Sunnah that supports this nuanced position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
The passage distinguishes between those who have not received the call (unreached) and those who have (reached). Note the different rulings: wajib vs. mandub.
2
Understand the reasoning behind the distinction
The purpose of the call is to clarify that fighting is for religion, not worldly gain. If they accept, fighting stops. For those already aware, a prior call is not needed.
3
Locate the hadith evidence
Two hadiths: the raid on Banu al-Mustaliq and on Khaybar. These show that the Prophet attacked without a prior call because the call had already reached them.
4
Reconcile apparent contradictions
The scholars reconcile the hadiths commanding a call with those showing sudden raids by applying the rule that the command to call applies to the unreached, while the raids are for the reached.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يُقاتَل من لم تبلغه الدعوة ونُدِبَت : أي الدعوة : أي نُدِب تجديد الدعوة. قوله : ولا يُقاتَل ؛ بصيغة المجهول ؛ أي لا يحل القتال بمن لم تبلغه دعوة الإسلام إلا أن يدعوه أولاً ليعلموا أنا إنما نقاتلهم على الدين لا على سلب الأموال وسبي الذراري، فإن أسلموا لم يحتج إلى القتال. قال في الهداية: ولو قاتلهم قبل الدعوة أثم للنهي، ولا غرامة لعدم العاصم وهو الدين أو الإحراز بالدار، فصار كقتل النسوان والصبيان. قوله : ونُدِبَت يعني الكفار الذين بلغهم خبر الإسلام يجوز قتالهم من غير دعاء إلى الإسلام لحصول علمهم به، نعم يستحب ذلك لتجديد العلم. وقد أخرج البخاري ومسلم عن ابن عون: كتبت إلى نافع أسأله عن الدعاء إلى القتال، فكتب إلي: إنما كان ذلك في أول الإسلام، وقد أغار رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم على بني المصطلق وهم غارون أي غافلون، وأنعامهم تسقى على الماء، فقتل مقاتلتهم، وسبي ذراريهم، وأصاب يومئذ جويرية بنت الحارث، حدثني به عبد الله وكان في ذلك الجيش. وأخرج الحازمي في كتاب الناسخ والمنسوخ عن ابن عمر: إن رسول الله أغار على خيبر يوم الخميس وهم غارون، فقتل المقاتلة وسبى الذرية. قال الحازمي: قد جمع بعض العلماء بين الأحاديث، فقال: الأحاديث الأول أي الدالة على الأمر بالدعوة قبل القتال محمولة على الأمر بدعاء من لم تبلغهم الدعوة، وأما بنو المصطلق وخيبر فقد بلغتهم الدعوة.
And one does not fight one whom the call has not reached, and it is recommended: meaning the call: meaning the renewal of the call is recommended. His statement: 'and one does not fight' in the passive voice; meaning it is not permissible to fight someone whom the call of Islam has not reached unless one calls him first, so that they know that we only fight them over religion, not for plunder of property and capture of offspring. If they embrace Islam, there is no need for fighting. He said in al-Hidayah: 'If he fought them before the call, he sins due to the prohibition, and there is no indemnity because there is no protector (ismah) which is religion or abode, so it is like killing women and children.' His statement: 'and it is recommended' means that the disbelievers who have heard the news of Islam, it is permissible to fight them without calling them to Islam because of their knowledge of it; yes, it is recommended to call them for renewal of knowledge. Al-Bukhari and Muslim narrated from Ibn 'Awn: 'I wrote to Nafi' asking him about the call to fight, and he wrote to me: That was only in the early days of Islam, and the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) raided Banu al-Mustaliq while they were heedless (ghafilun), and their cattle were being watered at the water, so he killed their fighters and captured their offspring, and on that day he took Juwayriyah bint al-Harith.' 'Abdullah told me this and was in that army. And al-Hazimi in his book al-Nasikh wa al-Mansukh narrated from Ibn 'Umar: 'The Messenger of Allah raided Khaybar on Thursday while they were heedless, so he killed the fighters and captured the offspring.' al-Hazimi said: Some scholars have reconciled between the hadiths, saying: The first hadiths, i.e., those indicating the command to call before fighting, are interpreted as ordering the call for those whom the call has not reached; as for Banu al-Mustaliq and Khaybar, the call had reached them.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on calling non-Muslims to Islam before engaging in combat. The author explains that it is not permissible to fight those who have never heard the message of Islam until they are first invited. This is to clarify that the war is for religious reasons, not for worldly gain. If they accept Islam, fighting is avoided. Imam al-Hidayah (al-Marghinani) states that fighting before the call is sinful because it is prohibited, but no monetary compensation (ghurmah) is due because there is no protected status (ismah) until they embrace Islam or take an abode of security. It is akin to killing women and children who are not legitimate targets. The phrase 'and it is recommended' refers to calling those who have already heard of Islam; since the message has reached them, fighting is permissible without a new call, but it is still recommended to call them to renew their awareness. The author supports this with hadiths: the early practice of calling before fighting was in the first period, but later the Prophet (peace be upon him) made sudden raids on Banu al-Mustaliq and Khaybar because the call had already reached them. Thus, the scholars reconcile the apparent contradiction: the command to call applies to those unreached, while sudden attack is allowed for those who have already received the message.

Key Terms
الدعوة (al-da'wah): The invitation to embrace Islam, including the basic message of tawheed and risalah.
ندب (nudub): A recommended act (mandub), not obligatory; in this context, renewing the call to those who already know.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this text, if a group of polytheists have already heard the call to Islam but remain polytheists, what is the ruling on initiating a surprise attack against them?
A) It is prohibited because the call must be renewed.
B) It is permissible and calling is only recommended (mandub).
C) It is obligatory to call them again.
D) It is permissible without any recommendation to call.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling distinguishes between those who have and have not heard the call: wajib for the unreached, mandub for the reached, supported by the practice of the Prophet.

Jihad — Armed Conflict Rules
intermediate
7 min
Rulings on the Call to Islam and Permissible Weapons in Jihad
Rulings on fighting before the call and permissible methods of warfare

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on fighting before the call and identify the permissible weapons of war after the enemy refuses.

Core Idea
Fighting without the call is forbidden; after refusal, all necessary force is permitted.

This lesson examines two key rulings in the fiqh of jihad: the requirement to invite the enemy to Islam before combat, and the permissible means of warfare after they refuse. The commentary on Al-Wiqayah clarifies that the call is obligatory for those who have not heard, but only recommended for those already aware. After the call is rejected, the Muslim commander may use mangonels, burning, and drowning as legitimate methods to break enemy power.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
The passage addresses (a) the obligation/recommendation of calling the enemy before fighting and (b) the permissibility of using mangonels, burning, and drowning after refusal.
These are distinct but connected: the call is a prerequisite for legitimate combat, and weapons are for after refusal.
2
Examine the proofs (dalil)
For the call requirement, the commentary cites the Hadith of Ali (from al-Daraqutni) and the prophetic raids on Banu al-Mustaliq and Khaybar. For weapons, it cites Quran 59:5 and the Prophet's use of mangonels at Ta'if and cutting/burning palm trees of Banu al-Nadir.
Note that the hadith of the raids show the call was not repeated if already known.
3
Distinguish between unreached and reached
The call is obligatory (wajib) for those who have never heard of Islam. For those who have already heard, the call is recommended (mustahabb) but not obligatory.
This distinction resolves apparent contradictions in the hadith.
4
Apply the ruling to permissible weapons
After the enemy refuses Islam or jizya, the Muslim army may use all necessary force, including mangonels (catapults), burning of persons and property, and drowning. These are permitted to cause grief, break power, and disperse the enemy.
The permissibility is based on prophetic precedent and Quranic verse.

ولا يقاتل من لم تبلغه الدعوة وندبت (ولا يقاتل من لم تبلغه الدعوة وندبت: أي الدعوة: أي ندب تجديد الدعوة غريب؛ أي بهذا اللفظ، وأخرج الدارقطني في «سننه» عن الحكم عن حسين بن ميمون عن أبي الحبوب عن عبد الله مولى بني هاشم قال: قال علي: «من كانت له ذمتنا فدمه كدمنا وديته كديتنا». قوله: ولا يقاتل؛ بصيغة المجهول؛ أي لا يحل القتال بمن لم تبلغه دعوة الإسلام إلا أن يدعوه أولاً؛ ليعلموا أنا إنما نقاتلهم على الدين لا على سلب الأموال وسبي الذراري، فإن أسلموا لم يحتج إلى القتال، وعلى هذا يحل ما ورد من الأمر بالدعاء إلى الإسلام قبل القتال على ما مر من الأحاديث، وسيأتي بعضها. قال في «الهداية»: «ولو قاتلهم قبل الدعوة أثم للنهي، ولا غرامة لعدم العاصم وهو الدين أو الإحراز بالدار، فصار كقتل النسوان والصبيان». قوله: وندبت؛ يعني الكفار الذين بلغهم خبر الإسلام يجوز قتالهم من غير دعاء إلى الإسلام؛ لحصول علمهم به، نعم يستحب ذلك لتجديد العلم. وقد أخرج البخاري ومسلم عن ابن عون قال: كتبت إلى نافع أسئلة عن القتال، فكتب إلي: إنما كان ذلك في أول الإسلام، وقد أغار رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم على بني المصطلق وهم غارون؛ أي غافلون، وأنعامهم تسقى على الماء، فقتل مقاتلتهم، وسبي ذراريهم، وأصاب يومئذ جويرية بنت الحارث، حدثني به عبد الله بن عمر، وكان في ذلك الجيش. وأخرج الحازمي في كتاب الناسخ والمنسوخ عن ابن عمر: «إن رسول الله أغار على خيبر يوم الخميس وهم غارون، فقتل المقاتلة وسبى الذرية». قال الحازمي: قد جمع بعض العلماء بين الأحاديث، فقال: الأحاديث الأول؛ أي الدالة على الأمر بالدعوة قبل القتال محمولة على الأمر بدعاء من لم تبلغهم الدعوة، وأما بنو المصطلق وأهل خيبر فإن الدعوة كانت بلغتهم.
لمن بلغته فإن أبوا حوربوا بمنجنيق، وتحريق، وتغريق (لمن بلغته فإن أبوا: أي عن الجزية، حوربوا بمنجنيق، وتحريق، وتغريق. قوله: فإن أبوا؛ هذا في حق من تقبل منه الجزية، ومن لا تقبل منه، كالمرتدين وعبدة الأوثان من العرب لا فائدة في دعائهم إلى الجزية؛ لأنه لا يقبل منهم إلا الإسلام، فإن أبوا عن الإسلام حوربوا، كذا في «الهداية». قوله: بمنجنيق؛ - بفتح الميم، وفتح الجيم، بعدها نون مكسورة، بعدها ياء مثناة تحتية ساكنة، ثم قاف –، هو معرب من: جيه نيك؛ أي ما أجود حالي، وهو آلة ترمى بها الحجارة الكبار على الجدران والحصون. قال في «غياث اللغات»: نوع از فلاخن بزراً که بر سوجوبي قولي تعبيه کنند وسنگهاي کلان دران نهاده بر دیوار قلعه زده دیوار في شكنندر واين معرب من جه نيك ست والادر خاص عربي جيم وقاف در هيج كلمه نياسده وجون در زمانه سابق زین آله بجهت قلعة كيري كمال مفيد بود لهذا تفاخراً باین اسم مسمى گشت. قوله: وتحريق؛ أي تحريق أنفسهم وأموالهم بالنار، وتفريق أموالهم وأنفسهم بالماء؛ وذلك لأن في مثل هذه الأشياء إلحاق الغيظ بهم وكسر لشوكتهم وتفريق جمعهم؛ ولذا جاز قطع الأشجار وإفساد الزروع. والأصل فيه قوله تعالى: (مَا قَطَعْتُم مِّن لِينَةٍ أَوْ تَرَكْتُمُوهَا فَأَيْمَةٌ عَلَى أُصُولِهَا فَبِإِذْنِ اللَّهِ وَلِيُخْزِي الْفَاسِقِينَ)، وقد أخرج أبو داود في «كتاب المراسيل»، وابن سعد في «الطبقات»: «إن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم نصب المجانيق على الطائف حين حاصر بأهله». وثبت في الصحاح الستة: «إنه قطع نخل بني النضير وهم طائفة من اليهود حين أجلاهم من المدينة وحرق».
And he does not fight one whom the call has not reached, and it is recommended (to call). (And he does not fight one whom the call has not reached, and it is recommended: meaning the call: meaning the renewal of the call is recommended as a rare word; i.e., with this wording. Al-Daraqutni narrated in his Sunan from al-Hakam from Husayn ibn Maimun from Abu al-Habbab from 'Abd Allah, mawla of Bani Hashim, who said: 'Ali said: 'Whoever has our protection, his blood is like our blood and his blood-money is like our blood-money.') His statement: 'And he does not fight' in the passive voice; i.e., it is not permissible to fight someone whom the call of Islam has not reached unless one first calls him; so that they know that we only fight them over religion, not to seize wealth and capture children. If they embrace Islam, there is no need for fighting. And accordingly, what has been reported of the command to call to Islam before fighting is permissible as per the hadiths that have passed, and some will come later. He said in al-Hidayah: 'If he fights them before the call, he sins due to the prohibition, and there is no indemnity for lack of a protector, which is religion or secure residence in the abode, so it becomes like killing women and children.' His statement: 'and it is recommended' means that disbelievers who have heard the news of Islam may be fought without calling them to Islam, because they have knowledge of it. Yes, it is recommended to do so for renewal of knowledge. Al-Bukhari and Muslim narrated from Ibn 'Awn who said: 'I wrote to Nafi' asking about fighting, and he wrote to me: That was only in the beginning of Islam. The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) raided Banu al-Mustaliq while they were heedless, and their cattle were being watered, so he killed their fighters and captured their children, and on that day he took Juwayriyah bint al-Harith.' 'Abd Allah ibn 'Umar narrated it to me, and he was in that army. Al-Hazimi in his book al-Nasikh wa al-Mansukh narrated from Ibn 'Umar: 'The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) raided Khaybar on Thursday while they were heedless, killed the fighters and captured the children.' Al-Hazimi said: Some scholars have reconciled the hadiths, saying: The first hadiths, which indicate the command to call before fighting, are interpreted as the command to call those whom the call has not reached. As for Banu al-Mustaliq and the people of Khaybar, the call had reached them.
For those whom it has reached, if they refuse, they are fought with a mangonel, burning, and drowning. (For those whom it has reached, if they refuse: i.e., the jizya, they are fought with a mangonel, burning, and drowning. His statement: 'if they refuse' applies to those from whom jizya is accepted. As for those from whom jizya is not accepted, such as apostates and Arab idol-worshippers, there is no benefit in calling them to jizya because only Islam is accepted from them. If they refuse Islam, they are fought. Thus in al-Hidayah. His statement: 'with a mangonel' - with fath on the mim and jim, then a kasra on the nun, then a sukun on the ya, then qaf – it is arabicized from 'Jeh Nik' meaning 'how good my state is', and it is a device with which large stones are thrown at walls and fortresses. He said in Ghayat al-Lughat: 'a type of large catapult that they set up on a .... and place large stones in it and strike the wall of the fortress and break the wall'. His statement: 'and burning' means burning their persons and property with fire, and dispersing their property and persons with water; because such things cause them grief, break their power, and disperse their assembly; hence cutting trees and spoiling crops are permissible. The basis for this is the saying of Allah Almighty: 'Whatever palm trees you cut down or left standing on their roots, it was by Allah's permission and to disgrace the disobedient.' And Abu Dawud in Kitab al-Marasil and Ibn Sa'd in al-Tabaqat narrated that the Prophet (peace be upon him) set up mangonels against Ta'if when he besieged its people. And it is established in the six authentic books that he cut down the palm trees of Banu al-Nadir, a group of Jews, when he expelled them from Madina, and burned (them).
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah deals with two interconnected rulings in the fiqh of jihad: the obligation (or lack thereof) of calling the enemy to Islam before combat, and the permissible means of warfare after they have refused. The passage begins with the base text stating that someone whom the call has not reached should not be fought, and that it is recommended to renew the call. The commentator explains that the passive voice indicates the prohibition of initiating combat without prior invitation. The purpose is to clarify that Muslims fight for the sake of religion, not for plunder or captives. If the enemy accepts Islam, fighting ceases. Quoting Al-Hidayah, the commentator emphasizes that fighting before the call is sinful, but no financial liability (ghuramah) arises because the enemy had no protection (ismah) – not by religion nor by secure residence in dar al-harb. This is analogous to killing women and children, who are not combatants. The recommendation (nadb) to call applies even to those who have already heard of Islam, based on the practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him) raiding Banu al-Mustaliq and Khaybar without prior invitation because the message had already reached them. The hadith from Nafi' indicates that early in Islam, raids without prior call were exceptional. The second portion addresses what happens after the call is made and the enemy refuses. If they refuse to accept Islam or pay jizya, they are fought with all necessary force, including mangonels (catapults), burning, and drowning. The commentator clarifies that for apostates and Arab polytheists, only Islam is accepted, so they are fought if they refuse. The mangonel (manjaniq) is defined as a siege engine for throwing stones. Burning and drowning are permitted because they cause grief and break enemy power. The proof is Quran 59:5, which permits cutting palm trees, and the hadith of the Prophet using mangonels at Ta'if and cutting/burning the palm trees of Banu al-Nadir. These rulings demonstrate the Hanafi position that war is waged with all necessary force after the invitation, but every effort is made to invite first.

Key Terms
دعوة (da'wah): The invitation to Islam or to accept jizya; a legal requirement before initiating combat against those who have not heard of Islam.
منجنيق (manjaniq): A siege engine (mangonel/catapult) used to throw large stones at fortress walls. It is permissible to use in jihad after the call has been made.
تحريق (tahriq): Burning of the enemy's persons and property as a permissible act of war, based on the Prophet's actions and Quran 59:5.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on fighting a group of disbelievers whom the call to Islam has never reached?
A) Permissible without any call
B) Prohibited unless they are first called to Islam
C) Permissible only if they attack first
D) Recommended to call but fighting is allowed anyway

Key Takeaway
Fighting before the call is sinful; after the call and refusal, all necessary force, including burning and mangonels, is permitted.

Permissible Methods of Warfare: Mangonels, Fire, and Flood
Commentary on permissibility of mangonels, burning, and flooding

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Quranic and Prophetic proofs for the permissibility of using mangonels, burning, and flooding in jihad, and explain the Hanafi position.

Core Idea
All necessary force, including mangonels, fire, and water, is permitted in jihad to break the enemy's power; the Quran and Sunnah confirm this.

This lesson examines the legal justification for using heavy siege weapons, fire, and water in warfare. The commentator cites the Quranic verse about cutting palm-trees and Prophetic precedents to establish that such methods are permissible. Understanding this ruling requires tracing the proof from the primary sources.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? The use of mangonels, burning, and flooding. The ruling is permissible (mubah).
These are methods of warfare, not the obligation to fight itself.
2
Locate the dalil
What proof is cited? Quran 59:5 and the hadith of the Prophet using mangonels at Ta'if and cutting palm-trees.
The proof is both Quranic and Prophetic.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the proof establish permissibility? The verse permits cutting trees to disgrace the enemy; this indicates that causing destruction is allowed. The Prophet's actions confirm it.
The illa (effective cause) is breaking the enemy's power.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Is there any disagreement? The passage states this is agreed upon (muttafaq alayh).
No major intra-madhhab difference.
5
Determine the thamarah
What is the practical consequence? The Muslim army can use any necessary force, including modern equivalents like artillery.
The ruling is operative for all conventional weapons.

The Book of Jihad / Its ruling and manner. For one whom it has reached, if they refuse, they are fought with mangonel, burning, and drowning. For one whom it has reached, if they refuse: meaning (refuse) the jizya, they are fought with mangonel, burning, and drowning, like the tribe of al-Mustaliq and the people of Khaybar, for the call had reached them. [1] His saying: 'if they refuse': this applies to one from whom jizya is accepted and one from whom it is not accepted, like apostates and idol-worshippers of the Arabs; there is no benefit in calling them to jizya because only Islam is accepted from them; if they refuse Islam, they are fought. Thus in al-Hidayah. [2] His saying: 'with mangonel' – it is an Arabicized word from 'Jih-e Nik' meaning 'how excellent my state', and it is a device for launching large stones at walls and fortresses. In Ghiyath al-Lughat it is described as a type of large slingshot that is set up on a platform and large stones placed in it to strike the castle wall and break it. [3] His saying: 'and burning': meaning burning them and their property with fire, and dispersing their property and persons with water; because in such things there is inflicting of rage upon them, breaking their power, and dispersing their gathering; therefore cutting trees and spoiling crops is permitted. The proof for it is His saying: 'Whatever palm-trees you cut down or left standing on their roots, it was by God's permission and so that He might disgrace the rebellious.' And Abu Dawud in Kitab al-Marasil and Ibn Sa'd in al-Tabaqat narrated that the Prophet set up mangonels against Ta'if when he besieged its people. And it is established in the six authentic books that he cut down the palm-trees of Banu al-Nadir, a group of Jews, when he expelled them from Medina, and burned. And shooting, even if there is a Muslim with them or they use them as shields with their intention not his intention, and cutting trees and spoiling crops without excuse. And shooting: meaning with spears and the like, such as lead weapons used in our time and similar, and its proof is His saying: 'And prepare for them whatever force you can and of horses tethered that thereby you may terrify the enemy of God and your enemy.' [2] His saying: 'even if there is with them': the waw is conjunctive, he said in al-Hidayah: 'There is no harm in shooting them, even if there is among them a Muslim captive or merchant, because in shooting there is repelling general harm by defending the very essence of Islam, and killing the captive and merchant is specific harm, and because a fortress is rarely devoid of a Muslim, so if one refrained on account of him, the door would be closed.' [3] His saying: 'or they use him as a shield': meaning the Muslim; tatarruq is taking cover with a shield, and making something a shield. He said in al-Hidayah: 'If they use Muslim children or captives as shields, they are not refrained from shooting them, and they aim at the disbelievers with the shooting, because if actual distinction is impossible, then intended distinction is possible, and obedience is according to capacity. And whatever they hit of them, there is no blood money upon them nor expiation, because jihad is an obligation, and penalties are not attached to obligations, unlike the case of extreme hunger, because one does not refrain for fear of liability, since there is saving one's own life. As for jihad, it is based on destroying life, so one refrains for fear of liability.' [4] His saying: 'without excuse...' its basis is what is established from the Prophet that he used to say to those he sent on expeditions: 'Fight in the name of God, in the way of God, fight those who disbelieve in God, fight and do not be excessive and do not...'
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the rules of warfare against non-believers who have received the call to Islam. It opens with the ruling that if the enemy refuses the call to Islam or jizya, they are to be fought using all necessary force, including mangonels (catapults), burning, and flooding. The commentator clarifies that this applies to those who may accept jizya, but for apostates and Arab idol-worshippers, only Islam is accepted; if they refuse, they are fought. The mangonel is described as a stone-throwing siege engine, and its use is justified by the Prophet's example at Ta'if and cutting the palm-trees of Banu al-Nadir. The proof is the Quranic verse (59:5) about cutting palm-trees. Then the passage addresses the situation where Muslims (captives or traders) are present among the enemy: the ruling is that it is still permissible to shoot and attack, because repelling the general harm to Islam outweighs the specific harm to those Muslims. If the enemy uses Muslim children or captives as human shields, the Muslims do not stop fighting but aim at the disbelievers. If Muslims are killed unintentionally in the process, there is no blood money or expiation, because jihad is a collective obligation and penalties are not attached to obligations. The commentator contrasts this with the case of extreme hunger where one may kill another to save oneself but must pay compensation. Finally, cutting trees and spoiling crops without excuse is prohibited based on the Prophet's command not to be excessive. The overall message is that in jihad, the defense of the Muslim community takes priority over individual lives when necessary.

Key Terms
منجنيق (manjanīq): A large stone-throwing siege engine used to breach fortress walls.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary Quranic proof for the permissibility of burning and flooding?
A) Surah al-Anfal (8:60) about preparing force.
B) Surah al-Hashr (59:5) about cutting palm-trees.
C) Surah al-Baqarah (2:190) about fighting in the way of God.
D) Surah al-Tawbah (9:5) about killing polytheists.

Key Takeaway
The Quranic permission to cut palm-trees and the Prophet's use of mangonels establish that all necessary force, including fire and water, is permissible in jihad to break the enemy's power.

Jihad — Armed Conflict Rules

When Muslims Are Present: The Ruling on Attacking Despite Muslim Shields
Commentary on ruling when Muslims are present among the enemy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling that permits attacking even when Muslims are present among the enemy, and state the consequences regarding blood money and expiation.

Core Idea
In jihad, repelling general harm to the Muslim community takes priority over preventing specific harm to individual Muslims; no compensation is due for accidental killing.

This lesson addresses a challenging scenario: the enemy holds Muslim captives or uses Muslim children as shields. The Hanafi ruling allows the Muslim army to continue attacking, aiming at the disbelievers, and if Muslims are killed unintentionally, no blood money or expiation is required. The justification is rooted in the principle of repelling general harm and the nature of jihad as an obligation.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario
The enemy has Muslim captives or uses Muslim children as shields. What is the ruling?
This is a common tactic to deter attack.
2
Identify the ruling
It is permissible to shoot at the enemy, aiming at the disbelievers. No diyah or kaffarah for accidental Muslim deaths.
The ruling is clear: the army does not stop fighting.
3
Locate the principle
The principle: repelling general harm (defending Islam) takes precedence over specific harm (killing a Muslim). Also, jihad is an obligation, and penalties are not attached to obligations.
This is a rule of priority in fiqh.
4
Note the exception
This contrasts with the case of extreme hunger (makhmasa) where compensation is due. Why? Because in hunger, one saves one's own life, which is not an obligation to destroy life.
The difference is the basis of the act.
5
Evaluate the thamarah
Practically, this ruling allows the Muslim army to continue the siege even if Muslims are inside, preventing the enemy from using them as a bargaining chip.
The enemy cannot exploit Muslim presence to avoid attack.

كتاب الجهاد / حكمه وكيفيته
لمن بلغته فإن أبوا حوربوا بمنجنيق، وتحريق، وتغريق
لمن بلغته فإن أبوا: أي عن الجزية، حوربوا بمنجنيق، وتحريق، وتغريق، المصطلق وأهل خيبر فإنّ الدعوة كانت بلغتهم.
[1] قوله: فإن أبوا؛ هذا في حق من تقبل منه الجزية، ومن لا تقبل منه، كالمرتدين وعبدة الأوثان من العرب لا فائدة في دعائهم إلى الجزية؛ لأنه لا يقبل منهم إلا الإسلام، فإن أبوا عن الإسلام حوربوا، كذا في «الهداية».
[2] قوله: بمنجنيق؛ - بفتح الميم، وفتح الجيم، بعدها نون مكسورة، بعدها ياء مثناة تحتية ساكنة، ثم قاف –، هو معرب من: جيه نيك؛ أي ما أجود حالي، وهو آلة ترمى بها الحجارة الكبار على الجدران والحصون. قال في «غياث اللغات: نوع از فلاخن بزراً که بر سوجوبي قولي تعبيه کنند وسنگهاي کلان دران نهاده بر دیوار قلعه زده دیوار في شكنندر واين معرب من جه نيك ست والادر خاص عربي جيم وقاف در هيج كلمه نياسده وجون در زمانه سابق زين آله بجهت قلعة كيري كمال مفيد بود لهذا تفاخراً باین اسم مسمى گشت.
(3) قوله: وتحريق؛ أي تحريق أنفسهم وأموالهم بالنار، وتفريق أموالهم وأنفسهم بالماء؛ وذلك لأنّ في مثل هذه الأشياء إلحاق الغيظ بهم وكسر لشوكتهم وتفريق جمعهم؛ ولذا جاز قطع الأشجار وإفساد الزروع. والأصل فيه قوله: مَا قَطَعْتُم مِّن لِينَةٍ أَوْ تَرَكْتُمُوهَا فَأَيْمَةٌ عَلَى أُصُولِهَا فَبِإِذْنِ اللَّهِ وَلِيُخْزِي الْفَاسِقِينَ )، وقد أخرج أبو داود في «كتاب المراسيل»، وابن سعد في «الطبقات»: «إنّ النبي الله نصب المجانيق على الطائف حين حاصر بأهله». وثبت في الصحاح الستة: «إنه قطع نخل بني النضير وهم طائفة من اليهود حين أجلاهم من المدينة. وحرق
ورمي، ولو معهم مسلم أو تترسوا به بنيتهم لا بنيته، وقطع شجر، وإفساد زرع بلا عذر

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the rules of warfare against non-believers who have received the call to Islam. It opens with the ruling that if the enemy refuses the call to Islam or jizya, they are to be fought using all necessary force, including mangonels (catapults), burning, and flooding. The proof is the Quranic verse (59:5) about cutting palm-trees. Key Terms

Jihad — Armed Conflict Rules

Ruling on Attacking Enemies When Muslims Are Present or Used as Shields
Statement of ruling with proof on permissible attack despite Muslim shields

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on attacking enemy fortifications containing Muslim prisoners or human shields, explain the rationale of general versus specific harm, and identify the legal consequences (no diyah/kaffarah).

Core Idea
Attacking when Muslims are among the enemy is permissible to repel general harm; no penalty for accidental killing because jihad is fard.

This passage from al-Hidayah addresses a critical dilemma in jihad: when the enemy uses Muslim prisoners or children as human shields, can the Muslim army still attack? The Hanafi school permits it, based on the overriding necessity of defending the Muslim community and the principle that public welfare outweighs private harm. The passage also clarifies the rules on destroying enemy resources.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
State when attacking is permissible despite Muslim presence according to the Hanafi school.
The ruling is that there is no harm (la ba'sa) in throwing weapons even if Muslims are present.
2
Understand the reasoning: general vs specific harm
Explain why repelling general harm (defense of Islam) takes precedence over specific harm (killing the Muslim prisoner or trader).
The text uses the principle that forts rarely lack Muslims, so forbidding attack would close the door to jihad.
3
Locate the Quranic and hadith proofs
Find the Quranic verse (al-Anfal:60) and the hadith on conduct in war that support the ruling.
The verse commands preparation of power; the hadith prohibits treachery and mutilation but does not forbid attacking when Muslims are present.
4
Note the exception for cutting trees and crops
Identify when destruction of property is prohibited (without excuse) and when it may be permissible.
The Prophet's instruction to not cut down palm trees unless necessary indicates that wanton destruction is haram.
5
Recognize the absence of penalties
Understand why no diyah (blood-money) or kaffarah (expiation) is due for accidentally killing Muslim shields.
Jihad is a fard, and penalties are not incurred when performing obligations, unlike the case of self-preservation (makhmasa).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورمي ، ولو معهم مسلم أو تترسوا به بنيتهم لا بنيته، وقطع شجر، وإفسادِ زرع بلا عذر قوله : ورمى أي بالرماح ونحوها كالات الرصاص المستعملة في زماننا ونحو ذلك والأصل فيه قوله : وَأَعِدُّوا لَهُم مَّا اسْتَطَعْتُم مِّن قُوَّةٍ وَمِن رِبَاطِ الْخَيْلِ ترهبون بِهِ عَدُوّ اللَّهِ وَعَدُوكُمْ قوله : ولو معهم الواو وصليّة قال في الهداية لا بأس برميهم وإن كان فيهم مسلم أسير أو تاجر لأن في الرمي دفع الضرر العام بالذب عن بيضة الإسلام وقتل الأسير والتاجر ضرر خاص ولأنه قلما يخلو حصن عن مسلم فلو امتنع باعتباره لانسد بابه قوله : أو تترسوا به أي بالمسلم التترس التستر بالترس وجعل شيء ترساً قال في الهداية إن تترسوا بصبيان المسلمين أو بالأسارى لم يكفوا عن رميهم ويقصدون بالرمي الكفار لأنه إن تعذر التمييز فعلاً فلقد أمكن قصداً والطاعة بحسب الطاقة وما أصابوه منهم لا دية عليهم ولا كفارة لأن الجهاد فرض والغرامات لا تقرن بالفروض بخلاف حالة المخمصة لأنه لا يمتنع مخافة الضمان لما فيه من إحياء نفسه أما الجهاد فمبني على إتلاف النفس فيمتنع حذراً عن الضمان قوله بلا عذر الخ الأصل فيه ما ثبت عن النبي أنه كان يقول لمن يبعث في السرايا اغزوا باسم الله في سبيل الله قاتلوا من كفر بالله اغزوا ولا تغلوا ولا تعذروا ولا تمنوا ولا تقتلوا وليدا وما قطعت من لينة أو تركتموها قائمة على أصولها فبإذن الله وليجزي الفاسقين في صحيح البخاري وصحيح مسلم وغيرها
And throwing, even if there is with them a Muslim or they shield themselves with him intending them not intending him, and cutting trees, and spoiling crops without excuse. His statement: 'and throwing' meaning with spears and the like, like the lead guns used in our time and the like. Its basis is His statement: 'And prepare against them whatever you can of power and horses to terrify thereby the enemy of Allah and your enemy.' His statement: 'even if there is with them' the waw is conjunctive. He said in al-Hidayah: There is no harm in throwing at them even if among them there is a Muslim prisoner or trader, because in throwing is repelling general harm by defending the fold of Islam, while killing the prisoner and trader is specific harm, and because a fortress is rarely devoid of a Muslim, so if one refrains due to him, its door would be closed. His statement: 'or they shield themselves with him' meaning with the Muslim; al-tatarrus is seeking cover with a shield or making something a shield. He said in al-Hidayah: If they shield themselves with Muslim children or prisoners, they are not refrained from throwing at them, and they intend with the throwing the disbelievers; because if actual discrimination is impossible, it is possible intentionally, and obedience is according to ability. Whatever of them they hit, there is no blood-money nor expiation for them, because jihad is an obligation and penalties are not associated with obligations, contrary to the case of severe hunger, because one does not refrain for fear of liability, as it involves saving one's life. As for jihad, it is based on destroying the soul, so one refrains out of caution from liability. His statement: 'without excuse' etc. Its basis is what is established from the Prophet that he used to say to those he sent on expeditions: 'Fight in the name of Allah, in the path of Allah, fight those who disbelieve in Allah, fight and do not transgress, and do not be treacherous, and do not mutilate, and do not kill a child. And whatever you cut of the palm trees or left them standing on their roots, it is by permission of Allah and that He may disgrace the rebellious.' Recorded in Sahih al-Bukhari and Sahih Muslim and others.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on attacking the enemy when Muslims are present among them or used as human shields, and also on cutting trees and spoiling crops. It begins by stating the general ruling: it is permissible to throw weapons at the enemy even if there are Muslim prisoners or traders with them, or if they shield themselves with Muslims, provided the intention is to target the disbelievers, not the Muslims. The proof is from the Quran: 'And prepare against them whatever you can of power' (al-Anfal: 60). From al-Hidayah, the reasoning is that repelling the general harm to the Muslim community takes precedence over the specific harm to the Muslim individual, since forts almost always contain Muslims and stopping would close the door to jihad. When Muslims are used as shields (tatarrus), the ruling is the same: they may be attacked, aiming at the disbelievers, but if it is not possible to distinguish, the intention suffices. There is no diyah (blood-money) or kaffarah (expiation) for accidentally killing such Muslims because jihad is an obligation (fard) and financial penalties are not incurred when fulfilling obligations, unlike the case of pressing hunger where killing to save oneself may incur liability. As for cutting trees and spoiling crops without valid reason (such as military necessity), it is prohibited based on the Prophet's command to not cut down fruit-bearing trees etc. The hadith is cited from Sahih al-Bukhari and Muslim. The passage thus establishes the Hanafi position that jihad allows necessary destruction in war but forbids wanton destruction.

Key Terms
رمى (ramā): To throw weapons (spears, guns, etc.) at the enemy.
تترس (tatarrus): Using someone as a human shield; seeking cover with a shield.
غرامات (gharāmāt): Financial penalties such as diyah (blood-money) and kaffarah (expiation).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling on attacking an enemy fortress that contains Muslim prisoners?
A) Not permissible under any condition
B) Permissible but with intention to kill Muslims
C) Permissible aiming at disbelievers, no diyah if Muslims killed
D) Permissible only if no other way

Key Takeaway
Attacking the enemy when Muslims are present is permitted in the Hanafi school to protect the wider community, and accidental Muslim casualties incur no worldly liability.

Ghulul and Muthlah: Definitions and Hanafi Rulings
Definition of ghulul and distinction from khud'ah, ruling on muthlah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish ghulul (treachery) from khud'ah (war deception) and state the Hanafi ruling on muthlah (mutilation) in combat.

Core Idea
Treachery is forbidden; war deception is permissible; mutilation is prohibited only when initially intended.

This passage from al-Hidayah and al-Fath clarifies two key rulings in the law of jihad: the absolute prohibition of treachery (ghulul) and the nuanced ruling on mutilation (muthlah). It also resolves a common confusion between treachery and permissible war deception (khud'ah). Understanding these distinctions is essential for correctly applying the Shari'ah in military conduct.

Guided Path
1
Define ghulul
Locate the definition of ghulul in the passage: it is khiyanah (betrayal) and naqd al-ahd (breaking of covenant). Note that this is forbidden.
2
Identify the hadith on war deception
The passage cites the hadith 'al-harbu khud'ah' (war is deception). Understand that this is a legitimate stratagem, not treachery.
The hadith is authentic (mutawatir in meaning).
3
Distinguish ghulul from khud'ah
The passage warns against confusing the two. Ghulul involves betrayal of trust or covenant; khud'ah is a tactic against the enemy without breaking a covenant.
Deception in war is allowed, but breaking a treaty is not.
4
Analyze the ruling on muthlah
The prohibition of mutilation applies only when intended as an initial act. If it occurs during combat (e.g., a duelist causes injuries), it is permissible.
The example of sequential injuries in a duel illustrates the point.
5
Apply the rule on retaliation
The passage from al-Fath adds that if a disbeliever injures multiple Muslims, each retaliation is delayed until the previous wound heals, showing mutilation is not punished when it results from legitimate qisas.
This reinforces the combat exception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الجهاد / حكمه وكيفيته وغلول، ومثلة) قال في الهداية الغدر الخيانة ونقض العهد وقد قال الحرب خدعة فتشتبه على الناس التفرقة بين الغدر وبين خدعة الحرب تعذروا ولا تمنوا ولا تقتلوا وليدا أخرجه مسلم وأصحاب السنن قوله ومثلة النهي عنها إنما هو إذا كانت ابتداء قصداً فأما إذا وقعت قتالاً فلا بأس به کمبارز ضرب فقطع إذنه ثم ضرب فقطع يده ثم ضرب ففقأ عينه وهكذا ولو جنى كافر على جماعة بأن قطع أنف رجل وأُذُنَي رجل ورجل آخر وفقاً عيني آخر فإنه يقتص منه لكل لكن يستأتى لكل قصاص إلى برء ما قبله كذا في الفتح قوله الحرب خدعة بفتح الخاء المعجمة وبضمها وسكون الدال المهملة هذا الحديث أخرجه الشيخان البخاري ومسلم وغيرهما ومن هذا الباب ما ثبت عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه كان إذا أراد غزوة ورى بغيرها أخرجه البخاري وغيره فإن الخدع عبارة عن إظهار أمر خلاف ما في الباطن قوله فتشتبه يعني يحتج في هذا المقام أنه لا فرق بين الخدع وبين الغدر فلمّا جاز الخدع بالنص فما بال المنع من الغدر ومنشأ هذا الاختلاف هو الاشتباه بين الخدع والغدر وعدم ظهور الفرق على كثير من الناس
Book of Jihad: Its ruling and manner, and regarding ghulul and muthlah. He said in al-Hidayah: Ghulul is treachery and breaking of covenant. And it has been said: War is deception. So it is confusing to people to differentiate between treachery and war deception. Do not mutilate and do not kill a child. Recorded by Muslim and the authors of Sunan. His statement: 'and muthlah': the prohibition of it is only when it is initially intended; but if it occurs during combat there is no harm, such as a duelist striking and cutting his ear, then striking and cutting his hand, then striking and gouging his eye, and so on. And if a disbeliever assaulted a group, cutting the nose of one man, the ears of another man and another man, and gouging the eyes of another, then retaliation is taken from him for each, but each retaliation is delayed until the healing of the previous one. Thus in al-Fath. His statement: 'war is deception' (with fatha and damma on the kha, sukun on the dal): this hadith is recorded by the two Shaykhs, Bukhari and Muslim, and others. From this chapter is what is established from the Prophet that when he intended a battle he would give a false impression of something else, recorded by Bukhari and others. For deception consists of showing something contrary to what is in the inner self. His statement: 'so it is confusing': meaning one argues in this context that there is no difference between deception and treachery. Since deception is permitted by text, why then is treachery prohibited? The origin of this confusion is the confusion between deception and treachery, and the lack of clarity of the difference to many people.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage defines ghulul (treachery) and clarifies the prohibition of muthlah (mutilation). It begins by quoting al-Hidayah: ghulul is khiyanah (betrayal) and naqd al-ahd (breaking of covenant). Then it presents the hadith 'al-harbu khud'ah' (war is deception), which might cause confusion: if deception is allowed, why is treachery forbidden? The passage then addresses this by showing the difference: ghulul is betrayal of trust or covenant, while khud'ah is legitimate stratagem in war. The prohibition of muthlah is explained: it is forbidden when done initially as a goal, but if it occurs naturally in combat (e.g., a duelist injures the opponent progressively), it is not blameworthy. A case from al-Fath is given: if a disbeliever injures multiple Muslims, retaliation (qisas) can be taken for each injury, but each retaliation must wait until the previous wound heals. This shows that mutilation is not prohibited when it is part of legitimate retaliation during battle. The passage thus clarifies the Hanafi position that war stratagems are permissible while treachery is not, and that mutilation is prohibited as an initial act but not when it results from combat.

Key Terms
الغدر (al-ghadr): Treachery, betrayal, breaking of covenant (naqd al-ahd).
المثلة (al-muthlah): Mutilation of the body, especially of the dead or during combat.
الخدعة (al-khud'ah): Deception, strategy in war.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It must occur only when the enemy is dead.
C) It must occur as an incidental result of combat, not as an initial intention.
D) It must be ordered by a qualified ruler.

Key Takeaway
Ghulul is haram because it breaks a covenant; khud'ah is permissible; muthlah is haram when done intentionally but not if it occurs in combat.

Ruling on Mutilation (Muthlah) in War
Explains prohibition of intentional mutilation and allowance during combat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between prohibited intentional mutilation and permissible incidental mutilation during combat according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Intentional mutilation is haram; incidental mutilation in combat is allowed.

In this passage, the Hanafi school clarifies a key distinction in the ethics of warfare: mutilation (muthlah) is prohibited only when done with premeditation. However, if it occurs naturally during battle—such as in a duel—it is not blameworthy. This nuanced ruling reflects the school's approach of balancing prohibition with practical realities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default prohibition
The text states that mutilation (muthlah) is prohibited. This is the default ruling based on prophetic tradition.
The default ruling is haram.
2
Apply the exception: if it occurs during combat
The prohibition applies only when mutilation is intentional from the beginning (ibtida'an qasdan). If it happens spontaneously in the heat of battle, it is not blameworthy.
Example: a duel where ears, hands, eyes are cut off successively.
3
Note the qisas rule for multiple victims
If a disbeliever injures multiple Muslims, retribution (qisas) is due for each, but each execution is delayed until the previous wound heals, to ensure precise retaliation.
This follows the Hanafi principle of exact justice in qisas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ومثله؛ النهي عنها إنما هو إذا كانت ابتداء قصداً، فأما إذا وقعت قتالاً فلا بأس به كمبارز ضرب فقطع إذنه، ثمّ ضرب فقطع يده، ثمّ ضرب ففقأ عينه وهكذا، ولو جنى كافر على جماعة بأن قطع أنف رجلٍ وأُذُنَي رجل ورجل آخر، وفقاً عيني آخر، فإنّه يقتص منه لكلّ لكن يُستأتى لكل قصاص إلى برء ما قبله. كذا في «الفتح»
His statement: 'and al-muthlah'; the prohibition of it is only when it is intentional from the beginning. However, if it occurs during combat, there is no harm in it, like a dueler who strikes and cuts off his ear, then strikes and cuts off his hand, then strikes and gouges out his eye, and so on. And if a disbeliever injures a group by cutting the nose of one man, the ears of another man and the eyes of another, then retribution is taken from him for each, but each retribution is delayed until the previous one has healed. This is as in al-Fath.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage clarifies the ruling on mutilation (muthlah) in jihad. The author states that the prohibition of mutilation applies only when it is performed with the initial intention and as a primary act. However, if mutilation occurs during the heat of battle without premeditation, it is not blameworthy. He gives the example of a duel where combatants exchange blows: if one cuts off the other's ear, then hand, then eye, it is part of fighting. He then discusses a case where a disbeliever injures multiple Muslims: retribution (qisas) is due for each injury, but execution is delayed until each previous wound has healed to ensure the exact measure of retaliation. This ruling follows the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
المثلة (al-muthlah): The act of disfiguring or cutting off body parts of a living being after incapacitation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) It is never prohibited

Key Takeaway
Hanafi fiqh prohibits mutilation only when intentional and premeditated; incidental mutilation during combat is permissible.

Ghadr vs Khid'at al-Harb: Covenant Makes the Difference
Commentary on al-Hidayah clarifying the distinction between ghadr and khid'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between ghadr (treachery) and khid'at al-harb (war deception) and state the Hanafi ruling for each based on the presence of a covenant.

Core Idea
War deception is permissible unless a covenant exists; breaking a covenant is treachery.

In the heat of battle, not every deceptive act is lawful. The Hanafi school draws a sharp line between permissible wartime tactics and forbidden treachery. This lesson unpacks that line, anchored in a prophetic hadith that protected covenants even during conflict. Understanding this distinction is critical for correctly applying the rules of engagement in jihad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two acts
Recognize the two scenarios: deception during ongoing war (khid'ah) and breaking a covenant (ghadr). The ruling differs based on the presence of an explicit agreement.
2
State the Hanafi ruling for each
Khid'ah is mubah (permissible); ghadr is haram (forbidden). The text says 'deception is not prohibited' as long as war continues, but breaking a covenant is prohibited.
3
Locate the dalil
The proof for the prohibition of ghadr is the hadith reported by Abu Dawud, Tirmidhi, and Nasa'i: 'Whoever has a treaty... must not tighten a knot nor loosen it until its term expires or he casts it back fairly.'
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but legally binding.
4
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith explicitly forbids tightening or loosening a covenant, which includes honoring it and not violating it. Breaking a covenant (ghadr) is thus prohibited. The permissibility of khid'ah is derived from the general principle 'al-harbu khud'ah' and the absence of a covenant.
5
Apply the distinction
If there is no covenant, deception is allowed. If there is a covenant (even a temporary ceasefire), deception becomes ghadr and is haram. The decisive factor is the existence of a formal agreement.
This principle prevents treachery while allowing tactical flexibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأقول : ما دام الحرب قائمة لا يحرم الخداع، بأن نريهم أنا لا نحاربهم في هذا اليوم حتى أمنوا فنحاربهم فيه ، أو نذهب إلى صوب آخر حتى غفلوا فنأتيهم بياتاً ونحو ذلك ، بخلاف ما إذا جرى بيننا وبينهم قرار على أن لا نتحارب في هذا اليوم حتى أمنوا ، فإنَّه لا تجوز المحاربة ؛ لأنَّ هذا استثمان وعهد، فالمحاربة نقض العهد، وهذا ليس من خداع الحرب، بل خداع في حال السلم، فيكون غدراً. قوله : فأقول ; أي في بيان التفرقة بينهما ، بحيث يظهر جواز أحدهما وعدم جواز الآخر، وحاصلها : إنّ الغدر عبارة عن نقض العهد الذي جرى بيننا وبينهم، والصلح الذي تقرر فيما بيننا وبينهم. فقد أخرج أبو داود والتِّرْمِذِيُّ والنسائي: كان بين معاوية له وبين الروم عهد ، وكان يسير نحو بلادهم، حتى إذا انقضى العهد غزاهم، فجاء رجل على فرس أو بزدون وهو يقول : الله أكبر الله أكبر وفاء لا غدر، فنظروا فإذا عمرو بن عبسة، فأرسل إليه معاوية فسأله، فقال : سمعت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يقول : مَن كان بينه وبين قوم عهد فلا يسد عقدة، ولا يحلّها حتى ينقضي أمدها ، أو ينبذ إليهم على سواء، فرجع معاوية بالناس. وأما الخداع في الحرب فهو عبارة عمّا يخدعُ به حال قيام الحرب ليعين على قتالهم.
I say: As long as war is ongoing, deception is not prohibited. For example, we show them that we will not fight them on that day until they feel secure, then we fight them; or we go to another direction until they become heedless, then we attack them at night, and so on. Contrary to when an agreement is made between us and them not to fight on that day until they feel secure, then fighting is not permissible because this is seeking safety and a covenant. So fighting would be breaking the covenant, which is not part of war deception but deception during peacetime, thus it is treachery. His statement: 'I say'; meaning in explaining the difference between the two, so that the permissibility of one and impermissibility of the other becomes clear. The summary is: Treachery (ghadr) is breaking a covenant made between us and them, and the peace agreement made between us and them. It is reported by Abu Dawud, al-Tirmidhi, and al-Nasa'i: There was a treaty between Mu'awiyah and the Byzantines, and he was marching towards their lands. When the treaty ended, he attacked them. Then a man came on a horse or a mule saying: 'Allahu Akbar, Allahu Akbar, fulfillment not treachery.' They looked and saw 'Amr ibn 'Abasah. Mu'awiyah sent for him and asked him. He said: 'I heard the Messenger of Allah say: Whoever has a treaty between him and a people must not tighten a knot nor loosen it until its term expires or he throws it back to them with fairness.' So Mu'awiyah returned with the people. As for deception in war, it is what one uses to deceive during ongoing war to aid in fighting them.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah distinguishes between two concepts: ghadr (treachery/breaking covenant) and khid'ah (war deception). The author states that deception is permissible as long as hostilities are ongoing. Examples include falsely indicating you will not fight that day, or feigning retreat to attack later. However, if there is an explicit temporary ceasefire or covenant, violating it is ghadr—prohibited. The author cites a hadith where the Prophet forbade breaking a covenant before its term or without proper annulment. Thus, the key difference is the existence of a black/white. This is a critical distinction for jihad rulings in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
الغدر (al-ghadr): Treachery: breaking a covenant or peace agreement during war.
خدعة الحرب (khid'at al-harb): War deception: tactics to mislead the enemy without violating an explicit covenant.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on deceiving the enemy during war when no covenant exists?
A) Haram
B) Makruh
C) Mubah (permissible)
D) Wajib

Key Takeaway
The key distinction is the presence of a covenant: war deception is permissible without a covenant, but with a covenant, it becomes forbidden treachery.

Definition and Prohibition of Ghalul (Embezzling Booty)
Defines ghalul and cites its Quranic prohibition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of ghalul, identify its prohibition, and explain the consequence per Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
Ghalul is secretly taking from the spoils before the imam's distribution — it is haram and a major sin.

The term 'ghalul' appears in the Quran and refers to embezzling war booty before proper allocation. This lesson unpacks its linguistic meaning, legal definition, and the strong prohibition established by a clear Quranic verse. Understanding ghalul is essential for anyone studying the laws of jihad and public trust in Islamic governance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic form
Note the word 'ghulul' (with damma on ghain) is on the measure of muf'al, meaning booty. This is a verbal noun indicating the act of taking from the spoils.
The root gh-l-l conveys the idea of treachery or taking secretly.
2
Understand the legal definition
Ghalul is defined as taking something from the ghanimah (spoils) secretly without the imam's permission. The permission of the imam is key because distribution is his authority.
This excludes open taking or permissible gifts from the imam.
3
Locate the evidence
The Quranic verse (3:161) states: 'And whoever embezzles shall come on the Day of Resurrection with what he embezzled.' This is a direct prohibition.
The verse threatens punishment in the Hereafter, indicating the gravity.
4
State the ruling and consequence
Per Hanafi fiqh, ghalul is haram. The perpetrator must return the stolen item and is subject to ta'zir (discretionary punishment). The sin also nullifies any right to a share from the booty.
This is agreed upon by all schools (muttafaq alayh).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والغُلُول : السرقة من المغنم. قوله : والغُلُول ; بضمن الغين المهملة، واللام الأولى : السرقة من المغنم، وهو على وزن مفعل بمعنى الغنيمة، يعني أخذ شيء من مال الغنيمة خفية بدون إذن الإمام، وهو المراد في قوله : وَمَن يَغْلُلْ يَأْتِ بِمَا ظَلَّ يَوْمَ الْقِيَمَةِ
And al-ghulul: stealing from the booty. His statement: 'and al-ghulul'; with the letter ghain having a damma, and the first lam: stealing from the booty. It is on the measure of muf'al meaning booty, i.e., taking something from the spoils secretly without the imam's permission. This is what is meant in His statement: 'And whoever embezzles shall come on the Day of Resurrection with what he embezzled.'
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage defines ghalul, a term for embezzlement from the war booty before distribution. The author explains the linguistic form: ghulul (with damma on ghain) is on the pattern of muf'al, meaning booty. The act is taking something from the ghanimah (spoils) secretly without the permission of the imam. The prohibition is supported by the Quranic verse from Surah Al Imran (3:161). In Hanafi fiqh, ghalul is a major sin and nullifies the one's share in the booty; it also carries worldly consequences such as returning the stolen item and being punished.

Key Terms
الغلول (al-ghulul): Stealing from the spoils of war before the official distribution by the imam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
