A) Taking booty openly with the imam's permission
B) Stealing from the booty before distribution, secretly and without the imam's permission
C) Any form of theft in a military context
D) Refusing to participate in jihad

Key Takeaway
Ghalul is haram and requires restitution; it is defined as secretly taking from the booty before the imam's distribution.

Abrogation of the 'Uraniyyin Hadith and the Prohibition of Muthla
Examination of naskh regarding the hadith of al-'Uraniyyin

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that the 'Uraniyyin hadith is abrogated by Qur'an 5:33 and evaluate the opposing argument based on the verse's scope.

Core Idea
The 'Uraniyyin mutilation punishment was abrogated by Qur'an 5:33; muthla is haram except in lawful qisas.

The hadith of al-'Uraniyyin describes a severe punishment involving mutilation. This lesson examines whether that ruling was abrogated by the later revelation of the hirabah verse (5:33). Understanding this naskh is essential for the Hanafi ruling that muthla (mutilation) is absolutely prohibited, with the only exception being lawful retaliation for identical wounds.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify that the Hanafi school holds that the 'Uraniyyin hadith is abrogated by the verse of hirabah (5:33). The Prophet's later practice confirms this.
The hadith involved cutting off hands and feet and gouging out eyes.
2
Identify the opposing view
Some scholars, like Abu al-Fath al-Ya'muri, argue that the hadith is not abrogated because the 'Uraniyyin committed additional crimes (apostasy, murder) beyond hirabah, so the verse does not restrict punishment.
They distinguish between muthla (initial mutilation) and retaliation.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The opponent points to the word 'innama' (only) in the verse, arguing it restricts punishment for hirabah but does not forbid additional punishment for separate crimes.
They also cite disagreement over the cause of revelation of the verse.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response uses the same 'innama' to argue for restriction of punishment; additional penalties beyond hirabah are not part of the hadd. They also rely on the report that after the verse, the Prophet never again ordered mutilation.
The Waqidi report confirms the abrogation.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
If abrogated, muthla is prohibited except in lawful qisas. If not, mutilation could be justified for multiple crimes. The Hanafi tarjih favors abrogation.
This difference has practical implications for Islamic criminal law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نسخت بقوله: «لاَ تَغْلُوا وَلَا تَغْدِرُوا وَلَا تُمَتِّلُوا». [١] قوله: نسخت ; قال الزَّيْلَعِيُّ في «نصب الراية»: «قال قتادة: وبُلغنا أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم كان بعد ذلك يحث على الصدقة وينهى عن المثلة، وفي لفظ لهما أي للبخاري ومسلم، قال قتادة: فحدثني محمد بن سيرين له: «إن ذلك قبل أن تنزل الحدود، وفي لفظ للبيهقي: قال أنس: فما خطبنا رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم بعد هذا خطبة إلا نهى فيها عن المثلة» (۳). (۲) وقال في «المعرفة»: حديث العُرْنِيين إما أن يحمل على النسخ كما روى عن ابن سيرين، وقتادة، وبه قال الشافعي، أو يحمل على أنه فعل بهم ما فعلوا بالرعاء، وقد جاء مصرحاً عند مسلم عن أنس له قال: «إنما سمل النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أعين أولئك لأنهم سملوا أعين الرعاة» (4). وقال أبو الفتح اليعمري في «سيرته»: من الناسِ مَن زعم أن حديث العُرْنيين منسوخ بآية المائدة: (إِنَّمَا جَزَاؤُا الَّذِينَ يُحَارِبُونَ اللهَ وَرَسُولَهُ) (٥) الآية، ومن الناس من أبى ذلك لما وقع الاختلاف في سبب نزولها، وقد ذكر البغوي وغيره لها قصة أخرى، وأيضاً فليس فيها أكثر مما تشعر به لفظة: «إنما» من الاقتصار في حد الحرابة على ما في الآية، وأما من زاد على الحرابة جنايات أخر، كما فعل أولئك، حيث زادوا بالردة وسمل أعين الرعاء وغير ذلك. وروى ابن سعد في خبرهم: إنهم قطعوا يد الراعي ورجله، وغرزوا الشوك في لسانه وعينيه حتى مات، فليس في الآية ما يمنع من التغليظ عليهم والزيادة في عقوبتهم، فهذا ليس بمثل، والمثلة ما كان ابتداء من غير جزاء. وقد جاء في «صحيح مسلم»: «إنما سمل النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أعينهم لأنهم سملوا أعين الرعاء، ولو أن شخصا بغى على قوم جنايات في أعضاء متعددة فاقتص منه للمجني عليه لما كان التشويه الذي حصل من المثلة المنهي عنها، وإذا اختلفت في نزول الآية الأقوال، وتطرق إليها الاحتمال فلا نسخ. انتهى كلامه. قلت: ومما يدل على نسخ حديث العُرْنيين بالآية ما رواه الواقدي في كتاب «المغازي»: حدثني إسحاق عن صالح مولى التوأمة عن أبي هريرة له قال: «لما قطع النبي أيدي أصحاب اللقاح وأرجلهم وسمل أعينهم نزلت هذه الآية: إِنَّمَا جَزَاؤُا الَّذِينَ يُحَارِبُونَ اللَّهَ وَرَسُولَهُ الآية، فلم تسمل بعد ذلك عين، قال: وحدثني أبو جعفر: قال ما بعث النبي الله بعد ذلك بعثاً إلا نهاهم عن المثلة». انتهى كلام الزيلعي
It was abrogated by his saying: 'Do not transgress, do not act treacherously, and do not mutilate.' [1] His saying: 'Abrogated'; al-Zayla'i said in Nasb al-Raya: Qatada said: 'And it reached us that the Prophet, peace be upon him, after that used to urge charity and forbid mutilation.' In one version from al-Bukhari and Muslim, Qatada said: 'Muhammad ibn Sirin narrated to me that this was before the revelation of the prescribed punishments.' In a version from al-Bayhaqi, Anas said: 'After this, the Prophet never addressed us in a sermon except that he forbade mutilation in it.' And he said in al-Ma'rifa: The hadith of the 'Uraniyyin is either interpreted as abrogated, as narrated from Ibn Sirin and Qatada, and al-Shafi'i said so, or interpreted that he did to them as they did to the shepherds. And it is explicitly stated in Muslim from Anas: 'The Prophet only gouged out their eyes because they gouged out the eyes of the shepherds.' Abu al-Fath al-Ya'muri said in his Sira: Some people claim that the hadith of the 'Uraniyyin is abrogated by the verse of al-Ma'idah: 'The punishment of those who wage war against Allah and His Messenger...' the verse, but others refuse that because of the disagreement regarding its cause of revelation. Al-Baghawi and others mention another story for it. Also, there is nothing in it more than what the word 'only' indicates of restricting the punishment of hirabah to what is in the verse. As for those who added other crimes to hirabah, as those people did, adding apostasy and gouging out the eyes of the shepherds and other things, Ibn Sa'd narrated in their story that they cut off the hand and foot of the shepherd, and stuck thorns in his tongue and eyes until he died. There is nothing in the verse to prevent imposing a severe punishment on them and increasing their penalty, so this is not muthla, and muthla is what is done initially without retaliation. And it has come in Sahih Muslim: 'The Prophet only gouged out their eyes because they gouged out the eyes of the shepherds. And if a person attacks a people with injuries to multiple limbs, then retaliation is taken from him for the victim, the disfigurement that results is not from the prohibited muthla. And if the sayings differ regarding the revelation of the verse and probability enters upon it, then there is no abrogation.' His speech ends. I say: Evidence for the abrogation of the hadith of the 'Uraniyyin by the verse is what al-Waqidi narrated in al-Maghazi: Ishaq narrated to me from Salih, the freed slave of al-Tu'ama, from Abu Hurayra: 'When the Prophet cut off the hands and feet of the owners of the milk animals and gouged out their eyes, this verse was revealed: 'The punishment of those who wage war against Allah and His Messenger...' the verse, and after that no eye was gouged out.' He said: Abu Ja'far narrated to me: 'The Prophet never sent a military expedition after that except that he forbade them from mutilation.' End of al-Zayla'i's words.
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage examines the abrogation (naskh) of the hadith regarding the 'Uraniyyin (the people of Uraynah) by the Qur'anic verse on hirabah (5:33). The commentator cites al-Zayla'i, who reports from Qatada that after the incident, the Prophet encouraged charity and forbade mutilation. The hadith originally involved the Prophet punishing the 'Uraniyyin by cutting off their hands and feet and gouging out their eyes for their crime of killing the shepherds and stealing the camels. There is a disagreement: some scholars, including al-Shafi'i, hold that this punishment was abrogated by the verse of hirabah, which prescribes crucifixion, cutting off hands and feet from opposite sides, exile, or execution. Others argue that the verse does not abrogate because it does not preclude additional punishments for additional crimes like apostasy and murder. The author supports abrogation by citing a report from al-Waqidi that after the verse, the Prophet never again ordered gouging out of eyes. The discussion concludes that muthla (mutilation) is prohibited as it changes Allah's creation, except where permitted by the Lawgiver. This sets the stage for the ruling on muthla.

Key Terms
المثلة (al-muthla): Mutilation, disfigurement of a living or dead body, especially by cutting off limbs or gouging out eyes.
نسخ (naskh): Abrogation of a legal ruling by a later revelation or sunnah.
الحرابة (al-hirabah): Highway robbery or armed rebellion causing corruption on earth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main evidence cited by the Hanafi side for the abrogation of the 'Uraniyyin hadith?
A) The Prophet's hadith commanding charity
B) The report from al-Waqidi that after the verse, no eye was gouged out
C) The fact that the 'Uraniyyin were apostates
D) The word 'innama' in the verse means 'only'

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the 'Uraniyyin hadith is abrogated by Qur'an 5:33 and the Prophet's later practice, making muthla absolutely prohibited.

Prohibition of Muthla (Mutilation) in Jihad
Ruling on muthla and its prohibition based on Quran

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state why muthla is prohibited in Hanafi fiqh and identify the Quranic proof from Surah An-Nisa.

Core Idea
Muthla is haram because it changes Allah's creation, an act inspired by Satan.

This lesson examines the prohibition of mutilation (muthla) in jihad. The text cites a Quranic verse where Satan vows to command people to alter Allah's creation. The Hanafi school derives from this that any act changing Allah's creation is forbidden unless explicitly permitted by the Lawgiver. Understanding this ruling clarifies the ethical boundaries of warfare in Islam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is muthla? What is its hukm? The passage states it is haram because it involves changing Allah's creation.
2
Locate the dalil
Which Quranic verse is cited? Surah An-Nisa 4:118-119, where Satan says he will command people to change the creation of Allah.
The verse is a hadith qudsi narrated by the Prophet (peace be upon him).
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
How does the verse prove prohibition? Since changing Allah's creation is attributed to Satan's promptings, it is inherently prohibited.
The phrase 'فَلَيُغَيِّرُنَّ خَلْقَ اللهِ' is key.
4
Note the exception
Is muthla ever permissible? Yes, if the Lawgiver has specifically permitted it, such as in lawful qisas (retaliation).
The exception is inferred from 'إلا فيما أذن لنا الشارع به'.
5
Apply to jihad
How does this ruling apply in warfare? Muslims must not mutilate the enemy, whether alive or dead, because it is a satanic act.
This applies to both combatants and captives, living or deceased.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[١] قوله : وفي المثلة تغيير خلق الله فتحرم ; لقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم حكاية عن قول الشيطان حين صار رجيماً وملعونا: وَقَالَ لَأَتَّخِذَنَّ مِنْ عِبَادِكَ نَصِيبًا مَفْرُوضًا وَلَأُضِلَّنَهُمْ وَلَأُمَيِّيَنَّهُمْ وَلَا مُرَنَّهُمْ فَلَيُبَتِكُنَّ وَإِذَا الأَنْعَامِ وَلَا مُرتَهُمْ فَلَمينَ خَلْفَ الله الآية، فدلّت هذه الآية على أن تغيير خلق الله له من مـضلات إبليس، فيكون منهياً عنه إلا فيما أذن لنا الشارع به.
[1] His saying: 'And in mutilation is changing the creation of Allah, so it is forbidden'; because of his saying, peace be upon him, narrating from the saying of Satan when he became stoned and cursed: 'And he said, I will surely take from among Your servants a portion [to mislead them]. And I will mislead them, and I will arouse in them [sinful] desires, and I will command them so they will change the creation of Allah' (4:118-119). So this verse indicates that changing the creation of Allah is from the misguidance of Iblis, thus it is forbidden except in what the Lawgiver has permitted us.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage states the ruling that muthla (mutilation) is forbidden because it involves changing the creation of Allah. The proof is the Quranic verse where Satan says he will mislead people and command them to change Allah's creation. Since this is from the promptings of Satan, it is prohibited. The exception is cases where the Lawgiver has specifically permitted it, such as in lawful retaliation (qisas) for identical wounds, which is not considered muthla. This ruling is foundational in Hanafi jihad law: mutilating the enemy, whether living or dead, is a grave sin.

Key Terms
المثلة (al-muthla): Mutilation, disfigurement of the body, especially of the dead or the living enemy in war.
تغيير خلق الله (taghyir khalq Allah): Changing the natural creation of Allah, such as by mutilation or cosmetic alterations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is muthla (mutilation) prohibited?
A) It is wasteful
B) It changes Allah's creation, which is from Satan's promptings
C) It causes excessive pain
D) It is not mentioned in the Quran

Key Takeaway
Muthla is haram because it changes Allah's creation, an act of Satan, and only lawful qisas is exempted.

The Prohibition of Killing Non-Mukallaf During Jihad
Ruling on protected persons in jihad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on killing non-combatants in jihad and identify the hadith proof.

Core Idea
Killing non-mukallaf in jihad is haram unless they actively fight.

Jihad, as a defensive or regulated military campaign, is governed by strict ethical rules. One of the most important is the protection of those who are not legally responsible (ghayr mukallaf) and are non-combatants. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi ruling based on a direct prophetic command.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categories of protected persons
The text lists four types: non-mukallaf (child, insane), old man (shaykh fani), and woman. Recognize that these are non-combatants.
Non-mukallaf means not legally accountable due to age or mental capacity.
2
State the ruling for each category
The ruling is a clear prohibition (haram) – it is forbidden to kill them. The text repeats 'bila qatl' (without killing).
The prohibition applies as long as they do not participate in fighting.
3
Examine the hadith proof
The author cites the hadith: 'Do not kill an old man who is perishing, nor a child, nor a woman.' This is a direct command from the Prophet.
In Hanafi usul, a negative command (nahy) indicates prohibition (haram).
4
Consider the exception
If any of these individuals actively engage in combat, the prohibition may be lifted. The text does not detail the exception, but it is implied by jihaad's nature.
The mufta bihi position: killing is permitted if they fight, otherwise forbidden.
5
Apply to a concrete scenario
A woman or child from the enemy camp is found; we do not kill them. If they attack, we may defend ourselves.
This ruling is consistent across the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وقتل غير مكلف؛ أي بلا قتل غير مكلّف منهم كالصبي والمجنون، وبلا قتل شيخ، وبلا قتل امرأة ; لحديث: «لا تقتلوا شيخاً فانياً، ولا طفلاً ولا امرأة»
His saying: 'And killing of non-mukallaf'; i.e., without killing a non-mukallaf among them like a boy or an insane person, and without killing an old man, and without killing a woman; due to the hadith: 'Do not kill an old man who is perishing, nor a child, nor a woman.'
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage gives the ruling that in jihad, it is forbidden to kill non-mukallaf (those not legally responsible) from the enemy. This includes children, the insane, the elderly (shaykh fani), and women. The proof is the famous hadith from the Prophet (peace be upon him) who instructed commanders not to kill these categories. Note that this prohibition applies as long as these individuals do not actively participate in fighting; if they fight, the ruling may differ. The Hanafi school consistently upholds this prohibition as part of the laws of war.

Key Terms
غير مكلف (ghayr mukallaf): One not legally responsible due to lack of mental capacity or immaturity, such as a child or insane person.
شيخ فان (shaykh fani): An aged, decrepit old man unlikely to fight.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the hadith cited in this lesson, which of the following groups is explicitly forbidden to kill in jihad?
A) All adult male combatants
B) Old men, children, and women
C) Only children and women
D) Only the elderly

Key Takeaway
Killing non-mukallaf (children, insane), shaykh fani, and women in jihad is haram unless they directly participate in combat.

The Prohibition of Mutilation (al-Muthlah) in Jihad
States ruling and provides proofs from Quran and Hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on mutilation in jihad and identify the Quranic and hadith proofs that support it.

Core Idea
Mutilation is Haram because it changes Allah's creation, an act Satan commands.

This lesson examines the legal status of mutilation (al-muthlah) in the context of jihad. The Hanafi school considers it absolutely prohibited, drawing on the Quran and Sunnah. We will explore the primary proofs and address the question of whether the Prophet's own action in a specific incident constitutes an exception or was later abrogated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The author states: 'And in mutilation is changing the creation of Allah, so it is prohibited.' Mutilation (al-muthlah) is the act, and the ruling is haram.
The ruling is explicit and absolute, not conditioned on the situation.
2
Locate the primary dalil from the Quran
The proof is Quran 4:119, where Satan says he will command people to change Allah's creation. The author uses this to argue that mutilation is from Satan's misguidance.
The verse is cited as evidence that any change of Allah's creation is prohibited unless permitted by the Lawgiver.
3
Locate the secondary dalil from the Sunnah
The hadith from Sahih Muslim is cited to show that the Prophet's blinding of the 'Uraniyyun was retaliation, not mutilation. This indicates that mutilation is prohibited even in war.
The hadith explains the exception: the Prophet only did it as qisas for the crime committed, not as a general practice.
4
Analyse the abrogation (naskh) issue
Al-Zayla'i argues that the hadith of the 'Uraniyyun is abrogated by the verse of hirabah (Quran 5:33). After that verse, the Prophet forbade mutilation in all expeditions.
This shows that even if some actions appeared to allow mutilation, the later revelation clarified the prohibition.
5
Note the practical exception for qisas
The passage clarifies that if a person commits crimes on multiple limbs, retaliation (qisas) may be taken for each crime without it being considered prohibited mutilation. This is because qisas is a legislated allowance.
Mutilation outside of qisas is haram; within qisas, it is permitted as an exact equivalent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المثلَةِ تغير خلقِ اللهِ تعالى فتحرم. وقد جاء في «صحيح مسلم»: «إنما سمل النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أعينهم لأنهم سملوا أعين الرعاء، ولو أن شخصا بغى على قوم جنايات في أعضاء متعددة فاقتص منه للمجني عليه لما كان التشويه الذي حصل من المثلة المنهي عنها، وإذا اختلفت في نزول الآية الأقوال، وتطرق إليها الاحتمال فلا نسخ. انتهى كلامه. قلت: ومما يدل على نسخ حديث العُرْنيين بالآية ما رواه الواقدي في كتاب «المغازي»: حدثني إسحاق عن صالح مولى التوأمة عن أبي هريرة قال: «لما قطع النبي أيدي أصحاب اللقاح وأرجلهم وسمل أعينهم نزلت هذه الآية: إِنَّمَا جَزَاءُ الَّذِينَ يُحَارِبُونَ اللَّهَ وَرَسُولَهُ} الآية، فلم تسمل بعد ذلك عين، قال: وحدثني أبو جعفر: قال ما بعث النبي بعد ذلك بعثاً إلا نهاهم عن المثلة». انتهى كلام الزيلعي. قوله: وفي المثلة تغيير خلق الله فتحرم؛ لقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم حكاية عن قول الشيطان حين صار رجيماً وملعونا: {وَلَأُضِلَّنَّهُمْ وَلَأُمَنِّيَنَّهُمْ وَلَآمُرَنَّهُمْ فَلَيُبَتِّكُنَّ آذَانَ الْأَنْعَامِ وَلَآمُرَنَّهُمْ فَلَيُغَيِّرُنَّ خَلْقَ اللهِ} الآية، فدلت هذه الآية على أن تغيير خلق الله له من مضلات إبليس، فيكون منهيا عنه إلا فيما أذن لنا الشارع به.
And in mutilation is changing the creation of Allah, the Most High, so it is prohibited. And it has come in Sahih Muslim: 'Indeed, the Prophet (peace be upon him) blinded their eyes only because they blinded the eyes of the shepherds, and if a person committed crimes against a people in multiple limbs and retaliation was taken from him for the victim, then the disfigurement that occurred from the prohibited mutilation would not be. And if the opinions differed about the revelation of the verse and probability approached it, then there is no abrogation.' End of his words. I said: And among what indicates the abrogation of the hadith of the 'Uraniyyun by the verse is what al-Waqidi narrated in the book 'al-Maghazi': Ishaq narrated to me from Salih, the freed slave of al-Tu'ama, from Abu Hurayrah who said: 'When the Prophet cut off the hands and feet of the owners of the milk camels and blinded their eyes, this verse was revealed: Indeed, the penalty for those who wage war against Allah and His Messenger... the verse, so after that no eye was blinded.' He said: And Abu Ja'far narrated to me: He said, 'The Prophet did not send any expedition after that except that he forbade them from mutilation.' End of al-Zayla'i's words. His saying: And in mutilation is changing the creation of Allah, so it is prohibited; because of the saying of the Prophet (peace be upon him) narrating from the saying of Satan when he became stoned and cursed: 'And I will surely mislead them, and I will surely arouse in them [sinful] desires, and I will command them so they will surely slit the ears of livestock, and I will command them so they will surely change the creation of Allah.' The verse, so this verse indicates that changing the creation of Allah is among the misguidances of Iblis, so it is prohibited except in what the Lawgiver has permitted us.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on mutilation (al-muthlah). The author states that mutilation is haram because it distorts Allah's creation. He then provides evidence: first, a hadith from Sahih Muslim where the Prophet's action of blinding is explained as retaliation, not mutilation. He also discusses the possibility of abrogation of the hadith of the 'Uraniyyun by the verse of hirabah (Quran 5:33). The author, al-Zayla'i, argues that the hadith is abrogated. Then the commentator adds a second proof from the Quran (4:119) where Satan promises to change Allah's creation, proving that mutilation is from Satan's suggestions and thus prohibited unless Shariah permits it. So the ruling is: mutilation is prohibited in jihad, except where Shariah explicitly allows it (e.g., qisas for specific crimes).

Key Terms
المثلة (al-muthlah): Mutilation of the body, especially after death or in battle, such as cutting off limbs or blinding.
تغيير خلق الله (taghyir khalq Allah): Changing the natural creation of Allah, which includes physical alteration of humans or animals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in the text, what is the primary evidence that mutilation is haram?
A) The hadith of 'Uraniyyun
B) Quran 4:119
C) Quran 5:33
D) The Prophet's sunnah forbidding it

Key Takeaway
Mutilation is haram in Hanafi law because it entails changing Allah's creation, a command of Satan, with the only exception being lawful qisas.

Prohibited Killing in Jihad: Non-Combatants and the Exception of the Queen
Expansion on who may not be killed in jihad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which categories of people are prohibited from being killed in jihad, identify the exception for a queen who leads and fights, and apply the underlying principle of repelling evil.

Core Idea
Jihad is not about destruction; only those who pose a threat may be killed.

In this passage from ʿUmdat al-Riʿāyah, the commentator expands on the base text's prohibition of killing certain individuals in jihad. We will explore the precise categories—children, the insane, the elderly, women, the blind, the crippled, and monks—who are protected, and the sole exception of a queen who actively leads and fights. This ruling is rooted in prophetic hadith and a clear legal principle: the aim of jihad is to elevate Allah's word and repel evil, not to annihilate indiscriminately.

Guided Path
1
List the Protected Categories
Read the passage and identify all the categories mentioned as prohibited to kill. These include non-accountable persons (child, insane), the decrepit old man, women, the blind, the crippled, and monks in seclusion.
Note that 'non-accountable' (ghayr mukallaf) is the overarching category for child and insane.
2
Examine the Prophetic Proof
The commentator cites two hadith: 'Do not kill a decrepit old man, nor a child, nor a woman' (Abu Dawood) and the Prophet's disapproval upon seeing a dead woman. Understand how these hadith establish the prohibition.
These hadith are the primary textual evidence (dalil naqli).
3
Apply the Principle of Repelling Evil
The commentator states that the purpose of jihad is not to destroy the world but to elevate Allah's word and repel evil. Therefore, only those whose evil is feared may be killed. Those who cannot fight (elderly, blind, etc.) are not killed because they pose no threat.
This principle distinguishes between combatants and non-combatants.
4
Identify the Exception: The Queen
Although women are generally protected, a queen (malikah) who has authority, plans war, and fights is an exception. She is killed because she is effectively a combatant.
The key is her active leadership and participation in warfare.
5
Note the Intra-Hanafi Difference on the Elderly
Abu Bakr al-Razi held that an elderly person of sound mind may be killed, but the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is not to kill the senile old man. Understand why this difference exists.
The difference hinges on whether the elderly person can still pose a threat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: وقتل؛ أي بلا قتل غير مكلف منهم كالصبي والمجنون، وبلا قتل شيخ، وبلا قتل امرأة؛ لحديث: «لا تقتلوا شيخاً فانياً، ولا طفلاً ولا امرأة»، أخرجه أبو داود، وفي الصحيحين والسنن: «إن النبي رأى في مغازيه امرأة مقتولة من الكفار فأنكر ذلك، وقال: هاه ما كانت هذه تقاتل؟»، وفي الباب أحاديث أخر. [١] قوله: وشيخ فان؛ على صيغة اسم الفاعل من الفناء، صفة لشيخ، قال في «الفتح»: «المراد بالشيخ الفاني الذي لا يقتل: من لا يقدر على القتال ولا الصياح عند التقاء الصفين، ولا على الإحبال؛ لأنه يجيء منه الولد، فيكثر محارب المسلمين»، ذكره في «الذخيرة». زاد الشيخ أبو بكر الرازي: إنه إذا كان كامل العقل يقتل، ومثله نقتله إذا ارتد والذي لا نقتله الشيخ الفاني الذي خرف وزال عن حدود العقلاء والمميزين، فهذا لا نقتله، ولا إذا ارتد. [٢] قوله: وأعمى ومقعد؛ وكذا من في معناهما كيابس الشق ومقطوع اليمني ومن خلاف، وكذا لا يقتل الراهب في صومعته، ولا أهل الكنائس الذين لا يخالطون الناس، فإن خالطوا قتلوا كالقسيس، والذي يجن ويفيق يقتل في حال إفاقته وإن لم يقاتل، كذا في «الفتح» و«السير الكبير». والأصل في هذا الباب: إن الغرض من الجهاد ليس تخريب العالم وإهلاك العباد من غير ضرورة، بل إعلاء كلمة الله ودفع شرهم فيقتل منهم من يخاف شره لا من لا يخاف شره، قال في «الجوهرة»: يجوز قتل الأخرس والأصم، وقطع اليد اليسرى، أو إحدى الرجلين؛ لأنه يمكن أن يقاتل راكباً. [٣] قوله: إلا ملكة؛ بفتح الميم واللام؛ أي تقتل المرأة إذا كانت ملكة لها رأي وتدبير الحرب، وتقاتل، وأما إذا لم تكن ملكة فلا تقتل.
His saying: 'and killing'; that is, without killing the non-accountable among them, such as the child and the insane, and without killing an old man, and without killing a woman, according to the hadith: 'Do not kill a decrepit old man, nor a child, nor a woman' - narrated by Abu Dawood, and in al-Sahihayn and the Sunan: 'The Prophet saw in his battles a woman killed among the disbelievers and disapproved of that, saying: 'Why was she killed? She was not fighting.'' And there are other hadiths on the topic. [1] His saying: 'and a decrepit old man' - on the pattern of the active participle from al-fana', describing an old man. He said in al-Fath: 'What is meant by the decrepit old man who is not killed is one who is not able to fight nor shout at the meeting of the two armies, nor to procreate, because he produces offspring thus increasing the number of Muslim fighters.' Mentioned in al-Dhakhirah. Shaykh Abu Bakr al-Razi added: If he is of complete intellect, he is killed, and similarly we kill him if he apostatizes. The one we do not kill is the decrepit old man who has become senile and departed from the limits of the sane and discerning; this one we do not kill, nor if he apostatizes. [2] His saying: 'and a blind person and a crippled person' - and similarly those in their meaning, like the one with a dry side and the one whose right hand is cut off and the one with a clubbed foot. Similarly, a monk in his monastery is not killed, nor the people of churches who do not mix with people. If they mix, they are killed like a priest. And one who becomes insane intermittently is killed during his lucidity even if he does not fight. Thus in al-Fath and al-Siyar al-Kabir. The principle in this chapter is that the purpose of jihad is not to destroy the world and annihilate people without necessity, but to elevate the word of Allah and repel their evil; so killed from them is one whose evil is feared, not one whose evil is not feared. He said in al-Jawharah: It is permissible to kill the mute and the deaf, and to cut off the left hand or one of the feet, because he can fight while riding. [3] His saying: 'except a queen' - with fatha on mim and lam; that is, the woman is killed if she is a queen who has an opinion and management of war and fights. But if she is not a queen, she is not killed.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage details whom it is prohibited to kill in jihad. The base text states 'and killing of non-accountable' and the commentator expands: it is prohibited to kill children, the insane, the elderly, and women, citing the Prophet's hadith. He then explains that 'elderly' refers to those too old to fight, and 'blind' and 'crippled' are also excluded. However, a queen (malikah) who leads and fights is an exception—she may be killed. The underlying principle is that jihad is not about destruction but to raise Allah's word and repel evil; thus only those who pose a threat are killed. The commentator also mentions other categories like the deaf and those with one hand or leg can be killed if they can fight. There is also an intra-Hanafi difference: Abu Bakr al-Razi says an elderly person of sound mind may be killed, but the senile old man is not killed even if he apostatizes.

Key Terms
غير مكلف (ghayr mukallaf): Non-accountable persons not legally responsible for their actions, such as children and the insane.
شيخ فان (shaykh fanin): A decrepit old man who is physically incapable of fighting or contributing to warfare.
ملكة (malikah): A queen who has political authority, plans and leads war, and fights herself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Which of the following is an exception to the general prohibition against killing women in jihad?
A) A woman who is old
B) A woman who is the queen and leads the army
C) A woman who is a mother
D) A woman who is blind

Key Takeaway
Jihad prohibits killing non-combatants; only those who pose an active threat, such as a fighting queen, may be killed.

Who May Be Killed in Jihad: The Hanafi Position
Explaining who may and may not be killed in jihad based on harm potential.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which non-combatants are protected from being killed in jihad according to the Hanafi school, and apply the principle of potential harm to determine permissibility.

Core Idea
Jihad targets only those who pose a threat; non-combatants like the elderly, blind, and women are safe unless they actively cause harm.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the Hanafi rules on killing non-combatants during jihad. It begins with a prophetic hadith wherein the Prophet disapproved of a killed woman, establishing that women not fighting are not to be killed. The commentary then expands to other categories: the decrepit old man, the blind, the cripple, and monks who isolate themselves. The key principle is that jihad is not about destruction but repelling evil, so only those whose evil is feared are targeted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hadith Proof
Locate the hadith cited by the author: the Prophet saw a killed woman and disapproved, saying 'She was not fighting.' This hadith forms the basis for the general prohibition of killing non-combatants who do not fight.
2
List the Protected Categories
From the text, list the individuals who are not to be killed: decrepit old man (who is senile), blind man, cripple, woman (except queen), monk in his monastery, and those who do not mix with people.
Note the nuance: the decrepit old man with complete intellect is killed.
3
Understand the Exception: Queen and Others
The text states that a queen is killed because she has power over the disbelievers. Also, the wealthy and those with military opinion are killed because their assistance causes severe harm.
This shows the principle: anyone who can cause harm, even if not a combatant, may be killed.
4
Apply the Principle of Harm Potential
The text says 'So those whose evil is feared are killed, not those whose evil is not feared.' Apply this principle to hypothetical cases: a blind person who can fight on horseback is killed; a deaf-mute who can fight is killed.
5
Analyze the Ruling on the Disbelieving Father
The passage ends with a unique case: a son should not initiate killing his disbelieving father, but someone else may. The father may initiate killing his son. This shows filial piety considerations even in jihad.
This is a separate point about familial relations in warfare.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشيخ فان، وأعمى، ومقعد، وامرأة إلا ملكة أخرجه أبو داود، وفي الصحيحين والسنن: «إنّ النبي رأى في مغازيه امرأة مقتولة من الكفّار فأنكر ذلك، وقال: ما كانت هذه تقاتل»، وفي الباب أحاديث أخر.
[١] قوله: وشيخ فان؛ على صيغة اسم الفاعل من الفناء، صفة لشيخ، قال في «الفتح»: «المراد بالشيخ الفاني الذي لا يقتل: من لا يقدر على القتال ولا الصياح عند التقاء الصفين، ولا على الإحبال؛ لأنه يجيء منه الولد، فيكثر محارب المسلمين»، ذكره في «الذخيرة». زاد الشيخ أبو بكر الرازي: إنّه إذا كان كامل العقل يقتل، ومثله نقتله إذا ارتد، والذي لا نقتله الشيخ الفاني الذي خرف وزال عن حدود العقلاء والمميزين، فهذا لا نقتله، ولا إذا ارتد.
[٢] قوله: وأعمى ومقعد؛ وكذا من في معناهما كيابس الشق ومقطوع اليمنى ومن خلاف، وكذا لا يقتل الراهب في صومعته، ولا أهل الكنائس الذين لا يخالطون الناس، فإن خالطوا قتلوا كالقسيس، والذي يجنّ ويفيق يقتل في حال إفاقته وإن لم يقاتل، كذا في «الفتح»، و«السير الكبير». والأصل في هذا الباب: إن الغرض من الجهاد ليس تخريب العالم وإهلاك العباد من غير ضرورة، بل إعلاء كلمة الله ودفع شرهم، فيقتل منهم من يخاف شره لا من لا يخاف شره، قال في «الجوهرة»: يجوز قتل الأخرس والأصم، وقطع اليد اليسرى أو إحدى الرجلين؛ لأنه يمكن أن يقاتل راكباً.
[٣] قوله: إلا ملكة؛ - بفتح الميم واللام -؛ أي تقتل المرأة إذا كانت ملكة لها سطوة وسلطنة على الكفار، ويقتل المقاتل من غير المكلفين والشيوخ، وكذا ذوو المال وذوو الرأي في أمور الحرب، فإن الضرر بإعانتهم أشد، فيقطع أصله بقتلهم.
[١] قوله: منهم؛ أي الشيخ الفاني والمقعد والأعمى والمرأة وغير المكلف، إلا أن الصبي والمجنون يقتلان ما داما يقاتلان لا بعد الأسر، وغيرهما لا بأس بقتله بعد الأسر، كذا في «الهداية».
[٢] قوله: وأب كافر؛ ذكر الأب اتفاقي، ولو قال: أصل كافر لكان أولى؛ لأن الحكم في الأم وأجداده وجداته من قبل الأم والأب كالأب، واحترز به عن الفرع المشرك وإن سفل، وللأب أن يبتدئ بقتله، وكذا سائر القرابات، كذا في «البحر» و «النهر».
[٣] قوله: فيقتله غير ابنه؛ برفع آخر المضارع، وهو استئناف، يعني لا يقتل الابن أباه الكافر ابتداءً، بل يقتله غيره، وظن الشارح له على ما سيذكره أنه النصب، وأنه منصوب بتقدير «أن» الناصبة ظن فاسد مبني على تكلف لا يحتاج إليه، فإنه يرد على ما ذكره من السببية: إن عدم القتل ليس سبباً لقتل غيره أباه.
And a decrepit old man, a blind man, a cripple, and a woman except a queen. Abu Dawood narrated it, and in the Sahihayn and Sunan: 'The Prophet saw in his battles a woman killed from the disbelievers and disapproved of that, and said: She was not fighting.' And there are other hadiths on the topic. [1] His saying: 'And a decrepit old man' - on the pattern of the active participle from al-fana', an adjective for shaykh. He said in al-Fath: 'What is meant by the decrepit old man who is not killed: one who cannot fight nor shout at the meeting of the two lines, nor can he impregnate; because he would produce offspring and increase the number of Muslim combatants.' Mentioned in al-Dhakhira. Sheikh Abu Bakr al-Razi added: 'If he is of complete intellect, he is killed, and likewise we kill him if he apostatizes. And the one we do not kill is the decrepit old man who has become senile and left the bounds of the rational and discerning; this one we do not kill, nor if he apostatizes.' [2] His saying: 'And a blind man and a cripple' - and likewise those in their meaning, like one with a withered side and one with the right hand cut off and the like. Likewise, the monk is not killed in his monastery, nor the people of churches who do not mix with people. If they mix, they are killed like the priest. And the one who is insane but becomes lucid is killed in his state of lucidity even if he does not fight. Thus in al-Fath and al-Siyar al-Kabir. And the principle in this chapter: The purpose of jihad is not to destroy the world and annihilate servants without necessity, but rather to elevate the word of Allah and repel their evil. So those whose evil is feared are killed, not those whose evil is not feared. He said in al-Jawhara: 'It is permissible to kill the mute and the deaf, and to cut off the left hand or one of the legs; because he can fight while riding.' [3] His saying: 'except a queen' - with fatha on the mim and lam - meaning the woman is killed if she is a queen who has power and dominion over the disbelievers. And the fighter is killed from among the non-obligated and the elderly, and likewise the wealthy and those with opinion in war matters, for the harm of their assistance is more severe, so their root is cut by killing them. [1] His saying: 'from them' - i.e., the decrepit old man, the cripple, the blind, the woman, and the non-obligated, except that the boy and the insane person are killed as long as they fight, not after capture; others are not killed after capture. Thus in al-Hidaya. [2] His saying: 'And a disbelieving father' - mentioning the father is incidental; if he had said 'a disbelieving origin' it would have been better, for the ruling applies to the mother and his grandparents from the mother and father like the father. And he restrains from the descendant polytheist even if low. And the father may initiate killing him, and likewise all relatives. Thus in al-Bahr and al-Nahr. [3] His saying: 'So someone other than his son kills him' - with dammah on the end of the present verb, and it is a new sentence. Meaning the son does not initiate killing his disbelieving father, rather someone else kills him. And the commentator's assumption that it is mansub by an implicit 'an' is a false assumption based on unnecessary affectation, for it would be objectionable to what he mentioned of causality: that not killing is not a cause for someone else to kill his father.
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya is a commentary on the text of al-Wiqaya, explaining the rules of who may be killed during jihad. The passage begins by citing a hadith in which the Prophet ﷺ saw a killed woman and disapproved, establishing that women who do not fight are not to be killed. The commentary then details the various categories of people who are not to be killed because they cannot effectively fight: a decrepit old man, a blind man, a cripple, and a woman (except if she is a queen, a fighter, a wealthy inciter, or a war strategist). The principle is that jihad is not about destruction but about elevating Allah's word and repelling evil; thus only those who pose a threat are targeted. There are nuances: a decrepit old man with intact intellect may be killed because he can still cause harm (e.g., ordering others). The blind and cripple are not killed unless they can fight (e.g., a blind person with a strategy, a cripple who can ride). Monks who isolate themselves are not killed unless they mix with people. A person who becomes lucid from insanity is killed in that state. The passage also discusses the ruling of a disbelieving father: the son should not initiate killing him, but if the father attacks, the son may defend himself. The grammatical point notes that 'fa-yaqtuluhu' is in the indicative as a new sentence, not subjunctive. The Hanafi position is that the aim of jihad is not indiscriminate killing but targeted elimination of threats.

Key Terms
شيخ فان (shaykh fanin): A decrepit old man who cannot fight, shout, or impregnate due to old age.
ملكة (malikah): A queen who has authority over the disbelievers and can command them in war.
أب كافر (ab kafir): A disbelieving father. The son is prohibited from initiating his killing but may defend himself if attacked.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) If she is a slave
B) If she is a queen with authority
C) If she is elderly
D) If she is blind

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prohibits killing non-combatants who cannot cause harm, but allows killing those who pose a threat, even if they are not fighters.

Jihad — Armed Conflict Rules

Killing Combatant Disbelievers and the Prohibition on a Son Initiating Killing His Kafir Father
Commentary on jihad rules with grammatical analysis of the father exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whom to kill in jihad, identify the exception for a Muslim son and his kafir father, and apply the condition for defensive killing.

Core Idea
A Muslim son may not initiate killing his kafir father, but may defend himself if attacked.

This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah clarifies the rules of engagement in jihad. While combatants and even non-combatant strategists may be killed, a special exception protects the father-son relationship: a son cannot initiate killing his disbelieving father. The ruling is grounded in a grammatical analysis of the matn and supported by prophetic practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify categories of non-Muslims who may be killed
From the commentary, list those who are permissible to kill: combatants, elderly, disabled if they fight, those inciting with wealth or strategic opinion. Note exceptions for children and insane who only fight while in combat, not after capture.
The category includes those who are harmful even if non-combatant.
2
Note the special exception for the kafir father
The matn states 'wa abun kafirun bid'an fayaqtulahu ghayru ibnihi' – a kafir father initially, so someone other than his son kills him. The son cannot initiate killing his father.
The exception applies to all ancestors (usul), not just father.
3
Understand the condition for defensive killing
If the father attacks the son and the son cannot defend himself except by killing, it is permissible. The exception is for initiation, not self-defense.
The text: 'idha qasada al-abu qatlahu, bihaythu la yumkinuhu daf'uhu illa biqatlihi, fa-innahu la ba'sa biqatlihi.'
4
Review the grammatical evidence for the ruling
The commentator argues for reading 'fayaqtulahu' with raf' (indicative) as a resumptive clause, not nasb (subjunctive), because the absence of killing is not a direct cause for another to kill. This grammatical point reinforces the legal ruling that the son's inaction does not obligate someone else to kill.
The six contexts for nasb after fa' are listed: command, prohibition, interrogation, negation, wish, offer.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Consider a Muslim soldier in battle who sees his kafir father among the enemy combatants. According to the Hanafi ruling, he must not initiate the attack. If the father attacks him, he may defend himself even if it results in the father's death.
This distinguishes the Hanafi position from potential opposing views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الجهاد / حكمه وكيï­ته
أو مقاتلاً منهم، أو ذا مال يحث به، أو ذا رأي في الحرب. وأب كافر بدءاً فيقتله غيرُ ابنه : أي لا يقتل الابن أباه الكافر ابتداءً، وهو احتراز عما إذا قصد الأب قتله، بحيث لا يمكنه دفعه إلا بقتله، فإنَّه لا بأس بقتله. سطوة وسلطنة على الكفّار، ويقتل المقاتل من غير المكلفين والشيوخ، وكذا ذوو المال، وذوو الرأي في أمور الحرب، فإنّ الضرر بإعانتهم أشدّ، فيقطع أصله بقتلهم. [١] قوله : منهم ؛ أي الشيخ الفاني والمقعد والأعمى والمرأةُ وغير المكلف، إلا أنّ الصبي والمجنون يقتلان ما داما يقاتلان لا بعد الأسر، وغيرهما لا بأس بقتله بعد الأسر، كذا في «الهداية». [٢] قوله : وأب كافر ؛ ذكر الأب اتفاقي، ولو قال : أصل كافر لكان أولى ؛ لأنّ الحكم في الأم وأجداده وجداته من قبل الأم والأب كالأب، واحترز به عن الفرع المشرك وإن سفل، وللأب أن يبتدئ بقتله، وكذا سائر القرابات، كذا في «البحر» و «النهر». [٣] قوله : فيقتله غير ابنه ؛ برفع آخر المضارع، وهو استئناف، يعني لا يقتل الابن أباه الكافر ابتداءً، بل يقتله غيره، وظنّ الشارح له على ما سيذكره أنه النصب، وأنه منصوب بتقدير «أن» الناصبة ظنّ فاسد مبني على تكلّف لا يحتاج إليه، فإنّه يَرِدُ على ما ذكره من السببية : إن عدم القتل ليس سبباً لقتل غيره أباه.
وقوله : فيقتله ؛ بالنصب : أي لان يقتله غيرُه، فالفعل المضارع ينتصب بأن مقدرة بعد الفاء إذا كان ما قبلها سبباً لما بعدها أي بعد عدة أشياء منها النَّفي () أن يصير عدم قتل الابن أباه سبباً لقتل غير الابن أباه بأن يشغله ويلبثه فينبغي ليجيء آخر فيقتله.
ولو سُلّم كونه سبباً من وجه باعتبار اللازم فالسببية المعتبرة في بحث «إن» معتبرة باعتبار المنفي، كما ذكروا في قولهم ألا تأتينا فتحدّثنا، لا باعتبار النفي، كما ذكره الشارح، وهذا ظاهر على كل من له أدنى ممارسة بالنحو، فكيف خفي على الشارح. [١] قوله : بعد عدة أشياء ؛ يعني لا بُدَّ أن يكون قبل الفاء أحد الأشياء الستة : أحدها : الأمر، نحو: زرني فأكرمك : أي ليكن منك زيارة فإكرام مني. وثانيها : النهي، نحو: لا تشتمني فأضربك ، ويندرج فيهما الدعاء نحو: اللهم اغفر لي فأفوز، ولا تؤاخذني فأهلك. وثالثها : الاستفهام، نحو: هل عندكم ماء فأشربه؟ ورابعها : النفي، نحو ما تأتينا فتحدّثنا ، ويندرج فيه التحضيض نحو قوله : لَوْلَا أُنزِلَ إِلَيْهِ مَلَكُ فَيَكون مَعَهُ نَذِيرًا (الفرقان:۸). وخامسها : التمني، نحو: ليت لي مالا فأنفقه، ويدخل فيه الترجي نحو قوله لَعَلى أَبْلُغُ الأَسْبَبَ أَسْبَبَ السَّمَوَاتِ فَاطَّلِعَ إِلَى إِلَهِ مُوسَى (غافر:٣٦-٣٧). وسادسها : العرض، نحو ألا تأتينا فتحدّثنا، وألا تنزل بنا فتصيب خيرا. [٢] قوله : بأن يشغله ؛ أي يجعل أباه مشغولاً بالمحاربة، بأن يوقب فرسه أو يطرحه عنها أو يلجئه إلى مكان ولا ينصرف عنه، ولا يتركه بل يوقعه في اللبث ؛ أي التأخير ليجيء آخر فيقتله.
The Book of Jihad / Its Ruling and Manner. Or a combatant among them, or one who incites with wealth, or one with strategic opinion in war. And a kafir father initially, so someone other than his son kills him: meaning the son does not initiate killing his kafir father. This is a precaution for when the father intends to kill him such that he cannot repel him except by killing him, then there is no harm in killing him. Power and sovereignty over the disbelievers: the combatant among non-accountable persons and the elderly are killed, and likewise those of wealth and those of strategic opinion in matters of war, for the harm through their assistance is more severe, so their origin is cut off by killing them. [1] His saying: 'among them' means the decrepit old man, the disabled, the blind, the woman, and non-accountable persons, except that the boy and the insane person are killed as long as they fight, not after capture; others, there is no harm in killing them after capture. This is in 'al-Hidayah'. [2] His saying: 'And a kafir father'—the mention of father is accidental; if he said 'a kafir ancestor' it would be better, because the ruling regarding the mother, grandfathers, and grandmothers from the mother's and father's side is like the father. And it excludes a polytheist descendant even if low, and the father may initiate killing him, and likewise other relatives, as in 'al-Bahr' and 'al-Nahr'. [3] His saying: 'so his other than his son kills him'—with the last letter of the verb in the indicative (raf'), and it is a resumptive clause, meaning the son does not initiate killing his kafir father, but rather someone else kills him. The commentator's thought, as he will mention, that it is in the subjunctive (nasb), and that it is subjunctive with the implied 'an' is a corrupt thought based on unnecessary affectation, for what he mentioned of causality is refuted: that the absence of killing is not a cause for another to kill his father. And his saying: 'fayaqtulahu' with nasb: i.e., 'so that another kills him', because the present tense verb is in the subjunctive with an implied 'an' after the fa' when what precedes is a cause for what follows, that is after several things including negation: that the son's not killing his father becomes a cause for someone other than the son to kill his father by his occupying him and delaying him, so that another may come and kill him. And even if it is conceded that it is a cause in some respect considering the necessary implication, the causality considered in the discussion of 'an' is considered with respect to the negated thing, as they mentioned in their saying 'ala ta'tina fatuhaddithuna', not with respect to the negation, as the commentator mentioned. This is clear to anyone with minimal practice in grammar, so how did it escape the commentator? [1] His saying: 'after several things'—means that before the fa' there must be one of six things: First: command, like 'zurni fa-ukrimuka' meaning let there be from you a visit, then from me honoring. Second: prohibition, like 'la tashtumni fa-adribuka', and invocation is included like 'Allahumma ighfir li fa-afuz', 'la tu'akhidhni fa-ahlak'. Third: interrogation, like 'hal 'indakum ma'un fa-ashrabahu?' Fourth: negation, like 'ma ta'tina fa-tuhaddithuna', and incitement is included like His saying: 'lawla unzila ilayhi malakun fayakuna ma'ahu nadhiran' (Al-Furqan:8). Fifth: wish, like 'layta li malan fa-unfiqahu', and hope is included like His saying: 'la'alli ablughu al-asbaba asbaba al-samawati fa-attali'a ila ilahi Musa' (Ghafir:36-37). Sixth: offer, like 'ala ta'tina fa-tuhaddithuna' and 'ala tanzil bina fa-tusiba khayran'. [2] His saying: 'by occupying him'—meaning he makes his father busied with fighting, by stopping his horse or throwing him from it or making him go to a place and not leaving him, rather he delays him so that another may come and kill him.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of al-Wiqayah regarding the rules of killing non-Muslims in jihad. The passage first states that combatants among non-Muslims may be killed, including those who incite with wealth or give strategic advice, even if they are non-accountable (like elderly, blind, disabled, women, children) or insane—but children and insane are only killed while actively fighting, not after capture. The commentary then addresses a specific case: a Muslim son may not initiate killing his kafir father. This is a precautionary ruling; if the father attacks the son and the son cannot defend himself except by killing, then it is permissible. The commentary notes that 'father' is an example; the same applies to any kafir ancestor (mother, grandparents). However, the son may kill a kafir descendant (child or grandchild) if needed. The grammatical discussion revolves around the phrase 'fayaqtulahu ghayru ibnihi' in the matn. The commentator argues that the correct reading is with raf' (indicative) as a resumptive clause, not nasb (subjunctive), because the absence of killing is not a direct cause for another to kill. He then explains the rules for nasb after fa' in Arabic grammar, listing six contexts where the verb is subjunctive (command, prohibition, interrogation, negation, wish, offer). The hadith of Dureid ibn al-Simmah (an old blind strategist killed in battle) is referenced to show that those with harmful opinions may be killed even if not fighting.

Key Terms
أب كافر (ab kafir): A father who is a disbeliever (kafir); the text specifies that a Muslim son may not initiate killing him in jihad.
فيقتله غير ابنه (fayaqtulahu ghayru ibnihi): So his other than his son kills him—meaning someone else should kill the kafir father, not the son.
سببية (sababiyyah): Causality in grammar—a condition for the verb after fa' to be in the subjunctive.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, under what condition may a Muslim son kill his kafir father?
A) He may never kill his father.
B) He may kill him if the father is a combatant.
C) He may kill him only in self-defense when the father attacks.
D) He may kill him if the father has wealth.

Key Takeaway
A Muslim son may not initiate killing his kafir father in jihad, but may defend himself if attacked—a ruling that balances military duty with filial piety.

Ruling on Taking a Mushaf and Women into Enemy Land
Explains the Hanafi ruling on taking mushaf and women into enemy territory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on taking a mushaf and women into enemy land and explain the distinction between a large and small army.

Core Idea
The hadith's prohibition is conditional: it applies only when safety is not predominant.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses two sensitive matters during jihad: carrying the Quran and taking women into enemy territory. The Prophet's prohibition is interpreted by the Hanafi school not as absolute but as dependent on the level of security. The key principle is 'al-ghalib ka al-muhaqqaq' (the predominant is like the certain).

Guided Path
1
Identify the hadith and its apparent meaning
Read the hadith cited: 'Do not travel with the Quran to the land of the enemy.' At face value, it seems absolute. Note that it is reported by multiple narrators.
The hadith is in Sahih Muslim and others.
2
Analyze the Hanafi restriction
According to Al-Hidayah, the prohibition applies only to a small army (sariyah) where loss is feared. In a large army ('askar 'azim) where safety is predominant, it is permissible. The predominant is treated as certain.
This is based on the usul principle 'al-ghalib ka al-muhaqqaq'.
3
Compare with the Maliki position
The Malikis take the hadith as absolute, prohibiting all travel with the Quran into enemy land regardless of army size. The Hanafi school considers this a valid interpretation but relies on the restriction.
Imam al-Shafi'i agreed with the Hanafi position.
4
Apply the same reasoning to women
Elderly women may go out in a great army for necessary tasks (cooking, nursing). Young women should remain at home to avoid fitna. If women must be taken, slave women are preferred over free women.
The same principle of predominant safety applies.

Source Text

كتاب الجهاد / حكمه وكيفيته
وإخراج مصحف وامرأة
[١]قوله : وإخراج مصحف ؛ لحديث: «لا تسافروا بالقرآن، فإنّي لا آمن أن يناله العدو، أخرجه مسلم، ، وعند البخاري ومسلم وأبي داود وابن ماجة ومالك : «نهى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أن يسافر بالقرآن إلى أرض العدو» (۳).
قال في «الهداية»: «لا بأس بإخراج النساء والمصاحف مع المسلمين إذا كان عسكراً عظيماً يؤمن عليه ؛ لأنّ الغالب هو السلامة، والغالب كالمتحقق.
ويُكره إخراج ذلك في سرية لا يؤمن عليها ؛ لأنّ فيه تعريضهن على الضياع والفضيحة، وتعريض المصاحف على الاستخفاف ؛ فإنّهم يستخفون بها مغايظة للمسلمين، وهو التأويل الصحيح لقوله : لا تسافروا بالقرآن في أرض العدو». ولو دخل مسلم إليهم بأمان لا بأس بأن يحمل معه المصحف إذا كان قوماً يوفون بالعهد ؛ لأنّ الظاهر عدم التَعَرُّض». انتهى وقال الزَّيْلَعِي في «تخريجه» (٤) : اعلم أن المصنف له حمل الحديث على الجيش الصغير الذي لا يؤمن معه ضياعه، والشافعي الله معنا في ذلك ، وأخذ المالكية بإطلاقه. [۲] قوله : وامرأة ؛ قال في «الهداية»: «العجائز تخرجن في العسكر العظيم لإقامة عمل يليق بهن كالطبخ والسقي والمداواة ، فأما الشواب فقرارهن في البيوت أدفعُ للفتنة، ولا يباشرن القتال ؛ لأنه يستدل به على ضعف المسلمين إلا عند الضرورة، ولا يستحب إخراجهن للمباضعة ؛ أي الجماع والخدمة، فإن كانوا لا بد مخرجين فبالإماء دون الحرائر» (ه).
Book of Jihad: Its ruling and manner. And taking out a mushaf and a woman. [1] His statement: 'And taking out a mushaf' due to the hadith: 'Do not travel with the Quran, for I do not feel secure that the enemy will attain it' reported by Muslim, and in Bukhari, Muslim, Abu Dawud, Ibn Majah, and Malik: 'The Messenger of Allah ﷺ forbade traveling with the Quran to the land of the enemy.' He said in Al-Hidayah: 'There is no harm in taking out women and mushafs with the Muslims if it is a great army that is secure; because the predominant is safety, and the predominant is like the certain. And it is disliked to take them out in a small raiding party that is not secure; because it exposes them to loss and disgrace, and exposes the mushafs to disrespect; for they will disrespect them out of anger towards the Muslims. And this is the correct interpretation of his statement: Do not travel with the Quran in enemy land.' And if a Muslim enters into their territory with a pledge of security, there is no harm in him carrying the mushaf with him if they are a people who fulfill covenants; because the apparent is non-harm.' End quote. And Al-Zayla'i said in his Takhrij: 'Know that the author (Al-Hidayah) interpreted the hadith as referring to the small army whose loss is not secure, and Ash-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) agrees with us in that, and the Malikis took it absolutely.' [2] His statement: 'and a woman.' He said in Al-Hidayah: 'Elderly women go out in the great army to perform work befitting them like cooking, watering, and treating wounds. As for young women, their place is in the houses, that is more repelling of fitna, and they do not directly fight because it indicates the weakness of the Muslims, except in necessity. And it is not recommended to take them out for sexual intercourse and service. If they must be taken out, then with slave women rather than free women.'
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the ruling on taking a copy of the Quran (mushaf) and women into enemy territory during jihad. The author begins by stating the ruling for the mushaf, citing the hadith prohibiting traveling with the Quran into enemy land. He then presents the Hanafi position from Al-Hidayah, which distinguishes between a large secure army ('askar 'azim) and a small vulnerable raiding party (sariyah). In a large army, where safety is predominant, there is no harm (la ba's) in taking women and mushafs. In a small party, it is makruh (disliked) due to risk of loss and disrespect. The author notes that this interpretation is the correct one for the hadith. He adds a further rule: if a Muslim enters with aman (safe conduct), he may carry the mushaf if the people are trustworthy. Al-Zayla'i then clarifies that the author (of Al-Hidayah) restricted the hadith to the small army, and that Imam Al-Shafi'i agreed with this interpretation, while the Malikis took the hadith as absolute prohibition. The second part discusses women. Elderly women may go out with a great army to perform necessary support tasks like cooking, watering, and medical care. Young women should remain at home to avoid fitna; they are not to fight except in dire necessity. It is not recommended (la yustahabb) to take them out for sexual intercourse or service; if unavoidable, slave women are preferred over free women.

Key Terms
مصحف (mushaf): A physical copy of the Quran, specifically a codex containing the complete text.
سرية (sariyah): A small military detachment sent on a raid or mission, typically without the full army's security.
عسكر عظيم ('askar 'azim): A large, powerful army that has overwhelming force and can ensure safety.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when is it permissible to take a mushaf into enemy territory during jihad?
A) Only when the army is small and vulnerable.
B) Only when the army is large and secure, and safety is predominant.
C) At all times, as the hadith is abrogated.
D) Never, as per the absolute prohibition.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school interprets the hadith forbidding travel with the Quran as conditional on the size of the army, using the principle that the predominant is like the certain.

Ruling on Taking Mushaf and Women into Enemy Territory
Rulings with proofs and conditions for taking Quran and women during jihad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on taking a mushaf and women into enemy territory, and explain the distinction between a large army and a small detachment.

Core Idea
The prohibition on taking a mushaf is conditional; if safety is assured, it is allowed.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya addresses the classical fiqh question of taking the Quran (mushaf) and women into enemy territory during jihad. The Hanafi school, guided by the principle of istihsan, distinguishes between large armies where safety is predominant and small detachments where risk is high. Understanding this contextual ruling is essential for applying the Shariah's balance between textual commands and practical realities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? The taking of a mushaf and women into enemy territory. State the conditional permission in a large army and the dislike in a small detachment.
2
Locate the dalil
The hadith prohibiting travel with the Quran. Note its transmission and textual form (imperative prohibition).
Hadith: 'Do not travel with the Quran...'
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the Hanafi interpretation derive the conditional ruling? The illa is fear of desecration; when safety is predominant, the prohibition ceases, making it permissible.
Wajh: al-ghalib ka al-muhaqqaq (the dominant is like certainty).
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Malikis hold absolute prohibition; Shafi'is agree with Hanafis in restricting the hadith.
Cross-madhhab comparison.
5
Apply the same analysis to women
Distinguish elderly women (permitted for necessary work) from young women (should stay home). Also note the ruling on taking women for sexual purposes (disliked).
Also note the definition of 'askar 'azim (min. 400) and sariyya (min. 100).

[1] His statement: 'And taking out a Mushaf' due to the hadith: 'Do not travel with the Quran, for I fear that the enemy may get hold of it,' narrated by Muslim, and according to Bukhari, Muslim, Abu Dawud, Ibn Majah, and Malik: 'The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) forbade traveling with the Quran to enemy territory.' It is not recommended to take them out for sexual intercourse (mubada'a) and service. If they must be taken out, then with slave women, not free women.' Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya, a commentary on Sharh al-Wiqayah, addresses the rulings on taking the Quran (mushaf) and women into enemy territory during jihad. The author begins by citing the Hadith prohibition against traveling with the Quran to enemy lands, reported in multiple collections. However, the Hanafi school interprets this prohibition restrictively based on the underlying rationale (illah) of fear of disrespect. The key text from al-Hidayah explains that if the Muslim army is large ('askar 'azim) such that safety is predominant, it is not prohibited (la ba's) to take out women and mushafs. Conversely, it is makruh (disliked) to take them out in a small detachment (sariyya) where safety is not assured, due to risk of loss and disgrace. The Hanafis see this as the sound interpretation of the Hadith. Al-Zayla'i notes that the author of al-Hidayah restricted the Hadith to a small army, and Imam al-Shafi'i agreed with this view, while the Malikis held to its general prohibition. Regarding women, the text distinguishes between elderly women ('aja'iz) who may go out to perform supportive tasks like cooking, watering, and medical care, and young women (shawab) who should remain at home to avoid fitna. Women do not directly fight except in necessity, and it is not recommended to bring them for sexual reasons. If they must be taken out, slave women (ima') are preferred over free women. Finally, the passage defines the minimum size of a 'askar 'azim (great army): according to Imam Abu Hanifa, four hundred; for a sariyya, one hundred. Some later sources suggest the great army should be twelve thousand based on a hadith about not being defeated due to small numbers. The commentary thus shows the Hanafi method of balancing textual evidence (Hadith) with practical considerations (dominant assumption of safety) to derive context-sensitive rulings.

Key Terms
مصحف (mushaf): A bound copy of the Quran; the text rules on taking it into enemy territory during jihad.
عسكر عظيم ('askar 'azim): A large army where safety is predominant; minimum sizes are given (400 according to Imam Abu Hanifa, or 12,000 according to some).
سرية (sariyya): A small military detachment; its minimum is 100 according to Imam Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as explained in this passage, when is it permissible to take a mushaf into enemy territory?
A) In any situation, as long as the Muslim is careful.
B) Only when accompanied by a large army whose safety is assured.
C) Never, because of the absolute prohibition in Hadith.
D) Only if the mushaf is carried by a woman.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on taking mushaf and women into enemy territory is conditional: permissible in a large army with assured safety, disliked in a small detachment, reflecting the Shariah's balance of textual prohibition and practical necessity.

Minimum Size of an Army and Detachment (Jaysh and Sariyya)
Commentary defining minimum numbers for jaysh and sariyya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definitions of jaysh and sariyya with their minimum numbers, and explain the proof from hadith used to recommend the size of a great army.

Core Idea
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, a jaysh is 400 fighters, a sariyya is 100, but a great army should be 12,000 due to hadith.

This passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah clarifies key military terminology in the chapter of jihad. Understanding these definitions is crucial for applying rulings related to expedition organization and divine assurance. We will examine the minimum numbers set by Imam Abu Hanifa and the hadith evidence for a larger force.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal definitions
Locate the terms jaysh and sariyya in the passage and note the minimum numbers stated by Imam Abu Hanifa.
The numbers are from al-Khaniyya and al-Inaya.
2
Understand the matn context
The passage comments on the phrase 'Except in an army that is feared to be defeated'. This exception allows certain rulings to change when the army might be overpowered.
3
Analyze the hadith evidence
The hadith 'Twelve thousand will not be defeated due to fewness' is cited to recommend the size of a great army. Determine its evidentiary value: it is a promise, not a command, so it is a recommendation.
The hadith is zanni in both transmission and meaning.
4
Distinguish between legal minimum and recommended ideal
The legal minimum for a jaysh is 400, but the great army (al-askar al-azim) is recommended to be 12,000. Understand that these are different concepts.
Confusing the two is a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage is a commentator's expansion on the matn phrase 'إلا في جيش يؤمن عليهم' from the chapter on jihad. The matn states an exception for a situation where there is an army (jaysh) that is feared to be defeated. The commentator clarifies what constitutes a jaysh and a sariyya (detachment) in legal terms. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the minimum number for a jaysh is 400 fighters, and for a sariyya (a smaller military unit) it is 100. These numbers are cited from the Hanafi works al-Khaniyya and al-Inaya. Additionally, a quote from al-Fath suggests that the great army (al-askar al-azim) should be 12,000, based on the hadith 'Twelve thousand will not be defeated from lack of numbers.' This hadith indicates that a force of 12,000 is sufficient against any enemy due to divine assurance, but the minimum for a jaysh remains 400 according to Imam Abu Hanifa. The passage thus provides normative definitions for military terminology used in jihad rulings.

Key Terms
جيش (jaysh): A military expedition consisting of 400 fighters or more, according to Imam Abu Hanifa.
سرية (sariyya): A smaller detachment of 100 fighters, according to Imam Abu Hanifa.

Hanafi Rulings on Peace Treaties: Permissibility, Payments, and Repudiation
Commentary on truce, payment, and repudiation rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for making peace with non-Muslims, explain the classification of payments, and apply the ruling on repudiation when betrayal is feared.

Core Idea
Peace treaties (sulh) are permissible if beneficial; payments are classified by timing; repudiation must be on equal terms.

This lesson covers the opening commentary on the chapter of truce (al-muwada'a). The matn states three key points: peace is made if it is good, money may be taken from them if needed, and treaties may be repudiated. The commentator, quoting al-Nahr, explains each point with Quranic verses and the Prophet's example at Hudaybiyya. A critical distinction is made regarding payments: money taken before descending on enemy territory is treated like kharaj and jizya, while after descent it becomes booty (ghanima). Understanding these rulings is essential for governing relations with non-Muslim states and ensuring contracts are valid and just.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three rulings in the matn
Read the matn carefully: 'وَصُولحُوا إن خَيْرًا، وَيُؤْخَذُ مِنْهُمْ مَالٌ إن لَنَا بِهِ حَاجَةٌ، وَنَبْذٌ.' Recognize that the chapter addresses three separate but related rulings: the permissibility of peace, the permissibility of taking money, and the permissibility of repudiating the treaty.
Each ruling has its own conditions and evidence.
2
Understand the condition 'if it is good' (إن خَيْرًا)
The commentator explains that peace is only permissible if it is beneficial (khayr) in the imam's judgment, not if it leads to greater evil. The Quranic proofs (8:61 and 3:139) show that peace is commanded when the enemy inclines to it, but Muslims must remain confident in their superior position. The Prophet's truce at Hudaybiyya exemplifies a peace from strength, not weakness.
This refutes the misconception that peace is only for the weak.
3
Classify the payment by timing
The commentator distinguishes two scenarios: if the money is taken before Muslims descend on the enemy's territory (i.e., during negotiations or while still en route), it is spent like kharaj and jizya—on public welfare. If taken after descending (i.e., during active warfare), it is booty (ghanima), subject to khums and distribution among fighters. This classification affects how the money is allocated.
The timing relative to 'nuzul' (descending) is the key factor.
4
Define repudiation (nabdh) and its ruling
Nabdh literally means 'throwing away' and here means breaking the covenant. The ruling is based on Quran 8:58: if betrayal is feared, the treaty may be repudiated 'on equal terms' (ʿalā sawāʾ), meaning openly and not treacherously. The Prophet repudiated the treaty with the polytheists of Mecca when they violated it. This ensures that Muslims do not break faith.
Repudiation must be announced, not done secretly.
5
Apply the rulings to the example of Hudaybiyya
The Prophet made a ten-year truce without payment (no money taken from Quraysh). This illustrates that peace can be concluded without payment. Later, when the Quraysh violated the treaty, the Prophet repudiated it openly, setting the precedent for nabdh. Consider how the classification of payment would apply if money had been involved at Hudaybiyya (it would be pre-descent, thus like jizya).
Hudaybiyya is the paradigmatic case for all three rulings.

Source Text

 وصولحوا إن خيراً، ويؤخذ منهم مال إن لنا به حاجة، ونبذ.

Chapter of truce and who may grant safe conduct; And they make peace if it is good, and money is taken from them if we have need of it, and repudiation. He said: 'And they make peace' meaning it is permissible to make peace with the disbelievers without money, and with money taken from them or money given to them by the imam if the imam fears destruction for himself and the Muslims, thus in al-Nahr. He said: 'if it is good' meaning if peace is good in the opinion of the imam, not if it leads to evils. The basis for this is His statement: 'And if they incline to peace, then incline to it and rely upon Allah' and His statement: 'And do not weaken and do not grieve, and you are superior.' And the Prophet made peace with the people of Mecca in the year of Hudaybiyya, year six, on the condition that war be laid aside between him and them for ten years, reported by Abu Dawud and Ahmad and others. He said: 'And money is taken from them'; he said in al-Nahr: it is spent in the ways of kharaj and jizya if it is before descending upon their territory with a messenger, but if we have descended upon them, it is booty, we take its khums and divide the remainder. He said: 'And repudiation' (nabdh) is in the passive form from nabdh (with fatha), which lexically means throwing, and what is intended is breaking the covenant. Allah said: 'And if you fear from a people betrayal, then throw back to them [their treaty] on equal terms. Indeed Allah does not like the treacherous.' And it is established that the Prophet repudiated the peace that was between him and the disbelievers...
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage is the opening commentary on the chapter of truce (al-muwada'a) and who may grant safe conduct (aman). The matn states three key points: (1) peace with the enemy is made if it is beneficial; (2) money may be taken from them if Muslims need it; (3) the treaty may be repudiated (nabdh). The commentator explains each. First, 'peace is made' means it is permissible to make peace without any payment, or with payment either taken from the enemy or given to them by the imam, but only if the imam fears annihilation of the Muslims. This is based on al-Nahr. Second, 'if it is good' means the peace must be genuinely beneficial in the imam's judgment, not leading to greater harm. The Quranic verses (8:61 and 3:139) command peace when the enemy inclines to it but also remind Muslims of their superior position; the Prophet's example at Hudaybiyya sets a precedent for a ten-year truce. Third, 'money taken from them' is classified: if taken before actual warfare (i.e., while still negotiating), it is treated like kharaj and jizya, spent on public welfare; but if taken after Muslims have come down upon them (i.e., during war), it is booty (ghanima) subject to khums. Fourth, 'repudiation' (nabdh) means cancellation of the treaty. The Quran 8:58 allows repudiation if betrayal is feared, on equal terms (so the enemy is informed). The Prophet repudiated the treaty with the polytheists of Mecca when they broke it. This passage thus provides the legal framework for initiating and ending peace treaties with non-Muslims.

Key Terms
موادعة (muwada'a): A truce or peace agreement with non-Muslims for a specified period.
أمان (aman): Safe conduct granted to one or more non-Muslims, ensuring protection of life and property.
صلح (sulh): Peace treaty; in this context, a truce with non-Muslims, which may involve payment.
نبذ (nabdh): Repudiation of a treaty; cancellation of the peace agreement on equitable terms.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is haram because treaties must be honored
B) It is permissible on equal terms, after openly informing them
C) It is wajib immediately without notice
D) It is makruh tahrimi

Key Takeaway
Peace treaties with non-Muslims are permissible if beneficial; payments are classified by timing (pre-descent vs. post-descent); repudiation must be open and on equal terms when betrayal is feared.

When Notice Is Required Before Dissolving a Treaty
Explains when notice is required before dissolving a treaty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when notice is required before dissolving a treaty with the enemy and apply it to distinguish between cases of enemy betrayal and Muslim-initiated dissolution.

Core Idea
If the enemy betrays first, no notice needed; if we initiate, we must inform them.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on dissolving a peace treaty (nabdh) with hostile non-Muslims. The key question is whether formal notification is required. The ruling distinguishes between two scenarios: when the enemy has already broken the covenant through betrayal, and when the Muslim authority decides to dissolve the treaty proactively. The evidence is drawn from the Quranic command in Surah al-Anfal 8:58.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the passage to distinguish between when the enemy initiates betrayal and when the Muslim side initiates dissolution without betrayal. Note the ruling for each.
The key phrase is 'if they betray first' vs. 'if we initiate dissolution'.
2
Locate the Quranic proof
Find the reference to Quran 8:58. Understand that the imperative 'fa-anidh ilayhim' ('announce to them') is used to justify the obligation of notice when initiating dissolution.
The proof is a dalil naqli from the Quran.
3
Understand the condition of notice and time
Note that when notice is required, it must be given in a way that allows the enemy to be informed widely. The passage mentions a period sufficient for the king to spread news to his realm.
This is to avoid any semblance of treachery.
4
Apply the nuance of partial betrayal
Consider the scenario where some fighters attack without the king's permission. The passage clarifies that only those fighters break the treaty if they have independent strength; otherwise, no breach occurs.
This shows the precision of Hanafi fiqh in attributing actions.
