And before dissolving [the treaty] if they [the enemy] betray [first]; meaning they are fought before dissolution if they start with betrayal. The wording: 'if it is better...' and 'the dissolution is more beneficial' – dissolution is the breaking of the treaty with informing them of that. And before dissolution if they betray first: that is, they are fought before dissolution if they start with betrayal. [Reference to conquest of Makkah]. The statement: 'if it is' i.e. if the dissolution is more beneficial; because when the benefit changes, dissolution becomes jihad and fulfilling the treaty becomes abandoning jihad in form and meaning, and it is necessary to inform them of that to avoid treachery, and it is necessary to consider a period in which the news of dissolution reaches all of them, and it suffices to wait until a time in which their king, after knowing of the dissolution, can convey the news to the borders of his kingdom. And this is contrary to the case when the breach of peace is based on their betrayal and treachery, not initiated by us; then there is no need to inform them because they have become breakers of the covenant, so we have no need to dissolve it such that we need to inform them. Thus in al-Hidayah and its glosses. [٢] His statement: 'with informing them of that' – its apparent meaning is that this condition is included in the concept of dissolution, and the author's statement 'And before dissolution...' etc. indicates that; but what is understood from many books is that dissolution is merely breaking the peace, and when it is transitive with 'ila' it includes the meaning of conveying news to them, as in His statement: 'so announce to them' (Quran 8:58), and some have explicitly stated that informing them is a condition for the permissibility of dissolution in the case where they have not betrayed. [٣] His statement: 'if they betray' – meaning if their king starts fighting us or some of his followers with his permission and consent; and if some of them fight without his permission, the covenant is broken only with respect to the fighters, not others, because their action does not bind others. This applies if the fighters have strength; if they have no strength, it is not a breach of covenant at all. Thus said al-Zayla'i in Sharh al-Kanz.
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_with_proof on the rules of dissolving a treaty (nabdh) with enemy combatants. It clearly distinguishes two scenarios: (1) when the enemy initiates betrayal (khiyanah), and (2) when the Muslim authority initiates dissolution without prior betrayal. 

The core ruling: If the enemy begins hostilities or breaks the covenant, we may fight them without prior notice – the treaty is effectively voided by their actions. But if we wish to dissolve the treaty preemptively (because it is no longer beneficial), we must give them formal notice (nabdh) and allow sufficient time for the news to reach all their parties. This is based on the Quranic command in Surah al-Anfal (8:58) which instructs the Prophet to announce the dissolution to avoid treachery.

The passage further clarifies the mechanism: the notice (informing them) is a condition for the permissibility of dissolution when there is no betrayal. The duration required is that which allows the enemy king to spread the news to his realm after learning of the dissolution. 

In contrast, if the enemy has already betrayed (e.g., their king attacks with authorization), we do not need to inform them because they have effectively nullified the treaty themselves. The commentator also notes a nuance: if only some of their people fight without the king's permission, the treaty is only broken with respect to those specific fighters, provided they have independent strength; otherwise, no breach occurs. 

The authority_weight is from Zahir al-Riwayah as derived from al-Hidayah, a core Hanafi text.

Key Terms
نبذ (nabdh): Dissolving a treaty or covenant with the enemy, which includes informing them of the termination.
خيانة (khiyanah): Betrayal or breach of trust by the enemy, which nullifies the treaty without need for formal notice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is it obligatory to inform the enemy before dissolving a peace treaty according to Hanafi fiqh?
A) Always, before any war action after a treaty
B) Only when the enemy has initiated betrayal
C) Only when the Muslim authority initiates dissolution without prior betrayal
D) Never, because treaties are binding indefinitely

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, dissolving a treaty requires formal notice only when the Muslim side initiates termination; if the enemy has already broken the covenant, no notice is needed.

Rulings on Peace with Apostates and Prohibition of Selling Arms
Rulings on truce with apostates and prohibition of arming them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for permissible truce with apostates and explain why selling arms to them is prohibited.

Core Idea
Making peace with apostates is allowed only if they dominate a town; selling them arms is always prohibited.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah addresses two critical questions in jihad rulings: when can the Muslim authority make a truce with apostates, and what items are prohibited from selling to them? The answers hinge on the status of the apostates' land and the principle of not cooperating in sin.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
The passage gives two separate rulings: (a) truce (sulh) with apostates, and (b) prohibition of selling weapons, horses, etc. to them. Identify each and note their legal categories.
The first is conditional, the second is absolute.
2
Determine the condition for the first ruling
The truce is only permissible if the apostates have overcome a town and their territory has become dar al-harb. If they have not, making peace would confirm them in apostasy, which is not allowed.
This condition is cited from Tanwir al-Absar and Manh al-Ghaffar.
3
Understand the financial rule
During a truce, we do not take any wealth (jizya) from apostates because jizya is only for dhimmis. However, if we take their wealth (e.g., as booty), it is not returned because their wealth is unprotected (ghayr ma'sum).
Contrast with rebels (baghy): their wealth is returned after war ends.
4
Analyze the prohibition on arms
Selling, giving, or lending weapons, horses, or any military equipment to apostates is prohibited. The basis is Quran 5:2: 'Do not cooperate in sin and transgression.'
The prohibition applies to any strengthening of enemies.
5
Apply the distinction with rebels
Unlike apostates, rebels' wealth is returned after conflict because their blood and wealth are protected. But during war, even rebel wealth is not returned to prevent aiding them in sin.
This shows nuanced fiqh of jihad.

Source Text

وصولح المرتد (۱) بلا مال ، ولا رد إن أخذنا (وصولح المرتد (۱) بلا مال ، ولا رد إن أخذنا) : يعني يجوز لنا أن نصالح المرتد، ولا نعجِّل في قتله ؛ لأنَّ إسلامه مرجو ، لكن لا نأخذ منه شيئاً ؛ لأنَّه يكون جزية، ولا يجوز أخذ الجزية من المرتدّ ، لكن لو أخذنا لا ترد إليه ؛ لأنه مال غير معصوم.

(1) أي المرتدون فلا بأس بموادعتهم ومعلوم أن ذلك إذا غلبوا على بلدة وصارت دارهم دار الحرب وإلا فلا ؛ لأن فيه تقرير المرتد على الردة ، وذلك لا يجوز. ينظر: «الفتح»(٥: ٤٥٩).

And make peace with the apostate (1) without wealth, and it is not returned if we take it. (And make peace with the apostate without wealth, and it is not returned if we take it): meaning it is permissible for us to make peace with the apostate and not hasten to kill him, because his Islam is hoped for, but we do not take anything from him, because that would be jizya, and it is not permissible to take jizya from an apostate; but if we did take it, it is not returned to him, because it is wealth that is not protected. (And weapons and horses are not sold.) [1] His statement: 'And make peace with the apostate' – it is mentioned in Tanwir al-Absar and its commentary Manh al-Ghaffar and others that making peace with apostates is only permissible if they have overcome a town and their abode has become a war abode, and if they have not overcome a town, making peace with them is not permissible, because then the peace would be establishing them upon apostasy. [2] His statement: 'without wealth' i.e. without taking wealth from them; if there is need to give wealth, it is permissible. (3) His statement: 'because his Islam is hoped for' – so it is permissible to delay fighting him hoping for his Islam. Abu al-Layth said in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir: this is when the apostates have overcome a city of Islam. [4] His statement: 'because it is wealth that is not protected' – for the wealth of apostates is booty for Muslims if they gain mastery over it, unlike when it is taken from the people of baghy (rebels), where it is returned to them after the war lays down its burdens, because it is not booty; except that it is not returned during war to prevent assisting them in sin. Thus in al-Binayah. [0] His statement: 'and it is not sold' – likewise it is not given as a gift nor loaned to them, and the basis for this is His statement: 'And cooperate in righteousness and piety, and do not cooperate in sin and transgression' (Quran 5:2). Selling these things to them and making them possess them strengthens them against fighting Muslims.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_statement on two connected issues regarding apostates (murtaddin) in the context of jihad: (1) the permissibility of making a truce (sulh) with them, and (2) the prohibition of selling weapons and horses to them. 

The first ruling: It is permissible to make peace with apostates and delay fighting them, provided that their Islam is still hoped for. However, there is a crucial condition stated by later authorities: such a truce is only valid if the apostates have gained control of a town and their territory has become dar al-harb. If they have not overcome a town, making peace with them is not allowed because it would effectively confirm them in apostasy. 

Regarding financial transactions: When making peace, we do not take any wealth from them, because taking wealth from apostates would be jizya, and jizya is only taken from dhimmis (People of the Book under Muslim protection), not from apostates. However, if we do happen to take their wealth (e.g., as booty in war), it is not returned to them, because their wealth is not protected (ghayr ma'sum). This is contrary to the case of rebels (ahl al-baghy) whose wealth is returned after the conflict, as it is not considered booty. But during war, even rebel wealth is not returned to prevent aiding them in sin. 

The second ruling: It is prohibited to sell, give, or lend weapons, horses, or any military equipment to apostates (and by extension to any enemy combatants), based on the Quranic command to cooperate in righteousness and not in sin (Quran 5:2). Selling such items would strengthen them in fighting Muslims, which is a form of cooperating in transgression. 

The authority_weight of the first ruling (truce with apostates) is from later fatawa (as cited from Tanwir al-Absar and Manh al-Ghaffar), while the prohibition of selling arms is grounded in Quran and is considered a firm ruling.

Key Terms
مرتد (murtadd): An apostate from Islam; in this context, a group of apostates who have overpowered a town and established dar al-harb.
صلح (sulh): A peace treaty or truce; here specifically with apostates, subject to conditions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition is it permissible to make peace with apostates according to the Hanafi school as stated in this passage?
A) Always, regardless of their strength
B) Only if they have not taken any wealth from Muslims
C) Only if they have overcome a town and their land becomes dar al-harb
D) Only if they pay jizya

Key Takeaway
Truce with apostates is permissible only when they dominate a town and hope of their Islam remains; selling them arms is always prohibited as it strengthens them against Muslims.

Peace with Apostates: Conditions and Ruling
Ruling on peace with apostates with conditions and wealth treatment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on making peace with apostates and identify the condition that makes it permissible.

Core Idea
Peace with apostates is permissible only if they have taken over a town; otherwise it confirms them in apostasy.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on making peace with apostates. The key distinction is whether the apostates have overpowered a town. If they have, a truce is allowed in hope of their return to Islam; if not, it is prohibited because it effectively confirms them in apostasy. We also explore the treatment of wealth taken from apostates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is making peace (musālahah) with an apostate or group of apostates. The ruling is permissible (jā'iz) under specific conditions.
The baseline assumption is that apostates are to be fought; peace is an exception.
2
Understand the condition of overpowering a town
According to the commentary, peace is only allowed if the apostates have overpowered a town and their abode has become a war abode (dār al-ḥarb). Without this, peace is not permitted because it would confirm them in apostasy.
This condition is cited from Tanwir al-Absar and Minh al-Ghaffar.
3
Note the prohibition of taking jizya from apostates
No wealth can be taken from them as part of the peace agreement, because that would be jizya, and jizya is not taken from apostates. However, if wealth is taken, it is not returned.
The reason wealth is not returned: it is unprotected (māl ghayr ma‘ṣūm) and becomes booty for Muslims.
4
Differentiate from rebels (baghī)
Unlike rebels, whose wealth is returned after the war, apostates' wealth is considered fay' (booty) once Muslims gain power over it.
This distinction is made to avoid assisting rebels in disobedience.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Given a town overtaken by apostates, evaluate the permissibility of a truce. If they control the town, a truce is permissible. If they do not, it is not permissible.
The hope of their return to Islam justifies the delay in fighting.

Book of Jihad / Chapter on Truce and Whose Protection is Permissible. The apostate is made peace with without wealth, and if we take (wealth) it is not returned. And weapons, horses, and iron are not sold to them. (Making peace with the apostate without wealth, and if we take it, it is not returned): meaning it is permissible for us to make peace with the apostate, and not hasten to kill him, because his Islam is hoped for, but we do not take anything from him, because it would be jizya, and taking jizya from an apostate is not permissible, but if we take it, it is not returned to him, because it is wealth without protection. (And weapons, horses, and iron are not sold to them). [1] His statement: 'and the apostate is made peace with'; it is mentioned in 'Tanwir al-Absar' and its commentary 'Minh al-Ghaffar' and others that making peace with apostates is only permissible if they have overpowered a town and their abode has become a war abode, and if they have not overpowered a town, making peace with them is not permitted, because then the peace would be a confirmation of their apostasy. [2] His statement: 'without wealth': meaning without taking wealth from them; if there is need to give wealth, giving it is permissible. [3] His statement: 'because his Islam is hoped for': so delaying his killing is permissible hoping for his Islam. Abu al-Layth said in 'Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir': this is when the apostates have overpowered a city of Islam. [4] His statement: 'because it is wealth without protection': indeed the wealth of apostates is booty for the Muslims when they overpower them, unlike what is taken from the people of baghy (rebels), where it is returned to them after the war lays down its burdens, because it is not booty, except that it is not returned during war, lest it be assistance in disobedience, as in 'al-Binaya'. [0] His statement: 'and it is not sold'; similarly it is not given as a gift and not lent to them; and the basis for it is His statement: 'And cooperate in righteousness and piety, but do not cooperate in sin and aggression' [Quran 5:2]; and selling these things to them and making them owners is strengthening them for fighting the Muslims. (1) meaning the apostates; there is no harm in making truce with them, and it is known that this is when they have overpowered a town and their abode has become a war abode, otherwise not, because in it is confirmation of the apostate upon apostasy, and that is not permissible. See: 'al-Fath' (5:459). And iron from them is also not sold. And there is a hadith to support it: 'The Messenger of Allah prohibited the sale of weapons during fitnah', reported by al-Bayhaqi, al-Bazzar, al-Tabarani, Ibn Adi and others, and its chain is weak. As for carrying food to them, it is permissible by istihsan. [1] His statement: 'and iron'; al-Ayni said in 'al-Binaya': al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said in 'Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir': selling iron to them is not disliked, because its essence is not an instrument for jihad and fitnah. This is one opinion. (1) In Sahih al-Bukhari (2:741) as a suspended narration, and Sunan al-Bayhaqi al-Kubra (5:327), and he said: raising it (to the Prophet) is a mistake, and the stopped version is more correct. And al-Jarh wa al-Ta'dil (8:102), and al-Kamil (2:51), and al-Uqayli's al-Du'afa (4:139), and Tarikh Baghdad (3:278), and Musnad al-Bazzar (9:63), and he said: This hadith we don't know anyone who narrates it from the Prophet except 'Imran ibn Husayn, and Abdullah al-Laqeeti is not well-known, and Bahr ibn Kunayz was not strong, but we have not preserved it from the Messenger of Allah except from this way, so we had no choice but to present it. And it was also narrated by Salam ibn Zurair from Abu Raja' from 'Imran as a stopped narration. And Mu'jam al-Tabarani (18:136), and al-Sunan al-Warida fi al-Fitan (2:409). And al-Haythami said in Majma' al-Zawa'id (4:87): In it is Bahr ibn Kunayz, who is abandoned. And Ibn Hajar said in al-Talkhis (3:18): (Weak, and the correct is that it is stopped). (2) Because Muslims need some of the medicines and goods from their lands; if we prevent them from what is in our land, they will also prevent what is in their land. And because if a trader enters their land to bring to Muslims what they benefit from from their land, he cannot avoid carrying to them some of what is found in our land; therefore we have permitted Muslims in that. See: al-Mabsut (4:1410), al-Muhit (1:135), al-Hidaya, Fath al-Qadir (4:461), and others. And from Abu Hurairah that he mentioned the story of Thumamah's acceptance of Islam, and at its end his statement to the people of Mecca when they said to him: 'Have you become a Sabian?' He said: 'By Allah, I have not become a Sabian, but I have accepted Islam and believed in Muhammad and believe in him. And by the One in whose hand is the soul of Thumamah, not a single grain from al-Yamamah (which was the agricultural land of Mecca) will reach you as long as I remain until Muhammad permits it.' And he returned to his country and prevented the carrying (of goods) to Mecca until Quraysh were strained. So they wrote to the Messenger of Allah asking him by their kinship to write to Thumamah to carry food to them, and the Messenger of Allah did so. Reported in Sunan al-Bayhaqi al-Kubra (9:66) and others.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling regarding making peace with apostates and the prohibition of selling weapons to them. The author explains that it is permissible to make peace with apostates, especially if they have taken over a town and made it a war zone. The reason is that their Islam is hoped for, so delaying fighting them is allowed. However, no wealth should be taken from them in the peace agreement, because it would be considered jizya, which is not permissible from apostates. If wealth is taken, it is not returned to them because their wealth is not protected—it is booty for Muslims. The commentary then addresses the prohibition of selling weapons, horses, and iron to apostates, citing the Quranic verse on non-cooperation in sin and a weak hadith. However, the sale of food to them is permissible by istihsan, due to mutual need and the story of Thumamah when he cut off supplies to Mecca but was later told by the Prophet to resume food delivery.

Key Terms
مصالحه (musālahah): Making peace or truce with apostates, not implying a permanent treaty but delaying hostilities.
مرتد (murtadd): A person who leaves Islam; in this context, a group that has overpowered a town.
مال غير معصوم (māl ghayr ma‘ṣūm): Wealth that is not protected; it can be taken as booty from apostates.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when is it permissible to make peace with apostates?
A) Always, because their Islam is hoped for.
B) Only when they have overpowered a town and their abode is war territory.
C) Only when they pay jizya.
D) Only when they are weak and few.

Key Takeaway
Peace with apostates is permissible only if they control a town; otherwise it is prohibited as confirmation of apostasy.

Jihad — Armed Conflict Rules

Prohibition of Selling Weapons to Apostates: Proofs and Exceptions
Rulings on selling weapons and food to apostates with proofs and istihsan exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi prohibition on selling weapons to apostates, identify the Quranic and hadith proofs, and state the exception for food by istihsan.

Core Idea
Selling weapons to apostates is prohibited because it strengthens them in sin, but selling food is permissible by istihsan due to mutual need.

This lesson covers the second major ruling in the passage: the prohibition of selling weapons, horses, and iron to apostates. The proof is drawn from Quran 5:2 (non-cooperation in sin) and a weak hadith. The lesson also discusses the intra-Hanafi difference regarding iron and the istihsan-based permission to sell food, supported by the story of Thumamah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibited items
The text explicitly prohibits selling weapons, horses, and iron to apostates. 'Weapons' (silāḥ) includes all instruments of war.
The prohibition extends to gifting and lending (ʿāriyah) as well.
2
Understand the Quranic proof
Quran 5:2 commands cooperation in righteousness and prohibits cooperation in sin. Selling weapons to apostates is cooperation in sin because it strengthens them against Muslims.
The verse is general (ʿāmm) and includes this specific application.
3
Examine the hadith proof
A hadith narrated by multiple sources prohibits selling weapons during fitnah. Though its chain is weak, it supports the ruling. The Hanafis use it as additional support.
The weakness of the hadith does not undermine the ruling because the Quranic evidence is sufficient.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi difference on iron
Abu al-Layth al-Samarqandi held that selling iron is not disliked because it is not inherently an instrument of war. However, the relied-upon position is prohibition.
The commentary mentions this as a 'face' (wajh) but does not adopt it.
5
Analyze the exception for food by istihsan
Selling food to apostates is permissible by istihsan (juristic preference) because Muslims need trade and could not obtain necessary goods from apostates' lands otherwise. The story of Thumamah supports this.
Istihsan overrides the apparent analogy (qiyas) that would prohibit all trade.

كتاب الجهاد / باب الموادعة ومن يجوز أمانه
وصولح المرتد بلا مال ولا رد إن أخذنا. ولا يباع سلاح، وخيل (وصولح المرتد (1) بلا مال ، ولا رد إن أخذنا) : يعني يجوز لنا أن نصالح المرتد، ولا نعجِّل في قتله ؛ لأنَّ إسلامه مرجو ، لكن لا نأخذ منه شيئاً ؛ لأنَّه يكون جزية، ولا يجوز أخذ الجزية من المرتدّ ، لكن لو أخذنا لا ترد إليه ؛ لأنه مال غير معصوم.
(ولا يباع سلاح، وخيل
[1] قوله : وصولح المرتد ؛ ذكر في «تنوير الأبصار» وشرحه «منح الغفار» وغيرهما : إنّ الصلح بالمرتدين إنّما يجوز إذا غلبوا على بلدة وصارت دارهم دار حرب، وإن لم يغلبوا على بلدة لا تجوز المصالحة بهم ؛ لكون الصلح حينئذ تقريراً لهم على الردّة.
[2] قوله : بلا مال ؛ أي بلا أخذ مال منهم، فإن احتاج إلى إعطاء المال جازَ إعطاءه.
[3] قوله : لأن إسلامه مرجو ؛ فجاز تأخير قتاله طمعاً في إسلامه، قال أبو الليث في «شرح الجامع الصغير»: هذا إذا غلب المرتدون على مدينة الإسلام.
[4] قوله : لأنه مال غير معصوم ؛ فإنّ مال المرتدين فيء للمسلمين إذا ظهروا عليه، بخلاف ما إذا أخذ من أهل البغي، حيث يردّه عليهم بعدما يضع الحرب أوزارها ؛ لأنه ليس بفيء، إلا أنه لا يردّه حال الحرب ؛ لئلا يكون إعانة لهم على معصية، كذا في «البناية) [0] قوله : ولا يباع ؛ وكذا لا يوهب ولا يعار منهم ، والأصل فيه قوله : وَتَعَاوَنُوا عَلَى الْبَرِ وَالتَّقْوَى وَلَا تَعَاوَنُوا عَلَى الْإِثْمِ وَالْعُدْوَانِ ﴾، وفي بيع هذه الأشياء منهم وتمليكهم تقوية لهم على قتال المسلمين.
(1) أي المرتدون فلا بأس بموادعتهم ومعلوم أن ذلك إذا غلبوا على بلدة وصارت دارهم دار الحرب وإلا فلا ؛ لأن فيه تقرير المرتد على الردة ، وذلك لا يجوز. ينظر: «الفتح»(٥: ٤٥٩).
وحديد منهم
ويشهد له حديث: «نهى رسول الله عن بيع السلاح في الفتنة)، أخرجه البَيْهَقِيُّ والبَزار والطبراني وابنُ عَدي وغيرهم، وسنده ضعيف، وأما حمل الطعام إليهم فجائز استحسانا.
[1] قوله : وحديد ؛ قال العَيْنِي في «البناية» : قال الفقيه أبو الليث له في «شرح الجامع الصغير»: إنّ بيع الحديد منهم لا يكره ؛ لأنّ نفسه ليس بآلة للجهاد والفتنة، هذا الوجه
(1) في «صحيح البخاري»(٢ : ٧٤١) معلقاً، وسنن البيهقي الكبير»(٥ : ٣٢٧)، وقال: رفعه وهم، والموقوف أصح. و«الجرح والتعديل (۸: ۱۰۲)، و«الكامل»(٢: ٥١)، و«ضعفاء العقيلي» (٤) : (۱۳۹) و (تاریخ بغداد ۳ (۲۷۸) ، ومسند البزار (٩ : ٦٣) ، وقال : وهذا الحديث لا نعلم أحداً يرويه عن النبي الله إلا عمران بن حصين، وعبد الله اللقيطي ليس بالمعروف، وبحر بن كنيز لم يكن بالقوي، ولكن ما نحفظه عن رسول الله إلا من فلم نجد بداً من إخراجه، وقد رواه سلم بن زرير عن أبي رجاء عن عمران موقوفا، و«معجم الطبراني (١٨ : ١٣٦) ، والسنن الواردة في الفتن (۲) : ٤٠٩) ، وقال الهيثمي في مجمع الزوائد (٤ : ٨٧) : فيه بحر بن كنيز، وهو متروك. وقال ابن حجر في «التلخيص»(۳: ۱۸) : (ضعیف، والصواب وقفه».
(٢) لأن المسلمين يحتاجون إلى بعض ما في ديارهم من الأدوية والأمتعة، فإذا منعناهم ما في ديارنا فهم يمنعون أيضاً ما في ديارهم ؛ ولأن التاجر إذا دخل إليهم ليأتي المسلمين بما ينتفعون به من ديارهم فإنه لا يجد بدا من أن يحمل إليهم بعض ما يوجد في ديارنا ، فلهذا رخصنا للمسلمين في ذلك. ينظر : «المبسوط» (٤ : ١٤١٠)، و«المحيط» (۱) : (۱۳۵) ، و«الهداية»، و«فتح القديره : ٤٦١)، وغيرها.
وعن أبي هريرة له أنه ذكر قصة إسلام ثمامة ، وفي آخره قوله لأهل مكة حين قالوا له : «أصبوت؟ فقال : إني والله ما صبوت، ولكني أسلمت وصدقت محمداً وآمنت به، وأيم الذي نفس ثمامة بيده لا تأتيكم حبة من اليمامة - وكانت ريف مكة ما بقيت – حتى يأذن فيها محمد ، وانصرف إلى بلده، ومنع الحمل إلى مكة حتى جهدت قريش ، فكتبوا إلى رسول الله يسألونه بأرحامهم أن يكتب إلى ثمامة يحمل إليهم الطعام، ففعل رسول الله » في «سنن البيهقي الكبير (٩ : ٦٦)، وغيره.
Book of Jihad / Chapter on Truce and Whose Protection is Permissible. The apostate is made peace with without wealth, and if we take (wealth) it is not returned. And weapons, horses, and iron are not sold to them. (Making peace with the apostate without wealth, and if we take it, it is not returned): meaning it is permissible for us to make peace with the apostate, and not hasten to kill him, because his Islam is hoped for, but we do not take anything from him, because it would be jizya, and taking jizya from an apostate is not permissible, but if we take it, it is not returned to him, because it is wealth without protection. (And weapons, horses, and iron are not sold to them). [1] His statement: 'and the apostate is made peace with'; it is mentioned in 'Tanwir al-Absar' and its commentary 'Minh al-Ghaffar' and others that making peace with apostates is only permissible if they have overpowered a town and their abode has become a war abode, and if they have not overpowered a town, making peace with them is not permitted, because then the peace would be a confirmation of their apostasy. [2] His statement: 'without wealth': meaning without taking wealth from them; if there is need to give wealth, giving it is permissible. [3] His statement: 'because his Islam is hoped for': so delaying his killing is permissible hoping for his Islam. Abu al-Layth said in 'Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir': this is when the apostates have overpowered a city of Islam. [4] His statement: 'because it is wealth without protection': indeed the wealth of apostates is booty for the Muslims when they overpower them, unlike what is taken from the people of baghy (rebels), where it is returned to them after the war lays down its burdens, because it is not booty, except that it is not returned during war, lest it be assistance in disobedience, as in 'al-Binaya'. [0] His statement: 'and it is not sold'; similarly it is not given as a gift and not lent to them; and the basis for it is His statement: 'And cooperate in righteousness and piety, but do not cooperate in sin and aggression' [Quran 5:2]; and selling these things to them and making them owners is strengthening them for fighting the Muslims. (1) meaning the apostates; there is no harm in making truce with them, and it is known that this is when they have overpowered a town and their abode has become a war abode, otherwise not, because in it is confirmation of the apostate upon apostasy, and that is not permissible. See: 'al-Fath' (5:459). And iron from them is also not sold. And there is a hadith to support it: 'The Messenger of Allah prohibited the sale of weapons during fitnah', reported by al-Bayhaqi, al-Bazzar, al-Tabarani, Ibn Adi and others, and its chain is weak. As for carrying food to them, it is permissible by istihsan. [1] His statement: 'and iron'; al-Ayni said in 'al-Binaya': al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said in 'Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir': selling iron to them is not disliked, because its essence is not an instrument for jihad and fitnah. This is one opinion. (1) In Sahih al-Bukhari (2:741) as a suspended narration, and Sunan al-Bayhaqi al-Kubra (5:327), and he said: raising it (to the Prophet) is a mistake, and the stopped version is more correct. And al-Jarh wa al-Ta'dil (8:102), and al-Kamil (2:51), and al-Uqayli's al-Du'afa (4:139), and Tarikh Baghdad (3:278), and Musnad al-Bazzar (9:63), and he said: This hadith we don't know anyone who narrates it from the Prophet except 'Imran ibn Husayn, and Abdullah al-Laqeeti is not well-known, and Bahr ibn Kunayz was not strong, but we have not preserved it from the Messenger of Allah except from this way, so we had no choice but to present it. And it was also narrated by Salam ibn Zurair from Abu Raja' from 'Imran as a stopped narration. And Mu'jam al-Tabarani (18:136), and al-Sunan al-Warida fi al-Fitan (2:409). And al-Haythami said in Majma' al-Zawa'id (4:87): In it is Bahr ibn Kunayz, who is abandoned. And Ibn Hajar said in al-Talkhis (3:18): (Weak, and the correct is that it is stopped). (2) Because Muslims need some of the medicines and goods from their lands; if we prevent them from what is in our land, they will also prevent what is in their land. And because if a trader enters their land to bring to Muslims what they benefit from from their land, he cannot avoid carrying to them some of what is found in our land; therefore we have permitted Muslims in that. See: al-Mabsut (4:1410), al-Muhit (1:135), al-Hidaya, Fath al-Qadir (4:461), and others. And from Abu Hurairah that he mentioned the story of Thumamah's acceptance of Islam, and at its end his statement to the people of Mecca when they said to him: 'Have you become a Sabian?' He said: 'By Allah, I have not become a Sabian, but I have accepted Islam and believed in Muhammad and believe in him. And by the One in whose hand is the soul of Thumamah, not a single grain from al-Yamamah (which was the agricultural land of Mecca) will reach you as long as I remain until Muhammad permits it.' And he returned to his country and prevented the carrying (of goods) to Mecca until Quraysh were strained. So they wrote to the Messenger of Allah asking him by their kinship to write to Thumamah to carry food to them, and the Messenger of Allah did so. Reported in Sunan al-Bayhaqi al-Kubra (9:66) and others.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling regarding making peace with apostates and the prohibition of selling weapons to them. The author explains that it is permissible to make peace with apostates, especially if they have taken over a town and made it a war zone. The reason is that their Islam is hoped for, so delaying fighting them is allowed. However, no wealth should be taken from them in the peace agreement, because it would be considered jizya, which is not permissible from apostates. If wealth is taken, it is not returned to them because their wealth is not protected—it is booty for Muslims. The commentary then addresses the prohibition of selling weapons, horses, and iron to apostates, citing the Quranic verse on non-cooperation in sin and a weak hadith. However, the sale of food to them is permissible by istihsan, due to mutual need and the story of Thumamah when he cut off supplies to Mecca but was later told by the Prophet to resume food delivery.

Key Terms
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference that allows an exception to a general ruling due to necessity or benefit.
فتنة (fitnah): Trial, discord, or civil strife; used in the hadith to refer to times of conflict.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the relied-upon Hanafi ruling on selling iron to apostates?
A) Permissible, because iron is not a weapon.
B) Prohibited, because iron can be used to make weapons.
C) Permissible only if the apostates are not at war.
D) Disliked (makruh) but not prohibited.

Key Takeaway
Selling weapons, horses, and iron to apostates is prohibited; selling food is allowed by istihsan.

Ruling on Selling Iron and Carrying Food to Enemy Territory
Commentary on the ruling of selling iron and carrying food to enemies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling iron to enemies and explain the role of istihsan in permitting the carrying of food.

Core Idea
Iron is not a weapon; selling it to enemies is not disliked, and carrying food is permissible by istihsan for mutual need.

This passage from a Hanafi commentary addresses two distinct commercial interactions with enemy territory: the sale of iron and the transport of food. While a weak hadith prohibits selling weapons during turmoil, the author distinguishes iron as a raw material, not a weapon, and clarifies the relied-upon ruling. For food, juristic preference (istihsan) permits trade to preserve mutual benefit. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for applying fiqh to modern dual-use goods.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
Read the passage to find the ruling on selling iron and the ruling on carrying food to enemy territory. Note the different bases for each.
Iron ruling is about its nature; food ruling uses istihsan.
2
Examine the dalil for the iron ruling
The passage cites a hadith prohibiting weapon sales during fitna. Determine its strength and how it relates to iron.
The hadith is weak and does not directly mention iron.
3
Understand the distinction between iron and weapons
Why does the author say iron itself is not an instrument of jihad? Consider the difference between raw material and finished weapon.
Iron can be used for peaceful purposes.
4
Analyze the istihsan reasoning for food
The passage provides two reasons for permitting food transport: mutual need and the practical necessity of a trader carrying goods. Identify these.
Istihsan overrides strict analogy (qiyas) that might prohibit it.
5
Recognize the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
The text notes a difference between the apparent transmission (zahir al-riwayah) and the verified view. Identify which view is mufta bihi.
The verified view (not disliked) is relied upon.

وحديد منهم
ويشهد له حديث: «نهى رسول الله عن بيع السلاح في الفتنة)، أخرجه البَيْهَقِيُّ والبَزار والطبراني وابنُ عَدي وغيرهم، وسنده ضعيف، وأما حمل الطعام إليهم فجائز استحسانا (٢).
[١] قوله : وحديد ؛ قال العَيْنِي في «البناية» : قال الفقيه أبو الليث له في «شرح الجامع الصغير»: إنّ بيع الحديد منهم لا يكره ؛ لأنّ نفسه ليس بآلة للجهاد والفتنة، هذا الوجه
(1) في «صحيح البخاري»(٢ : ٧٤١) معلقاً، وسنن البيهقي الكبير»(٥ : ٣٢٧)، وقال: رفعـه وهم، والموقوف أصح. و«الجرح والتعديل (۸: ۱۰۲)، و«الكامل»(٢: ٥١)، و«ضعفاء العقيلي» (٤) : (۱۳۹) و (تاریخ بغداد ۳ (۲۷۸) ، ومسند البزار (٩ : ٦٣) ، وقال : وهذا الحديث لا نعلم أحداً يرويه عن النبي الله إلا عمران بن حصين، وعبد الله اللقيطي ليس بالمعروف، وبحر بن كنيز لم يكن بالقوي، ولكن ما نحفظه عن رسول الله إلا من فلم نجد بداً من إخراجه، وقد رواه سلم بن زرير عن أبي رجاء عن عمران موقوفا، و«معجم الطبراني (١٨ : ١٣٦) ، والسنن الواردة في الفتن (۲) : ٤٠٩) ، وقال الهيثمي في مجمع الزوائد (٤ : ٨٧) : فيه بحر بن كنيز، وهو متروك. وقال ابن حجر في «التلخيص»(۳: ۱۸) : (ضعیف، والصواب وقفه». (2) لأن المسلمين يحتاجون إلى بعض ما في ديارهم من الأدوية والأمتعة، فإذا منعناهم ما في ديارنا فهم يمنعون أيضاً ما في ديارهم ؛ ولأن التاجر إذا دخل إليهم ليأتي المسلمين بما ينتفعون به من ديارهم فإنه لا يجد بدا من أن يحمل إليهم بعض ما يوجد في ديارنا ، فلهذا رخصنا للمسلمين في ذلك. ينظر : «المبسوط» (٤ : ١٤١٠)، و«المحيط» (۱) : (۱۳۵) ، و«الهداية»، و«فتح القديره : ٤٦١)، وغيرها.
وعن أبي هريرة له أنه ذكر قصة إسلام ثمامة ، وفي آخره قوله لأهل مكة حين قالوا له : «أصبوت؟ فقال : إني والله ما صبوت، ولكني أسلمت وصدقت محمداً وآمنت به، وأيم الذي نفس ثمامة بيده لا تأتيكم حبة من اليمامة - وكانت ريف مكة ما بقيت – حتى يأذن فيها محمد ، وانصرف إلى بلده، ومنع الحمل إلى مكة حتى جهدت قريش ، فكتبوا إلى رسول الله يسألونه بأرحامهم أن يكتب إلى ثمامة يحمل إليهم الطعام، ففعل رسول الله » في «سنن البيهقي الكبير (٩ : ٦٦)، وغيره.
(3) (البناية (٥ : ٦٧٥).
فصار كالعصير، وهو مثل ما قاله فخر الإسلام، وهو التحقيق إلا أن ظاهر الرواية بخلاف ذلك.
And [the ruling on] iron from them. And a hadith testifies to it: 'The Messenger of Allah forbade the sale of weapons during turmoil,' recorded by al-Bayhaqi, al-Bazzar, al-Tabarani, Ibn 'Adi and others, and its chain is weak. As for carrying food to them, it is permissible by istihsan. [1] His saying: 'and iron'; al-'Ayni said in al-Binayah: al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir: Indeed, selling iron to them is not disliked because its essence is not an instrument for jihad and turmoil. This is the aspect... He became like grape juice, and it is like what Fakhr al-Islam said, and it is the verified view, except that the apparent transmission is contrary to that.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_with_proof discussing two matters: selling iron to enemies and carrying food to them. The text begins by stating a hadith that prohibits selling weapons during fitna, but the hadith is weak. However, the ruling on selling iron is not disliked because iron itself is not inherently a weapon of war; it can be used for peaceful purposes. This is the position of Abu al-Layth al-Samarqandi. Then the passage addresses carrying food to enemies, which is permissible by istihsan (juristic preference) due to mutual need and benefit. The istihsan reasoning is that Muslims need medicines and goods from enemy territory, and if we prohibit trade, they will reciprocally prohibit trade with us. Also, a Muslim trader entering their land to bring beneficial goods will inevitably carry some goods from our land. Thus, we permit it. The passage also cites the story of Thumamah ibn Uthal, who prevented grain from reaching Mecca until the Prophet interceded, showing that food can be allowed after a treaty or need. The passage concludes with a comparison to grape juice (i.e., something that is not inherently harmful) and notes that the verified view is that selling iron is not disliked, though the apparent transmission (zahir al-riwayah) states otherwise. The authority weight: this is a commentary discussion, not a direct fatwa from the core texts. The istihsan ruling is from later works like al-Mabsut.

Key Terms
حديد (hadid): Iron; the raw material that can be used to make weapons, but is not itself a weapon.
حمل الطعام (haml al-ta'am): Carrying or transporting food to enemy territory.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference based on equity, public interest, or necessity, overriding strict analogy (qiyas).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on carrying food to enemy territory according to this passage?
A) It is haram because it strengthens the enemy.
B) It is permissible by istihsan due to mutual need.
C) It is disliked but allowed in necessity.
D) It is only allowed if the enemy is Muslim.

Key Takeaway
Selling iron to enemies is not disliked because iron is not a weapon, and carrying food is permissible by istihsan due to mutual need.

Validity of Amān from Free Muslims After Peace Treaty
Commentary on peace treaties and who can grant amān.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on amān granted by free Muslims after a peace treaty, identify the conditions for its validity, and explain the hadith evidence.

Core Idea
Any free Muslim's amān is valid and binding, even after a peace treaty.

In the chapter on jihad and truces, this passage addresses a crucial question: after a peace treaty (sulh) is in place, can ordinary Muslims grant safe conduct (amān) to enemies? The text clarifies that the peace treaty is revocable and does not restrict individual Muslims from granting amān. It then establishes that the amān of any free Muslim – man or woman – is valid and binding on all Muslims, regardless of their personal shortcomings. The proof comes from the famous incident of Umm Hani' and the Prophet's affirmation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the context
Recognize that the passage deals with amān granted after a peace treaty. The treaty is inherently temporary ('on the verge of being broken and terminated'), so it does not prevent individuals from granting amān.
2
Identify the ruling
The central ruling is that the amān of a free man or free woman is valid (sahih) and binding. This applies even if the granter is sinful, blind, elderly, or a minor with permission to fight.
3
Analyze the conditions
The passage lists conditions for a valid amān: it can be in any language that the enemy understands, and it can be verbal ('I grant safety') or even non-verbal (pointing a finger). The key is that the enemy hears and understands it from a Muslim.
4
Examine the dalil
The proof is from the hadith of Umm Hani' during the conquest of Mecca. She granted amān to a man, and the Prophet said: 'We have given refuge to whom you have given refuge, and granted safety to whom you have granted safety.' This establishes that a woman's amān is binding, and by extension the amān of any free Muslim. The passage also cites the general principle that the lowest Muslim can make a covenant on behalf of all.

Source Text

كتاب الجهاد / باب الموادعة ومن يجوز أمانه
ولو بعد صلح، وصح أمان حر وحرة
[1] قوله : بعد صلح ؛ لأنّ الصلح على شرف النقض والانقضاء.
[2] قوله : وصح ؛ بأي لغة ،كان، وإن كانوا لا يعرفون تلك اللغة بعد معرفة المسلمين بها، بشرط سماعهم ذلك من المسلمين فلا أمان لو كان بالبعد منهم ، وألفاظه : آمنت ولا بأس عليك، وتعال وكذا الإشارة بالإصبع تكفي كذا في «التنوير» وشروحه.

The Book of Jihad / Chapter of Truce and Who May Grant Amān. And even after a peace treaty, and the amān of a free man and free woman is valid. [1] His saying: 'after a peace treaty' because the peace treaty is on the verge of being broken and terminated. [2] His saying: 'and it is valid' in any language, even if they do not know that language after the Muslims know it, on condition that they hear it from the Muslims; so no amān if it is from afar. Its expressions: 'I grant safety' and 'no harm upon you' and 'come', and likewise pointing with a finger suffices. So it is in al-Tanwir and its commentaries. [3] His saying: 'amān of a free man and free woman' even if he is sinful, blind, an old man, or a boy who has been given permission to fight. So if one free man or free woman grants safety to a disbeliever or a group of them, or the people of a city or a fort, his amān is considered and fighting them becomes forbidden. And it is established in the two Sahihs and others that Umm Hani', the sister of 'Ali, said on the conquest of Mecca: 'O Messenger of Allah, my son claims that he will kill a man whom I have given refuge, so-and-so ibn Hubayrah.' The Messenger of Allah said: 'We have given refuge to whom you have given refuge, and granted safety to whom you have granted safety.' And in Sunan Ibn Majah marfu'an: 'Muslims' blood is equal and the lowest of them can make a covenant on their behalf,' and similar in Sunan Abi Dawud, the two Sahihs, and others. Footnotes: (1) Because the peace treaty is on the verge of breaking or termination, and it does not prevent anyone from bringing food and clothes into their lands. (2) i.e., from the Muslims, whether a disbeliever or disbelievers or people of a fort or city, such that it is not permissible for any Muslim to kill them. (3) In Sahih al-Bukhari (3:1157) and Sahih Muslim (1:498), etc. (4) In al-Muntaqa (1:194) and al-Mustadrak (2:153), and al-Hakim said: a sahih hadith according to the conditions of the two shaykhs but they did not record it, and Sunan Abi Dawud (3:180), Sunan al-Nasa'i (4:217), al-Mu'jam al-Awsat (6:153), etc.
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_with_proof on the validity of amān (safe conduct) granted by free Muslim men and women after a peace treaty. The text first explains that the peace treaty (sulh) is inherently temporary and revocable. Then it discusses the conditions for a valid amān: it can be in any language as long as the enemy understands it, and it suffices to say 'You are safe' or 'No harm upon you' or even a gesture like pointing a finger. The key ruling: amān given by any free Muslim (whether man or woman) is valid and binding on all Muslims, even if the person granting it is sinful, blind, elderly, or a minor who has permission to fight. This is proven by the hadith of Umm Hani' at the conquest of Mecca, where the Prophet affirmed her grant of safety. The passage also cites the general principle that the lowest Muslim can make a covenant on behalf of all. The footnotes reiterate that the treaty does not prevent normal trade and that the amān prohibits all Muslims from fighting those granted safety. The authority weight is based on authentic hadiths in Bukhari and Muslim.

Key Terms
أمان (amān): Safe conduct or guarantee of safety given to an enemy combatant, making it forbidden for Muslims to harm them.
صلح (sulh): Peace treaty or truce with enemy combatants, which is revocable (on the verge of breach).
حر (hurr): Free person, as opposed to a slave. Only free Muslims can grant amān that is binding.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, whose amān is considered valid and binding?
A) Only the caliph's amān.
B) Only a male warrior's amān.
C) Any free Muslim man or woman's amān.
D) Only a Muslim judge's amān.

Key Takeaway
The amān of any free Muslim, even a woman, is valid and binding on all Muslims, as established by the Prophet's affirmation of Umm Hani' and the principle of mutual guarantee.

Validity of Aman: Who Can Grant Safe Conduct in Jihad
Commentary on who can give aman and its validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid aman and distinguish between valid and invalid givers according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Aman from any free Muslim is valid; from a dhimmi, slave, or prisoner it is only personal, not public.

This passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah addresses a crucial aspect of jihad: the granting of safe conduct (aman) to enemy combatants. While the general principle is that any free Muslim can grant aman, there are important exceptions and internal disputes within the Hanafi school that shape the operative ruling. Understanding these nuances is essential for correctly applying the rules of war in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act of aman? Who can give it validly? The passage states that aman given by a free Muslim man or woman is valid, and fighting becomes forbidden upon it.
The ruling is that aman is sahih (valid) under certain conditions.
2
Locate the dalil
The commentator cites two hadith: the hadith of Umm Hani' (the Prophet upheld her aman) and the hadith 'The lowest Muslim can grant protection on their behalf.' These are found in Sahih al-Bukhari, Sahih Muslim, Sunan Ibn Majah, and Sunan Abi Dawud.
Both hadith are from authoritative sources (thubut qati).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith of Umm Hani' directly shows that a free Muslim woman's aman is valid and binding. The second hadith extends this to any free Muslim, even the lowest, indicating that the aman of any free Muslim is effective.
The dalala is zanni because the hadith could be interpreted with restrictions, as the intra-Hanafi dispute shows.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Fakhr al-Islam held that the aman of a free person is unconditionally valid, likening it to عصير (juice) – its sweetness is clear. However, the commentator states that the Zahir al-Riwaya (the relied-upon transmission) differs, implying some restriction.
The exact nature of the restriction is not detailed here, but it likely involves the imam's authority to repudiate harmful aman.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The commentator gives precedence to the Zahir al-Riwaya, meaning the mufta bihi position is that the aman of a free Muslim is valid but may be overridden by the imam if it is considered harmful (as indicated later in the passage).
This tarjih is supported by the later discussion on repudiation of harmful aman.

His statement: 'and it is valid' in any language, even if they do not know that language after the Muslims' knowledge of it, on condition that they hear it from the Muslims; so there is no aman if it is from afar. Its expressions: 'I grant aman', 'no harm upon you', 'come', and similarly pointing with the finger is sufficient, as in 'al-Tanwir' and its commentaries. His statement: 'aman of a free man and free woman'; even if he is a sinner, blind, decrepit old man, or a boy who has been given permission to fight. So if one of the free men or free women grants aman to a disbeliever, or a group of them, or the people of a city, or the people of a fort, his aman is considered, and fighting them becomes forbidden. And the aman of the dhimmi, the prisoner, the trader who is with them, the one who embraced Islam there but did not migrate to us, the boy, and the slave is void. His statement: 'if it is evil' meaning if one of the army grants aman and the Imam sees it as evil, he informs them that it is not considered, as in the revocation of a truce. His statement: 'and he is disciplined' in the passive voice from ta'dib; meaning the Imam disciplines the one who issued the aman without consideration of consequences. His statement: 'and the aman of the dhimmi... etc.' means their aman is not considered regarding the general public. As for the invalidity of the dhimmi's aman, it is because he has no authority over Muslims, and because he is suspected in the aman. Yes, if the dhimmi orders a Muslim to grant aman, saying 'Grant them aman', and the Muslim says 'I grant aman' or 'such-and-such Muslim has granted you aman', that is valid, as al-Sarakhsi mentioned. As for the invalidity of the aman of the prisoner and the trader, it is because they are subdued under the hands of the disbelievers and are not feared, and aman is specific to a place of fear. Yes, their aman is valid regarding themselves, so they cannot take anything from their property without permission. Similarly, the meaning of the invalidity of the aman of a restricted slave is regarding others; as for himself, it is valid, as in 'al-Bahr'.
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage is from the commentary on Kitab al-Jihad, specifically the chapter on truce and aman. The author first states that aman given by a free Muslim man or woman is valid. He then notes an intra-Hanafi dispute: Fakhr al-Islam held that the aman of a free person is valid under any circumstances, likening it to juice (i.e., its sweetness is clear), but the Zahir al-Riwayah (the relied-upon transmission) holds a different view, implying some restriction. The commentary then explains the conditions of aman: it can be in any language understood by the enemy, provided they hear it directly; gestures like pointing are sufficient. The aman of wicked, blind, old, or even a child authorized to fight is valid. This is supported by the hadith of Umm Hani' where the Prophet validated her protection. Another hadith states that the lowest Muslim can grant protection. The commentary then discusses when aman is considered evil: if the imam deems it harmful, it can be repudiated and the giver disciplined. Finally, it details whose aman is void: dhimmis, prisoners, traders residing with the enemy, a convert who has not migrated, and slaves. The reason is that these individuals lack authority or are under enemy control and thus not feared; aman works only where there is fear of combat. However, their aman is valid for themselves personally, meaning they cannot be harmed or their property taken without permission. The commentary elaborates that a dhimmi's aman is invalid due to lack of authority and suspected loyalty, but if he instructs a Muslim to grant aman, it becomes valid by the Muslim's action.

Key Terms
أمان (aman): Safe conduct or protection granted to an enemy combatant, rendering their person and property inviolable.
موادعة (muwada'ah): A truce or treaty of peace with non-Muslims.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The apparent or manifest transmission, referring to the most authoritative narrations of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani's six books.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on aman given by a restricted slave?
A) It is completely invalid under all circumstances.
B) It is valid for himself but invalid for the general public.
C) It is valid for the general public but not for himself.
D) It is valid only if the slave is authorized to fight.

Key Takeaway
The validity of aman depends on the giver's status as a free Muslim; those under authority (dhimmi, slave, prisoner) can only grant it for themselves.

Validity of Aman by Various Individuals in Jihad
Lists rulings on who may grant valid aman and why

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which individuals can grant valid aman for the Muslim army and state the Hanafi rationale for each category.

Core Idea
Only Muslims with authority to fight can grant binding aman; aman of dhimmi, captive, trader, non-migrant, minor, or slave is void for the army.

Aman (safe conduct) is a crucial tool in jihad, allowing Muslims to guarantee safety to enemies. However, not every Muslim can bind the entire army. This passage from 'Imdad al-Ri'aya clarifies which individuals' aman is considered void and why, drawing on the principle that aman requires authority and the capacity to inspire fear in the enemy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling
The matn states that the aman of six categories of individuals is void: a dhimmi, a Muslim captive, a Muslim trader living with the enemy, one who accepted Islam but did not migrate, a minor, and a slave. The commentary explains the rationale for each.
The ruling applies to general binding aman; there are exceptions for personal aman and when a dhimmi instructs a Muslim.
2
Analyze the rationale for each category
For each category, the commentary gives a reason: dhimmi has no authority over Muslims and is suspect; captive and trader are under enemy control and not feared; minor and slave lack capacity for jihad; non-migrant is not fully part of the Muslim polity.
Understanding the 'illa (cause) helps in analogizing to other cases.
3
Distinguish between general and personal aman
While the aman of these individuals is void for the army, it is valid regarding themselves. For example, a captive's aman protects his own person and property, but does not bind other Muslims.
This distinction is crucial for applying the ruling in practice.
4
Note the exception for authorized minors and slaves
The text makes an exception for minors and slaves who have been authorized (ma'dhun) by their guardian or master to fight. Their aman is valid because they have the capacity for jihad.
The phrase 'illa ma'dhunain' shows that authorization changes the ruling.
5
Apply the ruling to a new scenario
Given a hypothetical situation, determine whether a particular individual's aman is binding on the army, and explain why based on the principles learned.
Practice strengthens the ability to derive rulings from the given categories.

However, if the dhimmi commands a Muslim to give aman, saying 'Give them aman' and he says 'I give aman' or 'So-and-so the Muslim has given you aman,' it is valid. Thus mentioned by al-Sarakhsi. As for the invalidity of the aman of the captive and trader, because they are subdued under the hands of the disbelievers, so they do not fear them, and aman is specific to a place of fear. However, their aman is valid regarding themselves, so they do not take anything from their wealth without permission. Similarly, the meaning of the invalidity of the aman of a restricted slave is regarding others, but regarding himself it is valid. Thus in al-Bahr. (1) Because they are subdued under their hands, so they do not fear them, and aman is specific to a place of fear. See Majma' al-Anhur (1:639). (2) Yunus: from verse 61. (3) An-Najm: 9. (4) Al-Bahr al-Raiq (5:88). Book of Jihad, Chapter on Truce and Who May Give Aman. Except for the authorized and the insane. Except for the authorized and the insane: The captive means a Muslim captive in the hands of the disbelievers, and the trader means a Muslim trader with them. And Allah knows best. As for the invalidity of the aman of the restricted slave, minor, and one who became Muslim and did not migrate, because they do not have the capacity for fighting, so they have no right to aman. [1] His statement 'illa': an exception from the minor and slave; i.e., except the minor and slave who have been authorized by the guardian and master to fight.
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) from 'Imdad al-Ri'aya' on the matn of 'Al-Wiqaya' discussing the validity of aman (safe conduct) granted by various individuals in the context of jihad. The author, al-Qari, explains the rulings and their rationales.

The matn states that if a single person from the army grants aman and the imam considers it harmful, it is rejected (nubidha) and the grantor is disciplined (uddiba). The commentary clarifies that the imam should inform the army that the aman is not recognized, similar to annulling a treaty. 'Uddiba' means the imam punishes the person who gave aman without considering consequences.

The main ruling: The aman of the following individuals is void (lagha) in the sense that it does not bind the Muslim army collectively: a dhimmi (non-Muslim subject), a Muslim captive (asir) in enemy hands, a Muslim trader (tajir) living among enemies, a person who accepted Islam but did not migrate to Muslim territory, a minor (sabi), and a slave (abd). However, if the imam deems it harmful, he may still reject it and discipline the grantor.

The commentary then delves into why each is invalid. For the dhimmi: he has no authority over Muslims and is suspect in giving aman. But if the dhimmi instructs a Muslim to give aman, and the Muslim does so, it is valid. For the captive and trader: they are under enemy control, so they do not inspire fear in the enemy; aman is only effective where fear exists. However, their aman is valid regarding themselves, meaning they cannot be attacked or have their property taken without permission. For the minor and slave (who are restricted, i.e., not authorized to fight): they lack the capacity for jihad, so they have no right to grant aman. The exception is if they are authorized (ma'dhun) by their guardian or master to fight, then their aman is valid.

The passage also discusses the word 'adna' in the Quranic verses cited, which indicates the validity of aman by a single person (one soldier) because 'adna' can mean 'less' (in number) or 'nearer' (in proximity) or 'baser' (in character). The commentary from al-Sarakhsi's Sharh al-Siyar al-Kabir explains these derivations.

In summary, aman affecting the whole army is only valid from those with authority to fight and who are not under enemy coercion, unless the imam overrules it. The private aman of a captive or trader protects their own person and property.

Key Terms
الأمان (al-amān): Safe conduct or guarantee of security granted to enemy combatants or non-Muslims by Muslims.
الذمي (al-dhimmī): A non-Muslim permanently residing under Muslim rule with a pact of protection (dhimma).
الأسير (al-asīr): A Muslim captive held by the enemy.
التاجر (al-tājir): A Muslim trader residing in enemy territory for commerce.
المأذون (al-ma’dhūn): A minor or slave who has been explicitly authorized by guardian or master to participate in jihad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as explained in this passage, the aman of a dhimmi is considered void because:
A) The dhimmi has no authority over Muslims and is suspected in giving aman.
B) The dhimmi is not a Muslim.
C) The dhimmi is always under enemy control.
D) The dhimmi cannot be trusted due to his religion.

Key Takeaway
Aman binding the Muslim army is valid only from a Muslim with authority to fight and not under coercion; aman of a dhimmi, captive, trader, non-migrant, minor, or slave is void for the army but may protect the grantor personally.

Who Can Grant Aman? The Rule for Slaves and Minors
Commentary on who may grant aman, focusing on the exception for authorized slaves and minors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of aman granted by slaves and minors, and identify when authorization makes their aman valid.

Core Idea
Aman is invalid from those who cannot fight; authorization to fight grants capacity.

In the Hanafi school, the right to grant aman (safe conduct) is tied directly to the ability to fight. This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah explains that interdicted slaves and minors, who normally lack the right to engage in combat, cannot grant valid aman. However, if their master or guardian has authorized them to fight, they acquire the capacity to grant aman. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the exception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the excluded categories
List who cannot grant aman: the interdicted slave and minor, and one who embraced Islam but did not migrate.
The passage also mentions the insane, but the commentary focuses on slaves and minors.
2
Understand the rationale
The text states: 'because they do not possess the right to fight.' In Hanafi law, aman is an act of protection that only one who can fight can offer.
This is a key principle: the ability to grant aman is based on the ability to engage in jihad.
3
Recognize the exception
The exception is for those who have been given permission by their guardian or master to fight. Such a person is considered capable of fighting and thus can grant aman.
Authorization can be general or specific for a particular battle.
4
Connect to modern implications
Today, the principle applies in any context where protection is offered to non-Muslims in a conflict zone. Only those with authority to engage can grant safe conduct.
This shows the systematic consistency of Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلا مأذونين ومجنون) : المراد بالأسير: مسلم أسير في يد الكفّار، وبالتاجر : تاجر مسلم معهم. والله تعالى أعلم. وأما عدم صحة العبد والصبي المحجورين والذي أسلم ولم يهاجر ؛ فلأنهم لا يملكون القتال فلا حق لهم في الأمان. [١] قوله : إلا ؛ استثناء من الصبي والعبد ؛ أي إلا الصبي والعبد الذي أذن لهما من جهة الولي والمولى في القتال. وجي[؟]
Except the authorized and the insane: The intended meaning of 'the captive' is a Muslim captive in the hands of the disbelievers, and of 'the merchant' is a Muslim merchant among them. And Allah Almighty knows best. As for the invalidity of [aman from] a slave and a minor who are interdicted, and one who embraced Islam but did not migrate—because they do not possess the right to fight, thus they have no right to grant aman. [1] His statement: 'except' is an exception from the minor and slave; i.e., except the minor and slave who have been given permission from the guardian and master to fight.
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a ruling from the chapter of jihad concerning who is eligible to grant aman (safe conduct). The base text states 'except the authorized and the insane,' and the commentator clarifies the meaning. First, he defines two terms: 'al-asir' (the captive) refers to a Muslim prisoner in enemy hands, and 'al-tajir' (the merchant) refers to a Muslim trader residing among disbelievers. This is background context. Then he addresses the core ruling: the aman granted by an interdicted slave or minor is invalid. The reason given is that they do not possess the right to engage in fighting, so they lack the authority to grant aman. The same applies to one who embraced Islam but did not migrate (hijrah). The commentator then explains that the exception 'except the authorized' refers to a slave or minor who has been given explicit permission by their master or guardian to participate in combat. Such an individual is considered capable of fighting and therefore may grant aman. This passage highlights the Hanafi principle that the right to grant aman is contingent upon the ability to fight, which is absent in minors, slaves, and non-migrants unless specifically authorized.

Key Terms
الأمان (al-amān): A guarantee of safety granted to an enemy combatant or non-Muslim by a Muslim.
العبد (al-ʿabd): A male slave who is interdicted (mahjur) and thus does not have independent legal capacity to grant aman.
الصبي (al-ṣabī): A male minor who has not reached puberty and is under guardianship; cannot grant aman unless authorized.
مأذون (maʾdhūn): A slave or minor who has been given specific permission by their master or guardian to fight.

Definition of Maghnam and Fay' and Division of Booty
Commentary defining maghnam and fay' and rules of division

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish maghnam from fay' and state the Hanafi ruling on the division of booty taken by force.

Core Idea
Booty (maghnam) is divided after khums; fay' has no khums and goes to the treasury.

Understanding the distinction between maghnam and fay' is crucial for knowing how war spoils are handled in Hanafi fiqh. This lesson defines both terms, explains the rules of division, and provides the prophetic precedent from the conquest of Khaybar. You will learn that not all wealth from disbelievers is booty subject to khums.

Guided Path
1
Define maghnam and fay'
Identify the key characteristics of each term: maghnam is taken by force; fay' is taken without fighting.
Maghnam is also called ghanimah.
2
State the ruling on maghnam
Maghnam: khums (1/5) is wajib for the imam, the rest is divided among the warriors who participated in the battle.
3
State the ruling on fay'
Fay': no khums, placed directly into the public treasury (bayt al-mal).
4
Identify exceptions
Items taken as gift, theft, or stealth are neither maghnam nor fay' – they belong to the taker.
5
Apply the proof
The Prophet's division of Khaybar (half for his needs, half for Muslims) is the basis for the imam setting aside a portion (khums) before dividing the remainder.
This hadith is reported by Abu Dawud.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب المغنم وقسمته

[1] قوله : باب المغنم وقسمته ؛ المغنم - بفتح الميم وفتح النون، بينهما عين ، هو الغنيمة : اسم لما ينالُ من الكفّار عنوة بقوة الغزاة، وما أُخِذَ منهم من غير قتال ؛ كالخراج والجزية يُسمّى فيئاً ، فالمغنم خمسه للإمام، وباقيه للغانمين يقسم بينهم على ما سيأتي تفصيله. والفيء لا خمس فيه، بل يوضع في بيت المال، وما يؤخذ من أموال الكفار هدية أو سرقة أو خلسة أو هبة فليس بغينمة ولا فيء، وهو للآخذ خاصة، كذا في حواشي «الهداية)

[٢] قوله : قسم ؛ ماض من التقسيم وما بعده فاعله ، وهذا فيما فتحت قهراً، وأمّا المفتوح صلحاً فيجري الإمام على حسب صلحه.

(۳) قوله : بين الجيش ؛ أي جيش الغزاة ؛ أي قَسْمَ ما وُجِدَ من الكفّار من الأموال والذوات المسترقة وأراضي البلدة المفتوحة على الغزاة بعد إخراج الخمس لجهاته ، كذا في «الفتح». والأصل فيه ما ثبت عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم حين فتح خيبر حيث «قسمه نصفين، فنصف لنوائبه، ونصفا بين المسلمين» (۳) ، أخرجه أبو داود وغيره.

[٤] قوله : عنوة ؛ - بفتح العين المهملة - ، فسَّرَه صاحب «الهداية»(4) بالقهر ، وذكر شراحها : إنه تفسير له باعتبار اللازم الذهني، فإنّ الذهن ينتقل من معناه الأصلي إلى القهر، ومعناه الأصلي هو الذلّة ، يقال : عنا يعنو عنوة، ذلّ وخضع.
Chapter of Booty and its Division

[1] His statement: Chapter of Booty and its Division; "al-maghnam" - with fatha on the meem and fatha on the noon, with an 'ayn between them - is the booty: a term for what is obtained from the disbelievers by force through the strength of the warriors, and what is taken from them without fighting, such as kharaj and jizya, is called "fay'". So the khums of the maghnam is for the imam, and the rest is for the warriors, to be divided among them as will be detailed later. And the fay' has no khums in it, rather it is placed in the public treasury. And whatever is taken from the wealth of the disbelievers as a gift, or by theft, or stealth, or as a present, is not booty nor fay', and it is for the taker specifically. So it is in the glosses of al-Hidayah.

[2] His statement: "divided"; it is the past tense of division, and what follows it is its subject. This applies to what is conquered forcefully. As for what is conquered by peace, the imam proceeds according to his peace treaty.

(3) His statement: "among the army"; meaning the army of the warriors; meaning the division of what is found among the disbelievers of wealth, movable items, and the lands of the conquered town among the warriors after taking out the khums for its designated recipients. So it is in al-Fath. And the basis for it is what is established from the Prophet (peace be upon him) when he conquered Khaybar, as he "divided it in two halves: one half for his pressing needs, and one half among the Muslims" reported by Abu Dawud and others.

[4] His statement: "by force" ('anwat) - with fatha on the important 'ayn - the author of al-Hidayah interpreted it as "by subjugation", and the commentators mentioned that this is an interpretation based on the mental necessary implication, for the mind moves from its original meaning to subjugation. Its original meaning is "humiliation". It is said: 'ana ya'nu 'anwat, meaning he was humiliated and submitted.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the chapter heading 'Booty and its Division'. It begins by defining two key terms: al-maghnam (booty) and al-fay' (fay). Maghnam is wealth obtained from disbelievers by force through combat, and its one-fifth (khums) is given to the imam for the Islamic state's designated purposes, while the remaining four-fifths are distributed among the warriors. Fay', on the other hand, is wealth obtained without fighting, such as land tax (kharaj) and poll tax (jizya); it has no khums and is deposited directly into the public treasury. The author then notes that items taken as gifts, theft, or stealth are neither booty nor fay', but belong to the taker. The commentary then explains the word 'qasama' (divided), clarifying that the ruling applies to lands conquered by force; for lands conquered by peace treaty, the imam follows the terms of the treaty. The phrase 'among the army' is interpreted as referring to the warriors who participated in the battle, and the division includes movable wealth and lands after taking out khums. The legal basis for this is the Prophet's (peace be upon him) division of Khaybar, where he split it in half: one half for his own pressing needs (which functioned like the khums) and the other half among the Muslims. Finally, the word 'anwat' (by force) is explained: it literally means 'humiliation', but here it is used metaphorically to mean 'by force' or 'by subjugation', as the mind naturally connects the idea of humiliation with being overpowered.

Key Terms
المغنم (al-maghnam): The spoils taken from disbelievers by force after combat, also called al-ghanimah.
الفيء (al-fay'): Wealth obtained from disbelievers without fighting, such as kharaj and jizya, which is placed in the public treasury and has no khums.
عنوة ('anwat): By force; originally meaning humiliation, but used here to indicate conquest by overpowering the enemy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Maghnam is obtained by force and has khums; fay' is obtained without fighting and has no khums.
B) Both are the same and are subject to khums.
C) Maghnam has no khums, while fay' has khums.
D) Fay' is obtained by force and maghnam is obtained without fighting.

Key Takeaway
Maghnam (booty) taken by force is divided after setting aside the wajib khums for the imam; fay' obtained without fighting has no khums and is deposited in the treasury.
